Received 
      2nd July 2017
    First published on 29th November 2017AbstractOrganic semiconductors have attracted a lot of attention since the discovery of highly doped conductive polymers, due to the potential application in field-effect transistors (OFETs), light-emitting diodes (OLEDs) and photovoltaic cells (OPVs). Single crystals of organic semiconductors are particularly intriguing because they are free of grain boundaries and have long-range periodic order as well as minimal traps and defects. Hence, organic semiconductor crystals provide a powerful tool for revealing the intrinsic properties, examining the structure–property relationships, demonstrating the important factors for high performance devices and uncovering fundamental physics in organic semiconductors. This review provides a comprehensive overview of the molecular packing, morphology and charge transport features of organic semiconductor crystals, the control of crystallization for achieving high quality crystals and the device physics in the three main applications. We hope that this comprehensive summary can give a clear picture of the state-of-art status and guide future work in this area.
Chengliang Wang
Chengliang Wang is a Professor in Huazhong University of Science and Technology (HUST). He received his Bachelor's degree in Chemistry from Nanjing University in 2005 and PhD degree from the Institute of Chemistry, Chinese Academy of Sciences (CAS) in 2010. He then worked in the Chinese University of Hong Kong, University of Muenster and Technical University of Ilmenau. He was selected in the National 1000-Talents Program for Young Scholars and joined HUST in 2016. His research topics include the design and synthesis of novel conjugated materials, crystals, nanostructures and their applications in organic optoelectronics and flexible batteries.
Huanli Dong
Huanli Dong is a Professor at the Institute of Chemistry, CAS. She received her MS degree (2006) from Fujian Institute of Material Structure, CAS, and her PhD (2009) from the Institute of Chemistry, CAS. She was awarded the Outstanding Young Scientist of NSFC in 2012 and the Prize for Young Chemists of Chinese Chemical Society in 2014. Her research interests include molecular materials, crystals and devices of organic/polymeric semiconductors. Currently, she has more than 140 refereed publications with over 4500 citations.

He received his BS degree in Applied Physics from the School of Physics and Microelectronics Science, Hunan University in 2004 and his PhD degree from ICCAS in 2011. Then he became an Assistant Professor in ICCAS. In 2013, he worked as a postdoctoral fellow with Prof. Henning Sirringhaus in Cavendish Laboratory, University of Cambridge. In 2016, he moved back to ICCAS, as part of the Hundred Talents Program. His current research focuses on molecular electronics including crystalline organic semiconductors and device engineering and their circuit applications.

1 Introduction
Semiconductors are adopted in such a wide range of applications, such as in displays, computers, telephones, etc., that they have become virtually indispensable for daily life. A semiconductor is a material which has electrical conductivity between that of conductors and insulators. Although there is no precise definition of semiconductors, generally a material possessing a conductivity of about 10−8–103 S cm−1 is regarded as a semiconductor. The conductivity differences of the materials originate from their different energy band structures. In a conductor, usually the valence band (the highest occupied energy band) is partially filled. That is, there is an overlap between the valence band and the conduction band (the lowest unoccupied energy band). Where semiconductors (low energy gaps, usually <4.0 eV) and insulators (high energy gaps, usually >4.0 eV) are concerned, there is an energy gap (band gap) between the valence band and the conduction band. Under certain conditions, semiconductors can be conductive, e.g. as a result of the excitation of charges from the valence band into the conduction band. However, insulators are not conductive, due to their large band gaps (charge carriers are localized). The adjustable conductivity of semiconductors by doping or other techniques makes them promising for different kinds of applications, and therefore they have attracted particular attention since the 1940s. For example, it has been demonstrated that an external electric field can work effectively to tune both injection and accumulation of charges in semiconductors, which can yield high conductivity, and devices based on this mechanism are denoted as field-effect transistors (FETs). The importance of this research is demonstrated by a wide range of FET applications and by the fact that 6 scientists have shared the Nobel Prize in Physics on two occasions: in 1956 for applications in transistors and in 2000 for semiconductor heterostructures and integrated circuit.
The discovery of “organic semiconductors” dates back to the 1940s.1 Researchers then found that with halogens most polycyclic aromatic compounds can form semiconducting charge-transfer complex salts (e.g. perylene–bromine complex2 showed a conductivity of 0.1–1 S cm−1). Of particular note was the discovery of tetracyanoquinodimethane (TCNQ) complexes3,4 in the early 1970s with a metallic conductivity as high as 104–105 S cm−1. In 1977, Heeger, MacDiarmid, Shirakawa et al. reported high conductivity in doped polyacetylene.5 This led them to win the 2000 Nobel Prize in Chemistry for “The discovery and development of conductive polymers”. These findings indicated that organic compounds are capable of transporting current and appropriate molecular design and doping can tune the conductivity, which makes organic semiconductors fascinating for potential applications. In contrast to inorganic semiconductors which have continuous energy bands, organic semiconductors possess discrete “energy levels”, consisting of molecular orbitals. Charge transport depends on the ability of the charge carriers to pass from one molecule to another. This is closely related to π-bonding orbitals and quantum mechanical wave-function overlap, namely, the energy levels of the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO), the lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) and the energy band gap between the HOMO and LUMO energy levels. The possibility of tuning the conductivity of organic semiconductors using molecular design has attracted intensive interest and inspired many intriguing properties such as low-temperature processability, flexibility and diverse colours as well as cost-effective applications.6 As the three main applications of organic semiconductors, the research on organic field-effect transistors (OFETs), light-emitting diodes (OLEDs) and photovoltaic cells (OPVs) is therefore of great importance.
Single crystals of organic semiconductors are free of grain boundaries and have long-range periodic order as well as minimal traps and defects. Hence, crystals are particularly beneficial for revealing the intrinsic properties of organic semiconductors, providing a powerful tool for examining structure–property relationships, demonstrating the important factors for high performance devices and studying fundamental physics in organic semiconductors. This in turn further guides molecular design and syntheses. The first reported organic semiconductor crystals (naphthalene and anthracene crystals) date back to the 1940–1950s.7,8 After half a century's research, organic semiconductor crystals have become a tool with widespread use such as for determining the packing and intrinsic properties of semiconductor materials or today's advanced materials used in organic electronics and energy harvesting. By using the term “organic semiconductor crystal” to search the Web of Science, more than 43000 publications are found and more than 90% of these articles have been published since 2000. This suggests that organic semiconductor crystals are now one of the ‘hottest’ research areas.8–20 Therefore, a timely review in this field is not only of scientific importance but is also technologically imperative. A general overview highlighting the most important progress in this rapidly expanding field will ensure that the readers are not submerged by the flood of references. This “organic semiconductor crystals” review will comprehensively cover the introduction and history (Section 1), the structural, physicochemical and charge transport properties, the theoretical studies and the experimental investigation of semiconducting properties (Section 2), as well as the crystal growth methods (Section 3). Finally, we will focus on the device physics in optoelectronics using organic semiconductor crystals as prototypes, discuss the factors which influence the device performance, investigate the structure–property relationships for achieving high performance devices and examine the charge generation, injection, transport and combination mechanisms (Section 4). It is hoped that as a result of this comprehensive analysis, explicit information about the current research situation will guide further efforts in developing future applications of organic semiconductors in optoelectronics.
2 Physicochemical features of organic semiconductor crystals
2.1 Molecular packing features

2.1.1 Molecular structure and packing. 
Organic semiconductors usually contain conjugated systems, such as π-systems,6 with overlapping π-orbitals bridging the adjacent single bonds. This allows delocalization of the π-electrons across all the aligned π-orbitals of the molecule. As the most common π-conjugated systems in organic semiconductors, the CC bond is formed by two carbon atoms with sp2-hybridization where the sp2-orbitals form a triangle within a plane and the pz-orbitals are perpendicular to the plane. The orbital overlap of one sp2-orbital with that of another carbon atom can form a σ-bond. Because there is a very large energy difference (large energy gap) between its occupied bonding orbitals and unoccupied anti-bonding orbitals, the σ-bond will result in insulating properties and has no contribution to the semiconducting properties. However, the pz-orbitals can form π-bonds with smaller energy gaps, which is the reason that semiconductors usually possess π-conjugated systems. For larger planar molecules, the π-bonds become delocalized and form a π-system. The energy gap between the occupied and empty states in the π-systems becomes even smaller and they can exhibit semiconducting properties with efficient charge transport. For long chain compounds, the π-bonds become delocalized along the chain and form a one-dimensional electronic system. From this point of view, long-range order is a prerequisite for achieving large delocalized π-systems for efficient charge transport through interacting molecular π-orbitals.
From the physical description of the polaron theory (the most popular theory for charge transport, details can be found in Section 2.4), the system Hamiltonian can be regarded as a sum of three terms,21–23H = He + HL + Hint, where He, HL and Hint are the electronic component, the lattice component and the electron–lattice interaction respectively. It is clear that for small molecule semiconductors, long range intramolecular delocalization is not likely. Therefore, the charge transport will be largely determined by the packing of the molecules, i.e. the chemical structure, the molecular arrangement and the intermolecular interactions. These parameters will determine the electron state as localization in the molecule (small polaron: the electronic bandwidth 2J is much smaller than the maximum binding energy of the polaron A2/2Mω02) or delocalization over many molecules (large polaron: 2J ≫ A2/2Mω02). Herein, J is the electronic overlap integral describing the motion of the electron through the lattice, while A, M and ω0 are a constant, the relative mass and harmonic vibration frequency respectively.
The understanding of the molecular packing features is beneficial for both the design and synthesis of organic semiconductors and understanding the structure–property relationship and the limits of charge transport. An organic crystal can be considered as being made up of identical molecules stacked regularly and closely and held together by intermolecular interactions24 such as hydrogen bonds, charge transfer or van der Waals forces. It is indisputable that the properties of crystals are closely related to their molecular packing as well as to the properties of the parent molecules. For example, it has long been established that organic crystals are highly compressible, have a very low resistance to shear deformations and expand rapidly on heating. So-called negative thermal expansion behaviour has also been observed, which illustrates just how counterintuitive the physics of molecular crystals can be.25,26 It is worth noting that the mechanical and thermal properties of organic single crystals lie beyond the focus of the present review, although they also highly depend on the molecular arrangements of organic semiconductor crystals.
In inorganic semiconductor crystals, the whole crystal acts like a “single molecule”.27 The charge carriers (hole or electron) are delocalized in the crystals. In organic crystals, on the other hand, the situation is more complicated as mentioned above. The carriers can be localized or delocalized, which is dependent on the packing parameters. The following factors will influence the molecular packing and ultimately the charge transport: (i) the electronic structure of molecules, (ii) the relative positions of the molecules in the crystals and (iii) the morphology variations generated from the static or dynamic deviations of optimal single-crystal structures.28 In order to guarantee efficient charge transport and hence the high performance of organic semiconductor crystals, in addition to the nature of the molecule itself, optimally the molecular packing motifs should have strong and long range intermolecular interactions (C–H⋯π, π–π, etc.). The key point behind this philosophy is that the molecular packing features are to a large extent determined by the intermolecular interactions. Especially for a certain molecule, the lattice component and the electron–lattice interaction Hamiltonian are highly dependent on the packing features. The typical packing motifs in organic semiconductor crystals are summarized in the following. There are a few exceptions which are not elaborated here. One is the sandwich herringbone packing (e.g. perylene29 and 9,10-bis(thiophen-2-ylethynyl)anthracene (BTEA)30), which is not suitable for creating long range efficient charge transport. Another is some specific packing motifs, such as the two-dimensional π–π stacking motif (e.g., 1,2,5,6,7,8,11,12-octachloroperylene-3,4:9,10-tetracarboxylic diimide (Cl8-PTCDI)31 and organoplatinum(II) complexes32). Concerning the relevant typical molecular packing motifs they can be divided into four types, which are shown in a simplified manner in Fig. 2A–D6,33,34 with their representative molecules in Fig. 2E–H: (A) herringbone packing motif without π–π overlap (face-to-face) between adjacent molecules (e.g., Fig. 1E, pentacene35,36); (B) herringbone packing motif with π–π overlap between adjacent molecules (e.g., Fig. 1F, rubrene37,38); (C) lamellar packing motif, one dimensional (1D) π–π stacking (e.g., Fig. 1G, C8-PTCDI39,40); (D) lamellar packing motif, two dimensional (2D) π–π stacking (e.g., Fig. 1H, N,N′-1H,1H-perfluorobutyldicyanoperylene-carboxydi-imide (PDIF-CN2)41,42).
 Fig. 1  Typical molecular packing motifs in organic semiconductor crystals: (A–D) schematic diagrams and (E–H) the representative molecular arrangements. (E) Pentacene, Ref Code PENCEN from CSD; (F) rubrene, Ref Code QQQCIG04 from CSD; (G) C8-PTCDI, Ref Code GILFUD from CSD; (H) PDIF-CN2, Ref Code FEHROA from CSD. All the hydrogen atoms are omitted for clarity. Adapted with permission from ref. 6. Copyright © 2012 American Chemical Society. 
Of these four kinds of packing mode, the π–π stacking is believed to be the most efficient one for transporting charge carriers. This is because the large π–π overlap (a more delocalized state) can stabilize the +1 or −1 charged state and produce a more efficient route for transporting charge carriers from one molecule to its neighbour molecule. In a more delocalized state, the splitting of the HOMO/LUMO will result in a higher electron bandwidth (2J).43 In this case, large polaron or band-like transport (i.e. 2J ≫ A2/2Mω02) can be formed, which is promising for charge transport as mentioned in the physical description of the polaron theory (for more details see Section 2.4). Not surprisingly, much research effort has gone into obtaining π–π stacking in the solid state. The most facile and efficient way is by using chemical modification. This topic has been discussed previously in several reports.6,34 In addition to the semiconductor itself, there are also many other external conditions which can be exploited in order to improve the molecular arrangements, such as by controlling the growth methods44,45 (see Section 3).


2.1.2 Structural anisotropy. 
From the packing motifs, it can be seen that normally the molecular arrangements are different along different orientations. As a result, structural anisotropy is unavoidable. Consequently this is one of the most important properties of single crystals and utilizing this property is crucial for achieving high performance devices and revealing the charge transport mechanisms.46 Such structural anisotropy leads to anisotropic properties from optical absorption,47 thermal expansion,25 exciton diffusion48 to charge transport.9 Our focus now will be on the anisotropy of organic crystals and the effects on charge transport. In contrast to inorganic semiconductors, in which the charge carriers travel “as highly delocalized plane waves in wide bands”,27 the charge carriers in organic semiconductors have to move from one molecule to the neighbouring molecule when passing through the conductive channel. Therefore, the charge transport properties of the crystal are highly dependent on the molecule itself as well as the surrounding molecules, i.e. on the molecular packing features. In the semiclassical electron-transfer theory, the electron-transfer rate is highly dependent on the transfer integral (also called electronic coupling) and reorganization energy (see Section 2.4).28,43,49,50 The transfer integral is defined as the splitting of the frontier molecular orbital between adjacent molecules (the HOMO or LUMO energy levels). This affects the charge carrier transport from one molecule to its neighbour molecule. The reorganization energy describes the energy loss when a charge carrier passes through a molecule.50
It is suggested that increasing the transfer integral and lowering the reorganization energy are helpful in increasing the mobility (see Section 4.1.1.2).43,49,50 Additionally, decreasing the intermolecular distance can increase the splitting of the HOMO and LUMO exponentially. This higher splitting of the energy orbital means higher transfer integral and hence suggests higher mobility. On the other hand, the splitting of the HOMO and LUMO does not increase with the lateral increase of π–π overlap but rather exhibits oscillations in the values. Although the transfer integral does not absolutely increase with the increase of π–π overlap, normally the transfer integral along the direction with π–π overlap is much larger than the other orientation without π–π overlap.52 Also the mobility along this direction is much higher than those along other directions. An example of the mobility-anisotropy of rubrene along the a, b and c axes is presented in Fig. 2. The mobility-anisotropy ratio μb/μa can be as high as 3.537 and μb/μc can be as high as 3.0.51 Similarly, more examples of two-51,53–56 or three-dimensional57–59 anisotropy can also be commonly found (see Section 4.1.3).
 Fig. 2  Mobility-anisotropy of rubrene along the a, b and c axes. (A) Left: Schematic diagram of devices based on rubrene crystals. The electrodes were fabricated on the (100) face. A mobility of 5.3 cm2 (V s)−1 was found along the [010] direction and 1.8 cm2 (V s)−1 along the [001] direction; right: rubrene crystallographic structure along the two directions. Reprinted with permission from ref. 51. Copyright © 2006 American Chemical Society. (B) Polar plot of the mobility in the (001) face, in which the angle was measured between the [010] direction and the conducting channel, indicating that the maximum mobility is along the [010] direction with minimum mobility along the [100] direction and (C) four-probe conductivity as a function of gate voltage along the [010] and [100] directions. Reprinted with permission from ref. 37. Copyright © 2004 American Association for the Advancement of Science. 


2.1.3 Polymorphism. 
Polymorphism and phase transition in organic crystals are typical features of organic crystals.60,61 The polymorphism of organic crystals can be ascribed to the weak interactions between neighbouring molecules and the non-rigid structural properties of the molecules. Hence, the realization of crystals with preferred crystal structure through growth control is prerequisite for achieving high performance devices. The control of crystallization will be discussed in Section 3. Theoretically, when the molecules aggregate to form a crystal, there should be a state with global minimum energy, which is the state for thermodynamically stable crystal configurations (Fig. 3A). However, due to the non-rigid structural properties of the molecules and the weak interactions between neighbour molecules, the bonds in the molecules may stretch, twist or rotate and the molecules may move slightly in the crystals. All of these will result in the formation of a metastable state with relative minimum energy (kinetically stable state) during the crystallization process; in other words a different crystal phase can form. For example, ten crystal phases of CuPc have been reported. These include the lamellar packing motif (α phase) and the slipped π–π stacking motifs (β, γ and ε phases) with different herringbone angles (Fig. 3B).62–65 Normally, the kinetically stable state dominates in the fast process, whereas the thermodynamically stable state occurs in the equilibrium process.66 Therefore, fast crystallization is most likely to provide kinetically favoured crystals. For example, the rotation of the carbon–carbon bond in the BPEA molecule can lead to planar and non-planar states. More volatile solvents facilitate the formation of non-planar BPEA molecules in the crystals.67 The energy difference between these different states may be very small, which increases the possibility of phase transition and leads to more polymorphism. In this case, the molecular arrangements in different crystal structures may differ only slightly in the d-spacing distances or dihedral angles without distorting their packing motif.35 Therefore, different molecular arrangements can be achieved by slight changes of temperature, solvent, concentration or pressure during the crystallization process or even after formation of the crystals.68,69 For example, Jurchescu and co-workers70 reported the crystal structure of rubrene single crystals grown under different temperatures in the 100–300 K range. With increasing temperature, the rubrene molecules packed in the same orthorhombic symmetry and Cmca space group, but with a slight increase in cell volume (from 2730 to 2789 Å3) and a small molecular displacement along the long axis of the molecules in the crystals (from 6.15 to 6.18 Å). On the other hand, as mentioned above, negative thermal expansion behaviour has also been observed. All of these factors have an important impact on the charge transport properties, and this will be discussed in the next section.
 Fig. 3  (A) Thermodynamic and kinetic outcomes of crystallization. Reprinted with permission from ref. 66. Copyright © 2002 Nature Publishing Group. (B) Packing diagrams of four CuPc polymorphs. Reprinted with permission from ref. 65. Copyright © 2004 The Royal Society of Chemistry. 

2.2 Crystal growth features

2.2.1 Crystal engineering. 
The external crystal features and their relationship to internal chemical and crystal structure have long been of great interest to scientists. The systematic use of chemical structure and the intermolecular interactions to design and predict organic semiconductor crystals is of great significance, known as crystal engineering.71,72 The earlier73,74 polyhedral crystal shapes were built according to the stacking of unit cells (Hauy, 1792) with minimal total free energy (Gibbs, 1875):  where γi and Ai are the surface energy and the area of the ith face respectively. Later, it was extended by Wullf's (1901) theorem which suggested that the equilibrium form of the crystals would consist of such faces: having the distances from the growth origin which are proportional to the surface energies of the faces.75 Then the morphology simulation was proposed by Bravais (1866), Friedel (1907), Donnay and Harker (1937), quoted as Bravais–Friedel–Donnay–Harker (BFDH) law, which makes use of the framework of the crystal lattice but doesn’t count the atom or bond type and the partial charge.76 Further studies of the energetic effect on morphology were going into the evaluation of the interaction energies between non-bonded atoms or molecules. Then the lattice energy is the sum of the intermolecular interactions between a central molecule and each surrounding molecule within the crystal lattice. The quantification of the interaction energy was drawn by using the assumption that the surface energy is directly related to the chemical bond energies and the uninterrupted chains of strong intermolecular interactions. They defined that the formation of the crystals is determined by two energies: one is the layer energy in the slice (or face) and the other is the attachment energy which is the energy to attach the slice on the growing crystal surface. The growing rate of the face is proportional to the attachment energy. The attachment energy (AE) model assumes that the surface is a perfect lattice face and there is no surface rearrangement and relaxation, which is not likely real.77 In a real state, there is one interface between the solid and the fluid (liquid, gas or even solid), which exhibits an order–disorder phase transition under a critical condition (e.g. temperature or concentration). This surface roughening was introduced by Burton and Jackson et al., and was developed via the Monte Carlo model to the Ising model. For the temperature-dependent system, the critical temperature can be calculated by using the critical value of the average dimensionless bond energy. Simply speaking, in the Ising model, by identifying the strong bonds in the crystal structure, calculating the relative bond strengths, derivation and conversion of the connected nets (given by the molecules as the nodes and the intermolecular interactions as the links between nodes), the critical temperature and the theoretical morphology can be calculated and computed. Thus the morphology provided by the Ising model can be regarded as stable crystal morphology, which is close to the growth conditions for kinetic roughening. For vapour sublimation methods, the external factors are normally negligible, and hence are usually best comparable with the calculated crystal habits. On the other hand, for solution-grown crystals, the interactions between the solute and the solvent at the crystal/solution interfaces may influence the shape of the crystals.73,78 In the surface roughening model, the interaction between the solvent and the specific crystal faces reduces the interfacial tension and leads to a rough surface. The rough surface can promote the growth of this affected surface, which becomes morphologically less important. In contrast, the inhibition model was also proposed to predict the effect of the solvent on the crystal growth. According to the inhibition model, the adsorption of the solvent molecules upon the growing surfaces, forming an interfacial layer, inhibits the growth of the specific faces, because the growth of the solute molecules on the surfaces needs a desolvation process. The resultant affected surface will be morphologically important. In order to reduce the kinetic influence during the crystallization process on the morphology of the crystal, low supersaturations can be adopted. Similarly, the impurities can also change the growth rates and the crystal habit, with inhibition or enhanced nucleation effects at concentrations as low as 10−9 M. These will be further discussed in Section 3.


2.2.2 Morphology–structure relationship. 
For organic semiconductor crystals, there are several crystal growth features, which should be taken into consideration:24 (1) the crystal structures are usually of low symmetry (e.g. triclinic, monoclinic or orthorhombic) and the adjacent molecules are packed through non-bonded interactions; (2) the low crystalline symmetry will result in anisotropy of the physicochemical properties, e.g. the electric conductivity; (3) the intermolecular interactions are very weak, and hence lead to polymorphism and phase transitions (see Section 2.1.3). The aforementioned comparisons between different morphology simulation models74 have shown that for these organic semiconductor crystals, which are isotropic but without significant dipole effects, both the BFDH and attachment energy (AE) model can provide a good fitting to the observed morphologies. There is no obvious difference between the two models. Meanwhile, the BFDH model can be used to identify important morphologies through refinement by using energy calculations. The BFDH rule76 is widely adopted as a means for predicting the morphology of organic semiconductor crystals. Indeed it is found to work well in predicting the morphology of crystals, an example of which is the equilibrium crystal shape (ECS).30,75,79–81 For example, the shape of dibenzo[d,d′]thieno [3,2-b;4,5-b′]dithiophene (DBTDT) crystals grown by using the physical vapour transport (PVT) technique conforms well with the theoretically predicted morphology with respect to the aspects of not only shapes but also the exact dihedral angles between the relevant faces. These are shown in Fig. 4A and B.54 Similarly, hexathiapentacene (HTP) crystals have been found to be prone to growing along the [010] direction,82 which agrees with the theoretically predicted elongation orientation, i.e. the direction with the strongest intermolecular interactions (Fig. 4C and D).
 Fig. 4  (A) A typical DBTDT crystal with shape and dihedral angles coincident with (B) its theoretically predicted morphology. Adapted with permission from ref. 54. Copyright © 2009 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. (C) A typical HTP crystal showing a preferred orientation in the [010] direction which agrees well with the (D) theoretically predicted elongation orientation. Reprinted with permission from ref. 82. Copyright © 2007 American Chemical Society. 
According to the aforementioned rules, if the interaction in one direction (such as the π–π interactions or hydrogen bonds83) in a crystal is stronger than those in other directions, then the crystals tend to grow along this direction, forming morphologies consisting of one dimensional (1D) rods, needles or wires or two dimensional (2D) ribbons or disks. There are many examples of crystals existing as one or two dimensional morphologies:84 rubrene,38 9,10-bis(phenylethynyl)anthracene (BPEA),30 9,10-bis(naphthalen-2-ylethynyl)anthracene (BNEA),30 1,2-di(anthracen-9-yl)ethyne (H-Ant),85 9,10-diphenylanthracene (DPA),86 6H-pyrrolo-[3,2-b:4,5-b′] bis[1,4]benzothiazine (PBBTZ),80syn-bis(benzothieno)thiophene (syn-BBTT),52 dithiophene-tetra thiafulvalene (DT-TTF),87 triisopropylsilylethynyl pentacene (TIPS-PEN),88 copper phthalocyanine (CuPc),62,89 copper hexadecafluorophthalocyanine (F16CuPc),90,91 6,13-bis((triisopropylsilyl)ethynyl)-5,7,12,14-tetraazapentacene (TIPS-TAP),92 perylo[1,12-b,c,d] thiophene (PET),93 perylo[1,12-b,c,d]selenophene (PESE),94 hexathiapentacene (HTP),82 perylenetetracarboxylicdiimide (PTCDI),79,95 poly(3-hexylthiophene) (P3HT),96 thioacetate ended poly(para-phenylene ethynylene) (TA-PPE),97 5,10,15,20-tetra(4-pyridyl)porphyrin (H2TPyP)98 and others. Almost all of these semiconductors possess π–π stacking in their molecular arrangements (see packing features in Section 2.1).
On the other hand, if there is no orientation with dominant intermolecular interactions which means the interactions are comparable with each other along at least two orientations (C–H⋯π, or van der Waals force between alkyl groups), then it is likely that crystals present with 2D (ribbons or disks) or three dimensional (3D) (plates or particles) morphologies. For example, the interactions along the [100] (edge-to-edge) and the [110] (edge-to-face) directions in the a–b plane of dinaphtho[2,3-b:2′,3′-f]-thieno[3,2-b]thiophene crystals possess similar effective intermolecular contacts. This can be ascribed to the S⋯S interaction which enhances the edge-to-edge interaction and results in a balanced 2D electronic structure and 2D morphology.99 Likewise, there are extensive examples of this kind of materials.84 Of special note are those that possess a herringbone packing motif without π–π stacking and sandwich herringbone packing (see packing features in Section 2.1). Examples are pentacene,36,100–102 tetracene,9 anthracene,103 di(phenylvinyl) anthracene (DPV-ant),55anti-bis(benzothieno)thiophene (anti-BBTT, DBTDT),52,54 BTEA,30 C60104 and so forth. Therefore, by adjusting the intermolecular interactions through cautious tuning of the chemical structure, it is possible to obtain different self-assembled morphologies. For example, two cis–trans isomers52 of BBTT formed 1D and 2D self-assembled structures, respectively. This could be ascribed to the different packing modes in their single crystals, namely with or without π–π stacking.16 Similarly, by tuning the substitutes of anthracene, morphologies ranging from 1D to 3D micro/nanostructures were obtained from BPEA, BNEA to BTEA.30 Similar results can also be found in anthracene derivatives by adjusting the intermolecular hydrogen bonds.103 Hence, it can be expected that ultra-large 2D crystals can be obtained if the intermolecular interaction of one direction (e.g. van der Waals force between alkyl groups) is much weaker than those of the other orientations. This will also be discussed in Section 3.6. This probably is the reason why organic semiconductor molecules tend to form in a layer-by-layer growth manner (see below for details). This can also explain why 1,4-bis((5′-hexyl-2,2′-bithiophen-5-yl)ethynyl)benzene (HTEB) (a linear molecule with two long alkyl groups at both ends) presents as 2D large millimetre-sized crystal films.105 This interpretation can be proved by X-ray diffraction (XRD) results and the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns. The layer thickness of the crystals is close to the long axis of the molecules, which strongly suggests that the crystals have a tendency to grow in the plane perpendicular to the long axis of the molecules. Along the long axis of the molecules, there are only weak van der Waals forces between the alkyl groups. These results also indicate that the interaction along the long axis of the molecules is much weaker than that in the other directions (C–H⋯π interactions). As mentioned above, many external conditions, such as growth methods, temperature, solvent and concentration, can also affect the crystal growth. All these can lead to different morphologies. These will be further discussed in Section 3.


2.2.3 Structural identification. 
It is important to verify the crystal structure and identify the growth direction of organic crystals as a means for revealing the intrinsic reasons for preference of specific morphologies and anisotropy of charge transport. In-depth knowledge of this is vital for further guiding the applications of organic semiconductors. XRD (including single crystal X-ray diffraction for bulk single crystals, wide angle X-ray scattering (WAXS) and grazing incidence X-ray diffraction (GIXD) for thin film single crystals), synchrotron radiation and neutron diffraction techniques have made a valuable contribution to examining the molecular packing in crystals.106,107 Especially, GIXD provides a powerful way to identify the in-plane and out-of-plane orientations of even micro/nano crystals that are common for micro-devices based on organic semiconductors.108–110
Another potential method is to use atomic force microscopy (AFM), which has high resolution on the nanometer scale and can give a precise image of the crystal, especially the height profile. By measuring the height of the crystal steps, combining the length or width of the molecule, information about the crystal morphology and growth directions can be successfully obtained. One example is that these data can confirm either “face on” or “edge on” growth mode on the substrate, such as for graphene which possesses a “face-on” mode on the substrate. An estimated layer thickness of 0.4–0.7 nm111,112 was found by using AFM images. Fig. 5 shows an example of how to estimate the “edge on” mode adopted in the PDIF-CN2 crystals. Based on the experimental results that the height differences of the different layers are 1.9 × 1 nm, 1.9 × 3 nm, 1.9 × 5 nm respectively and the c-axis length of the primitive cell is 1.9 nm, it is reasonable to conclude that the crystals grow in a manner such that the c-axis is perpendicular to the substrate.113 Similar results can also be found for rubrene,114–116 HTEB,105 and dioctylbenzothienobenzothiophene (C8-BTBT)117 crystals among others. It should be noted that caution is needed when adopting this method and the height of the step or crystal should be as close as possible to the length of the molecule. The possible tilt of the molecule or unavoidable crystal defects will obscure the estimation or even give a wrong interpretation. Such an AFM technique can also be used to reveal the morphology of screw dislocations118 and the electronic disorder can be manifested as potential variations by scanning Kelvin probe microscopy.110
 Fig. 5  (A and B) AFM images of PDIF-CN2 crystal films, and (C) cross-sectional profile along the line in (B). (D) Schematic illustration of the crystal structure of PDIF-CN2. Reprinted with permission from ref. 113. Copyright © 2011 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) is another very useful technique for obtaining information about the growth and morphology of crystals. Different from AFM images, SEM images hardly give height information regarding the micro/nanostructures. However, SEM techniques can provide shape images as in AFM images. The growth direction can be inferred from the crystal shape and crystal structure according to two rules: (1) the crystals must have regular shapes, whose surface consists of lattice planes; (2) the dihedral angle between two specific lattice planes is fixed. Therefore, by using BFDH methods to simulate crystal shapes and the angles, every crystal surface can be identified in terms of precise Miller indices. Examples can be found in Fig. 4A and B.54 Of course, the obtained crystal morphology may be different from the simulated crystal shape because, as mentioned earlier, the crystal growth can be affected by many external factors such as temperature, solvent and solution concentration. Therefore, it should be noted that sometimes it is difficult to determine the orientation by using this method.
Another efficient way to verify a crystal's orientation is by using a combination of its transmission electron microscopy (TEM) image and the corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns together with crystal parameters. There are two methods to index the SAED pattern with the Miller indices: (1) first calculating the spacing distance (d-spacing) by measuring the lattice distance in the reciprocal lattice points (SAED pattern), which can be calibrated by the SAED pattern of polycrystalline Au films (the diameter of diffraction rings is constant), and then comparing the Miller indices in the table of reflection parameters; (2) first simulating the probable crystal growth direction and then indexing the other directions by utilizing the distance and angle relationship between this supposed direction and the other directions. If all the angles and distances are coincident with the crystal structure, then the whole SAED pattern can be indexed. Fig. 4C shows an example of how this method is used to explore the growth direction of the crystals. A more complex example is shown in Fig. 6, in which CuPc, F16CuPc and CuPc/F16CuPc heterojunction crystals are studied.119 In fact, if it is possible to obtain high resolution TEM (HRTEM) images, then the growth direction could be identified by the HRTEM image directly120–123 due to its crystallographic structure image at an atomic scale. It follows that if atomic scale HRTEM images could be obtained, then by using the d-spacing distances, that would give very clear crystal growth directions. Sometimes, the sensitivity of organic crystals to the high energy electron beam makes HRTEM, TEM and SAED techniques full of challenges for the identification of crystal growth features.124
 Fig. 6  The growth direction of (A) CuPc, (B) F16CuPc and (C) CuPc/F16CuPc heterojunction crystals obtained by a combination of TEM images and the corresponding SAED patterns. Reprinted with permission from ref. 119. Copyright © 2010 American Chemical Society. 

2.3 Non-ideal features
In the previous sections, although we have not mentioned the issue of defects in the crystals, it has to be acknowledged that they are actually unavoidable.20,125 It is pertinent therefore that we present a short summary of the defects here. In reality, crystals without the presence of any defects do not exist. The traps (e.g. oxidized molecules) can act as point defects,126 affecting the charge transport properties. Crystals with minimized impurities and defects are, therefore, ideal for revealing the intrinsic properties of organic semiconductors, investigating the important factors for high performance devices and demonstrating the fundamental physics. However, defects are not only of significance for examining the fundamental physics but are also useful for achieving specific applications.127 For example, the intentionally induced disorder of the crystals by using fluoro self-assembly monolayers (SAMs),128 ferroelectric material129 or by photooxidation130,131 opens new opportunities for exploring the electronic states in the crystals and various applications (e.g. increasing the conductivity or memory application). Moreover, some features, such as regular shapes, smooth surfaces, terraces and steps, are useful for determining the molecular packing and arrangements in the crystals (e.g.Fig. 5). In addition, the defect density will change the mobility dependence on temperature, providing a powerful tool for studying the charge transport mechanism in organic semiconductor crystals (see Section 2.4).125,132 Simply speaking, it was ever generally recognized that the charge transport mechanism in organic crystals can be understood through small-polaron theory: at low temperature, the charge carrier transport occurs by band-like transport and the mobility decreases with increasing temperature, while at high temperature, the charge carrier transport occurs by small-polaron hopping from one localized site to another site and the mobility increases with increasing temperature. However, the purification of the materials can change this temperature-dependence relationship, which suggests that the thermally activated mobility is due to the defects rather than the small-polaron hopping. This will be discussed in the next section. Grain boundaries, which are a kind of defects, make it a bit of a challenge for crystalline films to be recognized as single crystals. Studies133,134 suggest that the highest density of defects exists at the grain boundaries of the crystalline films, which definitely will limit the charge transport135,136 (Fig. 7A). Similarly, polymers, which do not have accurate molecular weight (length), make the growth of polymeric crystals full of challenges. In some reports, long-ranged, ordered polymeric aggregations are regarded as crystals.96 Finally, thermal lattice fluctuation is not negligible, which will affect the transfer integrals due to their sensitivity to the relative positions of adjacent molecules. In summary, non-ideal features widely exist in organic crystals.
 Fig. 7  (A) Representative trap density of states (DOS) data in small molecule organic semiconductors. The trap densities of single crystal (SC) FETs (red lines) are much lower than those of thin film transistors (TFTs, black lines), indicating that the structural defects such as grain boundaries tend to dominate in thin films. The trap densities of bulk single crystals (blue lines) are lower than those of SC-FETs. This suggests that the trap densities are much more likely to be on the surface or interface. Importantly, the highly hydrophobic CytopTM gate dielectric can significantly reduce the trap density. Adapted with permission from ref. 133. Copyright © 2010 The American Physical Society. (B) Distribution of band and localized states for polycrystalline pentacene. Acceptor-like states are formed around 0.29 eV above the VBM. Reprinted with permission from ref. 139. Copyright © 2009 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. VB: valence band, CB: conduction band. 
The traps in the crystals mainly originate from the presence of chemical impurities, structural imperfections and thermal fluctuations.132,133 Chemical impurities normally originate from the reactants, by-products, degradation or external species (e.g. moisture, oxygen gas, etc.) which exist during the process of crystal growth, device fabrication or characterization (Fig. 7A). The presence of impurities can manifest itself in various ways.133,137–141 For example, oxygen in pentacene crystals can exist as an oxygen intermolecular bridge, in which the oxygen atom is covalently bonded to carbon atoms on the center rings of two adjacent pentacene molecules. The active trap is located above the valence band maximum (VBM) around 0.3–0.4 eV (Fig. 7B).137,139 Water molecules stay in the intact molecular form between the molecular layers of pentacene.137 It should be noted that whether a chemical impurity acts as trap or not depends on the accessible energy levels. In other words, electronic levels of the trap should lie within the band gap of the semiconductor. Otherwise, the chemical impurities cannot act as trapping centers. Instead they only introduce local deformation of the semiconductor lattice and form scattering centers.132,133 Besides the aforementioned active trap in pentacene that is located above the VBM around 0.3–0.4 eV, an example is also reported in phthalocyanine crystals,142 in which traps with a density of 1019 cm−3 located 0.38 eV below the conduction band (CB) have been observed.
There is no doubt that the trap density is highly dependent on the growth techniques employed. For example, melting and vapour techniques can provide single crystals with higher quality than those from solution-processing techniques because of the absence of external species. Moreover, vapour-grown crystals possess a lower density of defects than those resulting from the melting techniques. The reason is that higher temperature is adopted in the melting methods. In this situation the impurities may form eutectic mixtures with the semiconductors,143 and degradation may occur.144,145 Besides, the slow self-assembly processes can facilitate the formation of an ordered structure and lead to a lower defect density. For example, it has been found that rapid growth of crystals will lead to a large density of screw dislocations,118 which can be revealed by using morphological characterization (AFM). These issues will be further discussed in Section 3. In addition, in order to reduce the defect density, the optimization of device fabrication and characterization146,147 should also be taken into consideration. Issues such as the utilization of polymeric (e.g. cytop133) or air/vacuum dielectric layers (Fig. 7A), or the modification of the dielectric layer by using SAMs,148 will be mentioned in Section 4.1. Typical SAMs include octyltrichlorosilane (OTS), decyltriethoxysilane (DTS), octadecyltrichlorosilane (ODTS), octadecyltrimethoxysilane (OTMS), β-phenylethyltrimethoxylsilane (β-PTS), hexamethyldisilazane (HMDS), heptadecafluorodecyltriethoxysilane (FDTS), etc. It is believed that the low dielectric constant and high water contact angle of the dielectric layer can facilitate the formation of low trap density, due to the low dipolar disorder and the water repellent nature. Podzorov and co-workers149 reported a trap healing method for reducing the surface traps by using surface functionalization. An inert non-conjugated polymer, perfluoropolyether (PFPE), was deposited at the surface of organic crystals, which can reduce the traps of pristine and photo-oxidized crystals, resulting in low trap densities and high mobilities. The exposure of crystals to light or air may lead to a sharp change in conductivity. The deposition of fluorinated SAMs on semiconducting crystals can chemically dope the semiconductor via a charge-transfer doping process, showing a surface potential shift150 as high as 540 meV and a 104-fold increase in the surface conductivity.128 Similar phenomena can also be observed under the conditions of humidity, ethanol, acetone, ammonia or other gas vapours. This suggests potential applications of defects for light detection or gas sensing, and these will be demonstrated in Section 4.1.4.
Annealing is another method for reducing the density of defects. This includes thermal and solvent vapour annealing, which can remove the chemical impurities, reconstruct the molecular arrangement and decrease the structural imperfections. Solvent vapour annealing has even been adopted as a method for growing single crystals, and this will be discussed in Section 3.3.4. However, it should be noted that appropriate utilization of impurities can lead to high performance. For example, it is difficult to eliminate 6,13-pentacenequinone (PQ), which is the reactant for producing pentacene and can be reintroduced by oxidation of pentacene. However, by utilizing PQ as a dielectric layer, the PQ molecules around the interface between the semiconducting and insulating layer will provide an intimate contact between them, rather than acting as impurities. This in turn leads to a high mobility for pentacene devices.36
The trap density can be probed by methods such as the space charge limited current (SCLC) method,132,140,151,152 photoluminescence measurements and field-effect transistor measurements (see Section 4.1). For example, the SCLC method153 is based on the analysis of the current density (J) versus the applied voltage (V) characteristics. At low voltages, the J–V curves are linear, i.e. showing ohmic behaviour. However, at high bias voltages, the J–V curves become space-charge-limited due to the injection of the charge carriers. If the contact between the electrode and the crystal is ohmic and the current is transport-limited, the SCLC J is given by the Mott–Gurney equation:  where ε, μ and d are the permittivity, mobility and thickness of the crystal, and θ is a factor that refers to the trap density. This factor represents the number of free carriers to the total number of carriers. After purification (by vapour sublimation), the trap density of pentacene132 can be reduced down to 1011 traps per cm3. Otherwise, the trap density can vary exponentially133 to 1021 traps per cm3 even in single crystals without any grain boundaries, due to the presence of chemical impurities, structural imperfections or thermal fluctuations.141 Normally, the trap density on the surface of the crystals is much higher than that in the bulk crystals, and the traps increase significantly at the grain boundaries and the interfaces between the crystals and the dielectric layers (or electrodes) (Fig. 7A). These observations reflect the importance of device physics in achieving high performance devices.148 The details will be presented in Section 4.
2.4 Charge transport features
Crystals provide a useful tool for revealing the charge transport mechanism due to their highly ordered structures and minimal defects. The charge transport situation in organic semiconductors is quite different from that in their inorganic counterparts. They are much more complex due to the weak and non-covalent bonds between neighbouring molecules. There are a lot of proposed theories which evaluate the charge carrier transport mechanisms in organic semiconductors, including the band-like transport, small polaron hopping, multiple trapping and release (MTR) models, and their revisions. Because there are a lot of literature reports addressing this topic,19,34,49,125,153–157 we will confine ourselves to briefly and concisely summarizing the recent progress in charge transport mechanisms, their features and working conditions.

2.4.1 Band transport and classic small-polaron hopping. 
In the earlier studies, it is generally well accepted that the charge transport mechanism in organic semiconductors mostly obeys the Holstein small-polaron theory.21,22,125,157–159 The basic features of this model may be written as  which contains three terms: the electronic component He, the lattice component HL and the electron–lattice interaction Hint. The wave function (an(x1,…,xN)) is a function of the lattice-vibration coordinates (x1,…,xN) and a discrete electronic coordinate. The sum over m describes the lattice Hamiltonian, which consists of the vibratory kinetic and potential energies of the molecules in the absence of the electron. The term −Axn represents the electron–lattice interaction, being a linear function of the vibration coordinate of the occupied site. Finally, the term proportional to the electronic overlap integral J gives the motion of the electron through the lattice. It should be noted that the total Hamiltonian cannot be studied exactly and hence only a qualitative expression is given here. According to this theory, (1) at low temperature, charge transport is similar to that which occurs in inorganic semiconductor crystals. In other words, the charge carriers are delocalized in the wide bands “as highly delocalized plane waves in wide bands”.27 The Hamiltonian in this case can be described as the electronic Hamiltonian with a part of nuclear Hamiltonian. The effective intermolecular coupling (or transfer integral) τ′ can be described as  with Nω = [exp(hω/kBT) − 1]−1, ω the frequency of the nuclear motion and g a parameter of quantification of the electron–phonon coupling term. Where the charge transport in ultrapure organic semiconductor crystals is concerned, the carrier in the bands (narrow bands) is like a particle with an effective mass (large polaron, delocalized),160m* = h2/(2|τ|L2), which is inversely proportional to the transfer integral (L is the distance between collision sites of the carrier). In this case, the propagation of this particle with an effective mass will be limited by the vibrations of the crystal lattice (phonons) or impurities that scatter the carriers. Then the mobility in this limit can be written asμ = etS/m*where tS is the average time between collisions and e is the electron charge. Because the vibration and the scattering become severe with increasing temperature (τ′ decreases and m* increases correspondingly), the mobility reduces with the increase in temperature (Fig. 8A), which is regarded as the signature property of band transport.125 (2) With the increase in temperature, the vibration of the crystal lattice and the scattering become even more vigorous so that the band narrowing becomes more pronounced. The result is that the polaronic band vanishes (τ′ → 0) and the effective mass becomes infinite. Then the carrier is no longer delocalized and the propagation of the polaron (small polaron, localized in one molecule) resembles more a hopping from one site to another site. Because this hopping transport is assisted by phonons, the mobility should be thermally activated which increases with increasing temperature. In other words, Arrhenius-like temperature dependence is observed. In the semiclassical electron-transfer theory, the electron-transfer rate43,161kET can be described to a good approximation as  where T is the absolute temperature, λ and τ are the reorganization energy and transfer integral (also called electronic coupling), and h and kB are the Plank and Boltzmann constants. This is the well-known Marcus equation.
 Fig. 8  (A) Electron and hole mobilities in ultrapure naphthalene as a function of temperature examined using TOF measurements; they exhibit a negative correlation with temperature. Reprinted with permission from ref. 169. Copyright © 1985 The American Physical Society. (B) Temperature dependence of carrier mobility for different insulators based on field-effect transistor measurements, which shows a transition from negative to positive correlation with temperature, with increasing dielectric constant. The mobility increases with decreasing dielectric constant, indicating that the charge carrier transports as a polaron. Reprinted with permission from ref. 166. Copyright © 2006 Nature Publishing Group. 


2.4.2 New insights. 
Recent theoretical and experimental results suggest that small-polaron hopping does not in fact exist, especially in high performance devices.161 The reason is that the small polaron can only be formed when the electronic coupling between neighbouring molecules (transfer integrals) is much smaller than the polaron binding energy (Holstein coupling): 2J ≪ A2/2Mω02. However, the transfer integrals are always found to be comparable to or even larger than the electron–phonon coupling (Peierls coupling).28 Moreover, the excited levels of the high frequency intramolecular vibrations, which are the main contribution to the polaron binding energy, are thermally inaccessible even at room temperature. Another proof is that the purification of the materials can change this temperature-dependence relationship from thermally activated mobility (signature of traditional small-polaron hopping) to the signature temperature-dependence of band-like transport. This suggests that the charge carriers are not localized in one molecule and the thermally activated mobility is due to the defects rather than the small-polaron hopping. All these research studies indicate that temperature-independence mobility or activated transport mobility is more likely due to the structural disorder and chemical defects,143 rather than to small-polaron hopping.26,125,162–164
When a charge carrier passes through a crystal lattice, the polarization of the charge carrier will lead to deformation of the surrounding molecules. The charge carrier together with the deformation of the surrounding molecules is called a polaron. The existence of a polaron can be confirmed by the dependence of the mobility on the dielectric constant. Theoretically, the mobility should increase with increasing dielectric constant (ε) because a high ε can result in a high induced electric field at the same voltage.165 However, the experimental results are inconsistent with this theory. On the other hand, the polaron can be used to solve this controversy. Because of its efficient mass, the propagation of the polaron is more hindered by a large dielectric constant, and this in turn leads to negative coefficient dependence of the mobility on the dielectric constant (examples can be found in Fig. 8B, from Vacuum to Ta2O5, the dielectric constant decreases successively, with mobility increasing on the contrary).166
If the charge carriers are delocalized (i.e. a large polaron is formed), the polaron will move as is the propagation of the carrier in the band. The band-like transport was widely accepted in earlier studies as the intrinsic transport mode of organic semiconductor crystals. This was based on the time-of-flight (TOF) results obtained by probing the charge transport in ultrapure organic semiconductor crystals at low carrier densities. The TOF method introduced by Leblanc and Kepler167,168 can investigate the drift mobility of the charge carriers by applying a pulse light to the crystals and by analysing the photoconductivity evolution of the crystals. It should be pointed out that the drift mobility is dependent on the drift distance (the thickness of the crystal), the applied potential and the drift time.168 From the TOF measurements, the carrier mobilities exhibited anisotropy along different directions and they increased with the decrease in temperature at temperature around RT (μ ∝ Tn, with n < 0, e.g. n = −2.9 for naphthalene in the temperature range of 150–300 K, Fig. 8A).169 This suggests that the charge carriers are transporting in a band-like transport mode.151,170–175 This feature can also be observed using SCLC measurements132,151,176 and ultrafast optical pump-terahertz probe techniques.170,177 Using single crystals as the semiconductor layer in transistors, in which grain boundaries are eliminated and defects and traps are minimized, it has also been possible to observe intrinsic charge transport. The mobility anisotropy feature178 has been studied widely and has already been mentioned in the molecular packing features (see Section 2.1.2). The temperature-dependence mobility manifests itself widely,179,180 even in polycrystalline thin films.181 For example, by using rubrene single crystals as the semiconductor and air as the dielectric layers, Podzorov and co-workers182 reported that the charge transport in highly pure rubrene single crystals is intrinsic transport, i.e. polaronic band-like transport at high temperature (150–300 K). The Hall effect (see Section 4.1.1.4) is another feature of band-like transport, which can reveal the intrinsic mobility, even if the charge transport is affected by the trapping. The reason is that the trapping charges cannot contribute to the Hall voltage due to its zero momentum (no Lorentz force). The Hall effect observed in rubrene also exhibited an increase in mobility with the decrease in temperature, suggesting that the intrinsic charge transport is in fact band-like transport.20,183–186
A thermally activated mobility correlation is also observed in single crystal devices. In the earlier research,187,188 this was regarded as a feature of small-polaron hopping and small-polaron hopping was considered as the carrier transport mechanism in devices with low mobility.27 However, as mentioned above, in recent years it has been acceptable that small polarons are unlikely to exist in organic semiconductor crystals, especially for the intrinsic charge transport in high performance devices. Podzorov et al. pointed out that thermally activated transport in pure crystals under low temperature or in doped rubrene crystals is probably due to the multiple trapping and release (MTR) of carriers. According to the MTR model, crystal defects, such as impurities, structural disorder or surface states, induce the formation of localized electronic states (traps). Two kinds of traps are suggested: these include the deep trap (>few kT from the HOMO or LUMO energy level) and the shallow trap (<few kT). When the gate voltage is lower than the threshold voltage (VT), the charge carriers will be localized in the deep traps and this shifts the Fermi level to the HOMO (or LUMO) energy level. Hence, deep traps only affect the threshold voltage while not determining the mobility. When the gate voltage is higher than VT, the deep traps are filled and then charge carriers become localized in the shallow traps. In this situation, the trapping and release of the traps dominate the charge transport process. Because temperature can strongly affect the rate of trapping and release, mobility is also highly dependent on temperature. At high temperature, the period during which a polaron is trapped (τtr) in the shallow trap is much smaller than when it moves between neighbouring traps (τdiff). Therefore, the effective mobility is close to the intrinsic trap-free mobility (μ0):  The charge carriers therefore transport the same as that seen in intrinsic band-like transport with threshold-less operation, anisotropic intrinsic mobility and band-like temperature-dependence mobility. However, at low temperature, τtr is much larger than τdiff, so the mobility can be described by the following equation:  The proof of the MTR mode can be found by observing a decrease in mobility with cooling (the Arrhenius dependence μ(T) = μ0exp(−T0/T)) and the vanishing of the anisotropy.182 Similar work has also been reported for tetracene with non-monotonous temperature dependence.151,189 On the other hand, from TOF results obtained using DPA crystals, Pflaum and co-workers173 insisted that the temperature independence of the mobility behaviour of DPA at low temperature (<100 K) is due to the intrinsic band-like transport, in which the charge carriers are still delocalized and the mobility saturation at low temperature originates from the finite band-width. The Hall-effect is also observed in some crystalline thin films,190 with thermally activated mobility.191,192 However, scientists found that the charge carriers are delocalized in the single crystals or the crystal domain of the thin films (also see below). They concluded that the charge transport in the domains is qualitatively the same as that found in the single crystals, i.e. band-like transport. On the other hand, the decrease in mobility with cooling is due to the dynamic disorder, fluctuations, or the grain boundaries, rather than small-polaron hopping. By analysing the onset temperature (T*) where the temperature correlation changed from negative to positive (Fig. 9), Morpurgo and co-workers193 claimed that the transport in single crystals is band-like transport, which exhibit an increase in mobility upon lowering the temperature. A further reduction in temperature results in a decrease of mobility, which is due to the extrinsic disorders. From Fig. 9A–C, the material with a longer chain length always shows smaller T* and the onset temperature increases from vacuum, cytop to PMMA dielectric layers. These results fit well with a phenomenological mobility-edge model that describes band-like transport. T* is smaller when the band-tail width (Et) is smaller, i.e. the strength of disorder is smaller (Fig. 9D). The calculations also show that the disorder is largely due to the potential fluctuations generated by permanent, randomly oriented electrical dipoles or charged species adsorbed on the crystal surface.
 Fig. 9  Temperature dependent mobility of PDIF-CN2 (full squares) and its derivatives with two more CF2 units (open circles) with (A) vacuum, (B) cytop and (C) PMMA gate dielectrics. (D) Plot of T* versus Et extracted from panels A–C. Adapted with permission from ref. 193. Copyright © 2014 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Usually, it is generally recognized that the charge carriers are delocalized in band-like transport (extended-state band conduction). However, some research has suggested that crystals with localized charge carriers can possess band-like transport. The localization or delocalization state of charge carriers can be investigated by using electron spin resonance (ESR) and optical charge modulation spectroscopy (CMS). ESR spectroscopy128,194,195 is a technique by which the electronic states of unpaired electrons can be analysed. It can be used to investigate the spin density distribution of paramagnetic carriers and probe the molecular orientation by observing the anisotropic and different linewidth of the ESR signal due to hyperfine coupling of the π electrons. A study of pentacene crystalline thin films suggests that at room temperature the charge carriers are localized within about 10 molecules.194 Optical charge modulation spectroscopy (CMS) is an electro-optical method for the in situ characterization of gate-induced charge carriers. According to the CMS data for single crystalline TIPS-PEN thin films (the grain size is much larger than the channel dimension), it has been suggested that the localized charge carriers can also undergo band-like transport and that their transport is limited by thermal lattice fluctuations.181 An attempt to underscore this contradiction is proposed by extending the polaron band theory to non-local electron–phonon coupling.125,157,163,196–201 The non-local electron–phonon coupling also leads to narrowing of the polaronic band and a negative temperature coefficient on the mobility. However, this does not change the qualitative description of the polaronic band model. With increasing temperature, the dynamic disorder becomes dominant and the electronic states become localized by disorder. By assuming that the electronic dynamics are much faster than the lattice vibrational frequencies which are responsible for the intermolecular coupling, Fratini and Ciuchi202 observed the coexistence of coherent band-like states and incoherent localized dynamic disorder states. This crossover region is extremely promising because it solves the contradiction between the localized states and temperature-dependence mobility.
As mentioned above, the band narrowing was regarded as a prominent feature of the Holstein small polaron model. With increasing temperature, the band becomes narrower and the transport switches from band-like transport to hopping mode. The band narrowing with increasing temperature can be observed by using angle-resolved photoelectron spectroscopy (ARPES) measurements.203–206 ARPES provides a powerful tool for obtaining the bandwidth and the effective mass (m*). In addition, the mean free path of the carriers can be obtained by measuring the energy of the photoemitted electrons as a function of their angular distribution. The mean free path of the carriers (l) is defined as  where kB is the Boltzmann constant, T is the absolute temperature and τ is the transfer integral. The energy dispersion of rubrene single crystals observed by ARPES showed that the mean free path of holes in rubrene crystals is a few times longer than the crystal lattice constant. This indicates that the holes are delocalized over several molecules and act almost as free carriers. Recent calculations unexpectedly demonstrate that the bandwidth narrowing is unlikely to be caused by electron–phonon coupling and the aforementioned non-local electron–phonon coupling probably results in thermal broadening of the bandwidth.200,207 Li et al. believed that the bandwidth narrowing originates from the thermal expansion of the crystal lattice.26 Remarkably, the transfer integrals, which closely follow the temperature dependence of the energy bands, also decrease along the a axis with increasing temperature, although the crystal shows a negative thermal expansion along this direction. This suggests that the transfer integral is highly dependent on the relevant molecular orbital overlap rather than simply on the spatial overlap between adjacent molecules.
In summary, the study of charge transport in organic semiconductors has progressed rapidly, not only based on theories but also on experimental data. Recently, it has become acceptable that (1) the charge transport mechanism at low temperature is similar to conventional theories, i.e. the charge carriers are delocalized in the polaronic band; (2) with increasing temperature, the charge carriers become localized by the thermal disorder, rather than localized as small polarons. However, the analyses and final interpretation of the charge transport mechanism are often obscured by many factors.166,180,208 These include the impurities, defects, the contacts, the phase transitions under different temperatures among others and all of these make the intrinsic charge transport picture controversial and unsatisfactory. Fortunately, mobility dependence on temperature, mobility anisotropy, ESR, ARPES, CMS spectroscopy, the Hall effect, the electrical characteristics of single crystal transistors and theoretical simulations all provide powerful tools for revealing the intrinsic properties and structure–property relationships.

2.5 Electronic states in organic semiconductor crystals
In Section 2.4, we mainly focused on the charge transport of the charge carriers in the molecular crystals. There are two kinds of charge carriers: electrons and holes. As mentioned above, the polarization of the charge carrier will lead to deformation of the surrounding molecules. The charge carrier together with this deformation is called a polaron. In organic field-effect transistors, the charge carriers are injected from the source electrode (i.e. the grounded electrode) into the interface between the semiconductor and the insulator and then propagate to the drain electrode, in the form of polarons. One kind of charge carriers is enough to form a conductive channel. The co-existence of both kinds of charge carriers in one conducting channel is called ambipolar transport. The polaron may be localized or trapped in the defects, leading to lower mobility. A further discussion of charge transport in organic field-effect transistors can be found in Sections 2.4 and 4.1.
Similarly, the charge carriers are also injected from the electrode and then move toward the other electrode in organic light-emitting diodes. In order to achieve light emission, both kinds of charge carriers are necessary for efficient light emission in the channel. Holes are injected from the anode and electrons are generated from the cathode. When they propagate toward the opposite electrodes and meet each other in the channel, they can combine to form excitons. The decay of excitons results in light irradiation. Because the diffusion distance of the excitons is usually very short (see below), the emission often occurs at the place where the excitons are formed. The absence of one kind of charge carriers due to the use of an inappropriate electrode or unipolar semiconducting material will lead to light emission near the electrode. The low mobility of one or both of the charge carriers will also reduce the emission or lead to emission near the electrodes. The trapping of charge carriers will diminish or even extinguish the emission. This will be further discussed in Section 4.2.
The term “exciton” is usually regarded as the excited state in organic materials.209–211 Besides the aforementioned association of the charge carriers, excitons can also be generated by direct photoexcitation. When the electron in the valence band (or HOMO orbit) absorbs one photon, it is photo excited into the conduction band (or LUMO orbit), leaving a hole in the valence band. If there is no interaction between the electron and the hole, i.e. their wave functions are eigenstates of the individual Hamiltonians, then the electron and the hole are free charge carriers. In contrast, the Coulombic interaction between the electron and the hole binds them into an exciton. When the electron jumps into the LUMO orbit after absorption of a photon, the spin property of the electron may remain the same or convert oppositely to its original spin, leading to singlet or triplet states respectively. After its generation, the exciton may diffuse, or evolve ranging from dissociation into electron–hole pairs, fusion of two excitons into one exciton, fission into two excitons, trapping or localization to annihilation or emission. In order to achieve highly photoconductive and efficient organic photovoltaic or solar cells, there is no doubt that the enhanced absorption of the photons, the long-range diffusion and efficient dissociation of the excitons, and the fast transport of the electron and hole free charge carriers to reduce the recombination of the electron–hole pairs are of significant importance.
After creation of the excitons, the exciton has to move to an interface where it can be ionized into an electron–hole pair. In the earlier studies, the diffusion length was considered to be very short (10–20 nm), which confined the efficient diffusion and dissociation of the excitons. In order to facilitate the diffusion of the excitons for efficient dissociation into free charge carriers, two possible approaches can be utilized: (i) decrease the distance between the absorption site and the dissociation interface. The fact212 that a surface coating of rubrene single crystals by using an inert and trap-free dielectric layer significantly decreases the photocurrent (the relative dark current is removed for comparison) indicates that the exciton dissociation is a surface phenomenon rather than a bulk effect (also see Section 4.1.4.2). Such a remarkable change probably can be ascribed to the quantum efficiency of exciton dissociation at the surface or the reduced mobility of the dissociated carriers due to the formation of polarons (see Section 2.4). On the other hand, suitable functionalization of the surface by using exciton splitters can reinforce the dissociation. Therefore, blending of n-type and p-type semiconductors is an efficient way to reduce this distance, leading to enhanced dissociation of excitons into free charge carriers, although the blending always decreases the charge transport and therefore may result in recombination of the charge carriers. (ii) Increase the diffusion length of the excitons. A high degree of order and the delocalized states make it possible to achieve a large diffusion length in the order of microns or more. Podzorov et al.48,212–214 have found that the diffusion of triplet excitons in the single crystals can be over microscopic distances (micron magnitude). By using localized photoexcitation and spatially resolved detection of excitonic luminescence, the exciton diffusion can be visually observed. Moreover, the triplet exciton is strongly anisotropic with long-range diffusion along the orientation of π–π stacking (Fig. 10A).48 The quenching of photoluminescence with the increase of photoconductivity by surface functionalization of exciton splitters suggests that the long-lived triplet excitons are generated by fission of singlets and the enhancement of photoconductivity is due to these triplet excitons. The generated singlet excitons214–216 have a short lifetime of nanosecond magnitude; however, the singlet excitons can fissure into triplet excitons with a lifetime of microsecond level. More details about the fission of singlets can be found in previously reported reviews.217 Time-resolved photoluminescence decay is widely used to study the fission, fusion and quenching of excitons. Fig. 10B shows one example of photogenerated singlet decay and the recombined singlet decay. The photogenerated singlets have a short lifetime of picosecond level. However, a singlet exciton can fissure into two triplet excitons and the two triplet excitons can recombine into a singlet exciton. Because the triplet excitons have a much longer lifetime of microsecond level, the subsequent radiative relaxation of the recombined singlets shows delayed fluorescence.
