From this point of view, reasonable utilization of transition of short-lived singlets to long-lived triplets is useful to increase the diffusion distance. Besides, ambipolar molecular crystals (single component or co-crystals) are one of the most efficient media for organic photovoltaic cells, because they possess the maximum blending level at the molecular scale, are highly ordered and are free of grain boundaries. The molecular scale blending makes it possible to reach the dissociation interface as soon as the excitons are generated, while the highly ordered structure and absence of grain boundaries facilitate the propagation of the charge carriers to the electrodes. These will be further discussed in Sections 4.1.4.2 and 4.3.
 Fig. 10  (A) Anisotropy in exciton diffusion, showing preferential diffusion along the π–π stacking (b axis) in rubrene single crystals, obtained by using localized photoexcitation and spatially resolved detection of excitonic luminescence. Reprinted with permission from ref. 48. Copyright © 2011 American Physical Society. (B) Typical time-resolved photoluminescence decay based on a tetracene single crystal, showing the regimes of prompt (inset) and delayed fluorescence that correspond to the radiative singlet decay and triplet decay respectively. The triplet is generated from fission of singlets and the recombination of two triplets forms a radiative singlet. Reprinted with permission from ref. 218. Copyright © 2014 Macmillan Publishers Limited. 
The fusion and the fission of the excitons are also very important to organic solar cells. The fusion of two excitons can produce a high-energy exciton, which is of particular interest in the energy harvesting system for converting two low-energy photons into a high-energy photon. This high-energy photon then can be absorbed by the wide bandgap material. Especially, the fusion of two long-lived low-energy triplets into a singlet with a shorter lifetime is meaningful for enhancing the utilization of low-energy photons to realize energy harvesting. On the other hand, the fission of singlets is of significant importance because the high-energy exciton can be split into two low-energy excitons which are close to the band edge. Then the high-energy photon can generate two excitons rather than one exciton with the loss of excess energy. Similarly, the fission of the shorter-lived singlet into two longer-lived triplets is of particular significance because they can diffuse over a longer distance. The utilization209,219–222 of high-energy and low-energy photons in the wide solar spectrum through fission and fusion of the excitons respectively can break the upper Shockley and Queisser limit223 of ∼31% in single junction solar cells and increase the overall efficiency of solar cells by as much as 44%.
3 Techniques for controlling the crystallization of organic semiconductors
The crystallization of organic semiconductors is a prerequisite for investigating the various properties and the structures of semiconductors. Simultaneously, crystals with minimized impurities and defects are necessary not only to reveal the intrinsic properties of organic semiconductors, but also to investigate the important factors for high performance devices and demonstrate the fundamental physics. Besides, the growth methods and conditions are also essential to the morphologies and the molecular arrangements, as mentioned in Sections 2.1 and 2.2. The summarization of growth control of organic semiconductor crystals will be helpful in revealing the crystal growth mechanism, understanding the intrinsic reason of crystallization, guiding the future crystal growth and producing crystals with desired size, shape and molecular arrangement for various purposes such as organic optoelectronics and semiconductor industry. An in-depth study of the crystallization of organic semiconductor crystals will benefit further understanding of organic thin film and crystal growth mechanisms and promote their applications in organic electronics.
3.1 Melting techniques
A common method for growing organic semiconductor crystals in earlier research is zone refining.8,224,225 It involves moving a heater or freezer very slowly (several or tens of microns per minute) in order to melt and crystallize the materials. It is also known as the zone melting or zone freezing technique226 (Fig. 11A). In a typical zone refining process, multiple heater zones are aligned with minimized length (e.g. 1 cm).172,227 Normally, the system is maintained in an inert gas atmosphere, which protects the semiconductors from the air and reduces evaporation of the semiconductors. Depending on the difference in the solubility of the impurities in the liquid melt and the solid crystal, the impurities will move to the melting and crystallization interface. Finally, they accumulate at both ends of the system after repeated movement of the heater from one side to the other.228 Highly pure crystals are obtained in the middle of the system (Fig. 11B). Due to the absence of external chemicals, the zone melting technique is regarded as one of the most useful methods for achieving ultra-pure single crystals. In the early research on organic semiconductor crystals, zone refining was often used as a technique for purifying semiconductors.173,227,229 Because of its ability to yield large crystals, it is also utilized for photocurrent studies, especially for TOF measurements, and the fabrication of organic lasers. Until the 1990s, the highest mobility based on organic semiconductors was achieved by using zone melting methods.230
 Fig. 11  (A) Schematic diagram of a typical zone melting apparatus, which contains one molten zone (heater). Reprinted with permission from ref. 227. Copyright © 2013 Elsevier B.V. (B) Zone-refined anthracene. The impurities moved to both ends of the ampoule, as shown in the dark and light parts at both ends. Reprinted with permission from ref. 228. Copyright © 2010 Springer-Verlag Berlin Heidelberg. 
Bridgman and Stockbarger methods are two typical zone-melting techniques.8,143,171–173,231,232 They differ with respect to the moving objects, i.e. the heater (or freezer) and the crucible respectively. Normally, the ampoule is sealed in the Bridgman process and the whole ingot is melted. The crystals grow either spontaneously or on a small seed crystal at the cooler end of the ampoule. Due to the sealed nature of the apparatus, pre-purification of the source materials is usually needed.15 Another method for growing crystals is utilizing a seed crystal and subsequently pulling it out of the melt or by keeping it stationary. This is called the Czochralski method.231 This particular method cannot prevent the vapour from escaping from the system, and hence it can only be used for low-vapour-pressure materials.8 The liquids should be cooled down as slow as possible in order to balance the crystallization and the structural reconfiguration. Hence, the temperature gradient of the different zones and the moving speed of the heater should be as minimal as possible.233 The challenge for crystal growth using melting techniques is the stability of the organic semiconductors at high temperature. In addition, impurities often affect the melting and crystallization by forming eutectic mixtures with the semiconductors.143 It is also worth pointing out that these techniques require a large amount of raw materials as well as relatively expensive and complex apparatus.227 Sometimes, pre-deposited organic films are used as the source materials to grow crystals. The films are sandwiched between two slides (e.g. glass) and the slides act as a template for confining the molten liquids.234–236 Organic semiconductor melts such as anthracene237–239 can be used as solvents for growing crystals of other organic semiconductors with high melting points. The melting solvents can also be other similar aromatic materials.240 With decreasing temperature, the solute becomes supersaturated and hence will nucleate and form crystals. By using this method, the semiconductor crystals can be grown at a much lower temperature. For example, in the case of copper phthalocyanine (CuPc), the crystals can be obtained at 235–260 °C using anthracene as solvent. Without the presence of the solvent, CuPc will not even start to melt at temperature higher than 450 °C and will decompose around 540 °C.238,239 Hence this method is very appropriate for semiconductors which decompose around their melting and sublimation points and have low solubility in most common solvents. Additionally, not much raw materials are required and the solvents can be removed by using organic solvents after crystal growth.
3.2 Vapour techniques
Generally, materials have three states: solid, liquid and gaseous. Due to the localized nature of the molecules in the solid state, it is difficult to form long-range ordered structures through structural reconfiguration or reordering (phase transition).69 It is possible, however, to grow crystals from their liquid and gaseous states. The molecules have high energy to move around and hence form ordered structures through structural reconfiguration involving melting or vapour techniques. As shown in a typical phase diagram (Fig. 12A), it can be seen that a material will change from solid to gaseous state at low temperature (lower than the melting point) with the reduction in the system pressure. As a result, the vapour technique provides a facile way for growing high quality crystals at low temperature.
 Fig. 12  (A) Schematic diagram of a typical three phase diagram; (B) centimeter-sized crystal of tetracene obtained by using the vapour-Bridgman growth method. Reprinted with permission from ref. 144. Copyright © 2005 American Institute of Physics. 

3.2.1 The vapour-Bridgman technique. 
The Bridgman technique is a variation of the zone-melting method used to grow organic semiconductor crystals. In the zone-melting method, the materials are usually melted in the first step. In contrast to the aforementioned Bridgman method which forms crystals from molten liquids, the vapour-Bridgman technique uses the vapour of the materials to grow single crystals. Due to the more active state of the molecules in vapour (gas) than in the molten liquid, the vapour-Bridgman technique is more favourable for the recombination of molecules for crystal growth. Therefore, in early research most organic semiconductor crystals were obtained using the vapour-Bridgman technique.8,143,231,232
Pflaum et al.228,241 improved the vapour-Bridgman technique which could be used to grow crystals with low stability around the melting point, by keeping the heater zone below the melting point. In other words, the materials are not melted in the apparatus and the molecules are not evaporated but rather sublimed into their gaseous states. Using this method, large crystals useful for TOF measurements can be achieved (Fig. 12B).144,242 Similar methods243,244 have also been reported where the heater or the source is not moved. Due to the simultaneous sublimation of the target material and impurities, one problem with crystal growth by these methods is that their purity is not as good as that obtained from melting methods.144,228,243 Therefore, it was necessary to develop novel methods for the growth of crystals with high purity, which culminated in the physical vapour transport (PVT) method, wherein inert carrier gases are introduced into the system to separate the impurity from the target materials.


3.2.2 Physical vapour transport techniques. 
In a typical phase diagram, it can be seen that the direct solid–gas exchange has a lower temperature than the liquid–gas exchange, providing a lower system pressure. Therefore, if the system pressure is decreased,245,246 it is possible to change the solid source into the gaseous state directly under a low temperature. This temperature relates to the sublimation point and is normally lower than the melting point and the decomposition point. This is very meaningful for the sublimation and subsequent crystallization of organic semiconductors which are usually not stable at high temperature. In addition, the molecules in gaseous state have higher energies to recombine into crystals and this in turn facilitates the formation of high quality crystals. Due to the difference in vapour pressure (or different sublimation points), materials will crystallize in different temperature zones.132,228,247 It is worth noting that based on this property, doping of a semiconductor can also be achieved by using the mixture with dopants (similar sublimation points and crystal lattices are necessary) in a suitable ratio as the starting materials.248,249 In order to achieve high quality single crystals, usually an inert carrier gas250 such as Ar, N2, He, H2 is introduced into the system to transport the semiconductor molecules from the high temperature zone to the low temperature zone. This can facilitate the separation of impurities and hence is beneficial for producing high quality crystals. The PVT method was pioneered for organic crystal growth245,251 by Kloc et al. in the 1990s, and has become one of the most popular methods for preparing organic semiconductor crystals.9,10
As shown in Fig. 13, a typical PVT (called the vaporization–condensation–recrystallization (VCR) process in some reports91,98) apparatus consists of a tube furnace with 2 or 3 temperature zones and a quartz tube with open ends. The starting materials are placed and evaporated in the high temperature zone,84,245,246 and then transported by the carrier gas to the low temperature zone for crystallization. As shown in the example in Fig. 13, heavy and light impurities crystallize either in front of or behind the pentacene crystallization zone as well as forming co-crystals with pentacene. The temperature of the high temperature zone controls the sublimation rate and plays an important role in the crystal growth. When the temperature is a little higher than the sublimation point, the crystal will grow slowly (e.g. 10−7 cm s−1)252 which is helpful in forming large and high quality crystals. Changing this temperature can result in different crystal morphologies or molecular arrangements.253,254 For example, by using temperatures of 350 °C and 325 °C for sublimation, 1D wires and 2D disks of pentacene can be obtained, respectively.253 This indicates that when the temperature is as close as possible to the sublimation point, it is favourable for equilibrium crystal morphology. The temperature and position of the low temperature zone, the gas flow and the temperature gradient are all essential for the crystal growth. For example, different morphologies16 of anti-BBTT (DBTDT) were obtained at different growth temperatures. This strongly suggests that the growth temperature can significantly affect the morphology. The probable mechanism is that a higher temperature facilitates the movement of the molecules. In this situation, the molecules prefer growing along the orientation with stronger intermolecular interactions, which will result in crystals with an equilibrium crystal shape. Similar results have been reported for rubrene crystals.37,38,252,255 Different growth temperatures can even lead to different molecular arrangements. For example, α and β CuPc can be obtained with growth temperature lower than 192 °C and higher than 214 °C respectively.63 It is found that a steady gas flow with low velocity (usually 100–300 sccm (sccm = standard cubic centimetre per minute)) and a steady and low temperature gradient (usually 2–5 °C cm−1) facilitate the growth of large crystals. Because of the subsequent change in the sublimation point, the sublimation speed is also influenced by the system pressure by balancing the gas flow and pumping. For example, it has been reported251 that when the pressure is higher than 10 Torr and the temperature is fixed at 285 °C, the sublimation is inhibited for α-6T. Finally, it is plausible that crystal growth is independent of the nature of gas.251 However, argon (Ar)98,256 and hydrogen (H2)252,257 are preferred as carrier gases compared to nitrogen (N2) due to the content of the oxygen impurities found in nitrogen. Technologically, it is more difficult to remove oxygen from nitrogen which may form oxygenated by-products.258 The oxygenated by-products were observable when the content of oxygen exceeded 2 ppm and it was suggested that the oxygen concentration should therefore be less than 0.2 ppm. In addition, both the quantity and purity of the starting materials can affect subsequent crystal growth. Large amounts of starting materials lead to a higher evaporation rate and hence quicker crystallization. Clearly, high purity starting materials will facilitate the growth of high quality crystals.252 Finally, it should be mentioned that crystal growth free from light irradiation151 is necessary in order to prevent photoinduced reactions with O2.
 Fig. 13  Schematic diagram of a typical PVT process. The dark blue crystals are identified as pentacene and the red and yellow crystals as impurities. Adapted with permission from ref. 258. Copyright © 2005 American Chemical Society. 
In summary, compared with melting methods, the PVT method provides an efficient way for producing organic crystals at low temperature and therefore is more suitable for organic semiconductors with low thermal stability. Moreover, the PVT method is advantageous for the crystal growth of materials with poor solubility such as pentacene,258 rubrene37,182 and CuPc.62 Because of the absence of external chemicals and the efficient separation from impurities, PVT presents a very superb way for producing crystals with high quality (see Section 4.1.3). This method can even be used to grow crystals of monomers or precursors, which can be later treated using UV irradiation or other methods to form polymer crystals.259 However, it should be noted that if the organic semiconductors are sensitive to heat, then PVT is not a suitable crystal growth method.


3.2.3 Vapour sublimation. 
Normally, vapour sublimation is used for the deposition of thin films with a view to creating continuous and uniform thin films. Crystalline films with large crystal domains can be achieved by careful selection of the substrate with low nucleation density, high substrate temperature, and slow sublimation rate. Therefore, one possible method for growing single crystals by vapour sublimation is by using a crystal substrate or crystal seed260 to grow semiconducting crystals, e.g. epitaxial growth (e.g., KCl crystal for p-sexiphenyl (6P)261,262 or 2,5-bis(4-biphenyl)bithiophene BP2T263,264) and liquid crystal (LC) growth.265Fig. 14A and B shows an example where the growth of tetracene was induced by using a LC solvent. Crystals as large as tens of micrometers can be observed. It is recognized that the mechanism for the alignment is the LC distortion rather than the epitaxial interaction or anisotropic diffusion.265 Another method266,267 is to use a low vapour pressure solvent to assist the deposition. Ionic liquids (IL) are suitable candidates for this kind of solvents because they possess low vapour pressure and can be used under high temperature and high vacuum conditions. The liquid solvent is favourable for overcoming the nonequilibrium nature of the crystal growth in the vacuum sublimation. High temperature of the substrate is helpful for reaching a thermodynamic equilibrium. A schematic diagram of this method and the resulting pentacene crystals are presented in Fig. 14C and D. After crystal growth, the LC and the IL can be removed by normal organic solvents.265–267
 Fig. 14  (A and B) Vapor sublimation of tetracene crystals induced by an oriented LC solvent. The scale bar in the upper part of (B) is 100 μm and that in the bottom part is 20 μm. Reprinted with permission from ref. 265. Copyright © 2006 American Chemical Society. (C and D) Vacuum deposition of pentacene crystals assisted by the droplet of an ionic liquid solvent. Reprinted with permission from ref. 267. Copyright © 2012 American Institute of Physics. 

3.3 Solution-processing techniques
Organic semiconducting molecules can move freely in solutions, and this makes it possible to assemble them in high order when they are separated from the solvent molecules. Moreover, organic semiconductors usually possess poor thermal stability. Hence, for some materials,96,268 it is impossible to utilize PVT methods or melting methods due to their degradation or decomposition. However, it is possible to grow their crystals by using solution-processing methods.269–272 There are various solution-processing techniques14 which can be exploited for the growth of organic semiconductor crystals, including recrystallization, dip-coating, drop-casting, solvent-exchange, vapour-diffusion methods, etc. The basis of solution-processing techniques is the reorganization of the semiconducting molecules when they are separated from the solvents. Therefore, in order to crystallize molecules out of solutions, the first step is to make the solution saturated or supersaturated. There are three methods for achieving this purpose:82,273–279 (1) slow evaporation of solvents; (2) changing the temperature slowly to adjust the solubility of the organic semiconductor (normally, decreasing the temperature will result in lower solubility); (3) slow introduction of poor solvents into the solutions. Hence, the growth and the quality of the crystals are highly dependent on factors such as the growth method, solvent, solubility, concentration, temperature, atmosphere, pressure as well as the nature of organic semiconductors. For example, by reducing the evaporation rate, nanowire crystals of poly(para-phenylene ethynylene)s (TA-PPEs, TA stands for the thioacetate end groups)97 were obtained through the solvent-vapour diffusion method. Due to the slow evaporation, well-defined, nanowire crystals of TA-PPE were formed. From the structural analysis, it was shown that the π–π stacking direction in TA-PPE nanowires was predominantly perpendicular to the long axis of the nanowires, instead of being parallel to the long axis of the nanowires as was traditionally believed.96 In other words, the charge transport in these nanowires is intramolecular transport. Similar molecular arrangements can also be found in other literature reports.280,281

3.3.1 Dip-coating techniques. 
Dip-coating methods are usually adopted in order to obtain thin films.282 In a typical dip-coating process (Fig. 15A), the substrate is first immersed in the solution gradually with a view to preventing vibrations and bubble formation. Then the substrate is pulled out of the solution slowly, accompanied by precipitation and crystallization which occur at the withdrawing area. Therefore, the pulling speed is important and a suitable speed facilitates the formation of homogeneous and thin crystals.283,284 If the pulling speed is too fast, it may lead to discontinuous islands.284 In contrast, if it is too slow, the uniformity of the crystals will be compromised.285 Normally, in order to achieve stable and slow movement, some special equipment (e.g. Langmuir–Blodgett (LB) apparatus286) is needed.283,287 In addition, the solvent, the concentration, the temperature, the viscosity of the solution and the crystallinity of the materials all play critical roles in the crystallization process.287–290 For example, fast evaporation of solvent (low boiling point, high temperature, etc.) may result in crystal clusters along the contact line.290
 Fig. 15  (A) Schematic diagram of the dip-coating process for crystal growth; (B) typical AFM images of TIPS-PEN crystals obtained through the dip-coating technique. Reprinted with permission from ref. 284. Copyright © 2012 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Due to the slow evaporation, the obtained crystals are able to possess high quality with ordered molecular arrangement. Another advantage of dip-coating techniques is that they can yield crystals with the same orientation. Normally, they grow along the pulling direction (Fig. 15A and B). One problem that is encountered, however, is that the solution of small molecular weight semiconductors usually has low viscosity. This leads to a low wetting of the substrate, especially for hydrophobic self-assembled monolayer (SAM) modified substrates. Therefore, dip-coating methods are usually adopted for the deposition of polymeric semiconductors285 and are rarely adopted for growing organic semiconductor crystals. There are two possible ways which can solve this problem, but each exhibits its own disadvantages: (1) increasing the viscosity of the solutions, which usually results in the formation of thin films or polycrystals; (2) choosing substrates with better wettability,283,291 which may lead to thin films and a poor interface between the crystal and the substrate. Another shortcoming is that the dip-coating method often results in the formation of dendritic microstripes rather than a regular morphology.283,292 In addition, the use of non-volatile solvents leads to prolonged entrained films.293 In other words, the external withdrawing force facilitates the evaporation of the solvent which results in lower quality products than those obtained using self-assembly methods.


3.3.2 Drop-casting techniques. 
Drop-casting methods are one of the most efficient approaches for growing organic crystals. It is generally recognized that crystals obtained using this method will be of high quality. The reason is that this method is free from external forces, such as the withdrawing force used in the dip-coating method. Such external force accelerates the separation of the semiconductor from the solvent. Therefore, the drop-casting method is usually regarded as one of the self-assembly crystal-growing methods. Thus, the growth of the crystals (morphology and molecular arrangement) is first determined by the material itself, i.e., its chemical structure and intermolecular interactions. For example, under the same growth conditions (solvent, concentration, temperature and atmosphere), a range of morphologies from 1D to 3D micro/nanostructures were realized from BPEA, BNEA to BTEA.30 The morphology has a close relationship with the intermolecular interactions, i.e., the long range π–π interactions weaken their dominant role in going from BPEA to BNEA and completely vanish in the BTEA crystals. Such morphology–structure relationship has been discussed in Section 2.2.2.
Undoubtedly, the growth of crystals using this method is also highly dependent on factors such as the solvent, concentration, temperature, atmosphere and substrate surface. Theoretically, the self-assembly process depends mainly on three important intermolecular interactions between (1) solvent molecules; (2) semiconductor molecules; and (3) semiconductor and solvent molecules.294 The interaction between the solvent molecules determines the solvent evaporation, which is also impacted by the temperature, the atmosphere pressure and whether the system is an open or a quasi-closed system. The interaction between the semiconductor molecules governs the crystallinity of the semiconductors. Finally, the interaction between the semiconductor and solvent molecules determines the solubility of the semiconductor295 and hence affects the self-assembly process and plays a role in tuning the molecular arrangements. For example, BPEA can assemble into crystals with different phases depending on the selection of chlorobenzene or dichloromethane solvent to control the evaporation velocity.67 Another interesting example296 is that the residual solvent can also etch the crystals into nanotubes by dissolving the centre of the nanowires, leading to hollowed structures.
Due to the slow self-assembly process, the drop-casting method can be used to produce polymeric crystals96 and co-crystals. For example, co-crystals can be formed by dissolving two semiconductors in a solution with a defined ratio. The co-crystals, especially the electron-donor–electron-acceptor (D–A) co-crystals, are extremely intriguing for charge transfer complex applications. They can be used as organic conductors or ambipolar semiconductor crystals for application in inverters, integrated circuits, OLEDs or OPVs. For example, using simple drop-casting methods, meso-diphenyl tetrathia[22]annulene (DPTTA) can be used as a donor to form co-crystals with not only planar TCNQ297 and TCNQ derivative298 but also nonplanar fullerenes (C60 or C70)299 as the acceptor.
The solvent can be either a single solvent or mixed solvents. There are a few factors that need to be considered. Firstly, solvents used in drop-casting methods should possess suitable boiling points and volatility.30,67,94,300–304 Usually, solvents with a high boiling point facilitate a slow self-assembly process, which is helpful in forming ordered molecular arrangements. Secondly, the semiconductor should have suitable solubility in the solvent. High solubility may prevent the precipitation until only a small amount of solution is left, leading to polycrystals. On the other hand, it is unlikely to obtain crystals or large crystals from solvents with low solubility. For some semiconductors, it is difficult to find a single solvent with ideal solubility, boiling point and volatility. Therefore, mixed-solvents can be adopted.305 One efficient way is to use a mixed-solvent system consisting of one solvent which has higher volatility and solubility for the semiconductor (e.g. toluene) and the other solvent which has lower volatility and solubility for the semiconductor (e.g. ethanol).92 Similar results306 can also be obtained by using toluene and IPA as mixed solvents and the authors found that an abrupt transition in morphology from crystalline films to large single crystals can be achieved when the volume ratio of the two solvents is close to the azeotropic point.
For some semiconductors, even mixed-solvents are incapable of producing high quality crystals. Then complementary methods such as solvent exchange (Section 3.3.3)88 or vapour diffusion (Section 3.3.4)92 may be useful for growing single crystals. They provide slow dilution of the semiconductor. Another problem in drop-casting methods is that well-aligned crystals are unlikely to be obtained over a large area. This might be solved by using inducing methods or printing techniques (Section 3.5). For example, one possible method307–309 for aligning organic crystals through drop-casting methods is to use a tilted substrate. It should be noted that the tilt angle and the surface energy must be appropriate in order to make sure that the lower drop line can spread down the substrate and the upper line can be pinned such that the crystals grow from the upper drop line to form crystal arrays.


3.3.3 Solvent-exchange techniques. 
Solvent-exchange techniques, which are also called phase transfer methods310–313 or liquid–liquid interfacial precipitation (LLIP) methods,122,314–316 are one of the frequently used methods for growing single crystals, through the slow permeation of two different solvents. A typical schematic diagram of a solvent-exchange method is shown in Fig. 16A. Such a solvent-exchange method normally requires the following: (1) the semiconductor should have different solubility in the two solvents; (2) the two solvents should be miscible with each other; (3) the densities of the two solvents should be different so that a distinct interface can be formed and the top layer should be lighter to avoid fast mixing. The most frequently used solvents are tetrahydrofuran, toluene, chlorobenzene, dichloromethane and chloroform. Typical poor solvents include hexane, petroleum ether, diethyl ether, methanol, ethanol and even water.79,317–320 Coincidently, these typical poor solvents are usually lighter than the good solvents although there are some exceptions such as the water–tetrahydrofuran system318 in which the lighter solution was placed upon the heavier poor solvent, or the water–methanol system321 and the chloroform–hexane system56 in which the heavier poor solvent or solution was put on the lighter ones. In order to form large high quality crystals, the injection of the poor solvent (to form the top layer) onto the solution of the semiconductor in the good solvent (to form the bottom layer) should be very cautiously done to prevent vibration.317 With slow permeation (or diffusion) of the poor solvent into the solution, the semiconductor molecules will precipitate to form crystals. For example, crystals of porphyrin derivatives319 were grown through such a solvent-exchange method by using hexane and toluene as the poor and good solvents respectively. Another example is PTCDI crystals which were obtained by using chloroform and methanol as the good and poor solvents respectively.79 C60 nanowires320 were also reported by using solvent-exchange methods. Iso-propyl alcohol (IPA) and m-xylene322 (or toluene314) were used as the poor and good solvents, respectively. Interestingly, when reduced graphene oxide (RGO) was dispersed in the IPA solvent,320 RGO wrapped C60 nanowires (shell–core structure) were obtained. In addition to judicious selection of the two solvents, the concentration of the semiconductor in the good solvent and the volume ratio of the good solvent and poor solvent also play important roles in the crystal growth. For example, by utilizing IPA and benzene (or m-xylene) as the poor and good solvents respectively, 1D nanowhiskers of C60 were successfully obtained. The replacement of good solvent (benzene) by carbon tetrachloride (CCl4) resulted in 2D hexagonal sheets. However, if the mixture of CCl4 and benzene was adopted as the good solvent system, then porous structures were achieved (volume ratio higher than 70:30).122,314,323–325 This slow penetration of the poor solvents into the semiconductor solutions often results in large crystals. This solvent-exchange method can also be used to grow supramolecular nanostructures, e.g., D–A complexes, such as C60 and ferrocene (Fc)315 or C60 and 5,10,15,20-tetrakis (4-methoxyphenyl) porphyrinato cobalt(II) (CoTMPP)316 co-crystals. In a process similar to the one mentioned above, the two semiconductors were dissolved in the toluene solution and then the poor solvent isopropyl alcohol (IPA) was added onto the surface of the solution to form a precipitation interface.
 Fig. 16  Schematic diagram of a typical solvent-exchange method (A) by injecting the poor solvent (or solution) onto the solution (or poor solvent) to form a distinct mixture interface. The mixtures are normally kept in a quasi-closed container on a stationary surface to retard evaporation and mixing; (B) by injecting the solution (or poor solvent) into the poor solvents (or solution), the crystals can be precipitated directly. 
In order to grow crystals directly onto a substrate or to form small crystals (or thin crystals) for device fabrication, the injection of the solution into a large amount of the poor solvent is sometimes utilized, as shown in Fig. 16B. In this case, the solution is diluted by the poor solvent quickly so that crystals precipitate directly.32,57,88,313,326–331 For example, TIPS-PEN microtubes332 were obtained by injecting the solution of TIPS-PEN in toluene into a large amount of acetonitrile and accompanied by sufficient aging. The aging process caused self-etching of the crystals by the residual toluene. A similar example296 is the case of a poor solvent in the solutions restraining the dissolution of the semiconductor in the residual good solvent and thereby inhibiting the formation of hollowed structures as well as reducing the evaporation speed. Another example is that of BPEA. It forms nanotubes333 when solvent-exchange methods are used by injecting the BPEA solution in ethanol (poor solubility) into boiling water (insoluble). On the other hand, by using a drop-casting method, BPEA nanorods30 and nanoribbons67 are formed through slow or fast evaporation respectively. Such a solvent-exchange method334 was also reported by using spray of the solutions onto the anti-solvent. Through optimization of the spray-printing conditions, crystals were obtained for a range of different organic semiconductors. Surfactants can be used to facilitate the nucleation and self-assembly process. This proved to be necessary in order to overcome insoluble issues in the poor solvents (e.g. perylene in water).86,335–337 Similar results338 are also reported for zinc meso-tetra (4-pyridyl) porphyrin (ZnTPyP). Here, cetyltrimethylammonium bromide (CTAB) was used as a surfactant to self-assemble ZnTPyP hollow structures by injecting its N,N-dimethyl-formamide solution into water (poor solvent). The injection of the solution into another solvent which contained seed crystals may produce heterojunctions. For instance, fac-tris(2-phenylpyridine) iridium–silver (Ir(ppy)3–Ag) heterojunctions were obtained by injecting the dichloromethane solution of Ir(ppy)3 into the Ag nanowire suspension (in ethanol).339 The reverse process in which the poor solvent was injected into the solutions was also reported.92,95,340–343 C60 single crystal nanorods344 can be obtained by this method using toluene and IPA as the good and poor solvents, and assisted by CTAB as the surfactant. This direct injection of one solution into another can also be used to crystallize co-crystals. An example is where the two components, i.e., the donor (coronene tetracarboxylate tetrapotassium salt) and the acceptor (dodecyl-functionalized methyl viologen derivative), were mixed in an aqueous solution containing 10% v/v methanol and methanol respectively.345,346 Of course, it should be recognised that this direct mixing of the solution with a poor solvent may result in crystals of low quality or a phase with low thermodynamic stability. This probably can be attributed to the fast mixing and precipitation. For example, by using solvent-exchange methods, kinetically favoured arrangements of rubrene347 are obtained through the injection of the rubrene chloroform solution into methanol or the injection of methanol onto the rubrene dichloromethane solution.


3.3.4 Solvent-vapour diffusion methods. 
From the above discussions, it is evident that high quality and large crystals can be achieved by lowering the mixing speed of the solution and poor solvent. With this target in view, solvent-vapour diffusion (SVD) methods are expected to further reduce the mixing speed. This method is particularly suitable for materials which are highly soluble in some solvents but are almost insoluble in the other solvents. For example, TIPS-TAP is highly soluble in normal solvents such as toluene or chloroform, but has low solubility in acetonitrile.92 With the slow diffusion of the poor solvent vapour into the solution, high quality crystals can be achieved. Similar results can also be found for the crystal growth of tetrachlorinated diperylene bisimide (C12-4CldiPBI) by using toluene and methanol as the good and poor solvents respectively348 and the growth of tetracene-doped anthracene crystals by putting the solution of the two materials (in the mixture of dichloromethane and methanol) in methanol vapour.349 The crystal growth89 of insoluble CuPc and F16CuPc was also reported by using saturated sulphuric acid and water as good solvent and anti-solvent vapour respectively. Hu et al. reported a vapour diffusion method where the good solvent is exploited for providing the vapour atmosphere. They ascribed this successful result to the additional solvent leading to slow evaporation of the solvent and thereby facilitating the self-assembly of the polymer molecules.97 In another example, cyclohexane was used as the solvent and THF was utilized for providing the vapour. Both of them are good solvents and the crystal growth is attributed to the polar/nonpolar co-solvent condition.350–352 Because of the slow evaporation, this method can also be used for the crystal growth of spiro-molecules353 and polymeric semiconductors.280,354 In order to increase the solubility of the semiconductor in the good solvent or accelerate the evaporation of the solvent, it is sometimes necessary to use high temperature.355 For some materials, a mixed solvent system might yield better results. Examples of either solution (e.g. toluene and acetonitrile mixture with a volume ratio of 10:4)92 or solvent vapour systems (e.g. chloroform and methanol mixture with a volume ratio of 3:7)356 have been described.
Similar to the vapour-diffusion method, where the additional solvent vapour diffuses into the solution to induce crystallization of the organic molecules, the solvent-vapour annealing (SVA) method also utilizes solvent-vapour diffusion technology. The difference is that in SVA, typically solid samples are used (e.g. thin films311,357–360), unlike in the SVD process, where the crystal growth is accomplished in solution. Hence, a good solvent should be exploited for providing the vapour in the SVA process, whereas in the SVD process either good or poor solvent can be used. For example, using SVA, tris(8-hydroxyquinoline)aluminium (Alq3) crystals were obtained by annealing the amorphous film of Alq3 in chloroform atmosphere.361 Further studies362 showed that a polymer substrate could assist the SVA process when the solvent is also a good solvent for the substrate. This can be attributed to the reconstruction of the polymer substrate. The thickness of the polymer is also very important for the crystal growth. A range of organic semiconductor films have been shown to self-assemble into crystals on polymer substrates after a SVA process. The SVA method can also be utilized in phase separation to produce C8-BTBT crystals from polymers.208,363 The devices based on this kind of crystals provided a good interface between the semiconductor crystals and the polymeric insulators, and exhibited a mobility as high as 9.1 cm2 (V s)−1. Similar results were also reported for polymer crystals364 and D–A co-crystals365 formed by annealing a ternary spin-coated film composed of the donor, the acceptor and an insulating polymer.

3.4 Chemical reaction methods
The particular chemical deposition method for growing organic semiconductor crystals mentioned here is defined as ‘crystal growth occurring concurrently with the chemical synthesis of the materials’. On the other hand, the methods for growth of crystals discussed in previous sections are accomplished after the synthesis of the materials. The chemical deposition of organometallic complex crystals can be achieved using a variety of techniques including vapour methods (chemical vapour deposition (CVD))366–369 or solution-processing techniques.4,370,371 For example, Cu-TCNQ (or Ag-TCNQ) crystals can be obtained by the chemical reaction of copper (or silver, as films or foils) with TCNQ in either vapour or solution.366–371
Yao et al. reported a colloidal chemical reaction method for growing organic semiconductor crystals. This method can be applied to PTCDI,372,373 perylene,374 rubrene,375 and aquozinc tetraphenylporphyrin376 among others. For example, n-type semiconductors (e.g. PTCDI and its derivatives) contain electro-withdrawing groups and can stabilize anions. As the first step, soluble precursors of PTCDI (radical anions or dianions377) were prepared by utilizing the reaction of PTCDI with a reducing agent. Then the precursors were oxidized by oxygen to form PTCDI. Along with the oxidation, the generated PTCDI molecules self-assembled into crystals from the solutions due to their insolubility.372,373 On the other hand, p-type semiconductors, such as rubrene, consist of electron-rich systems, which can stabilize radical cations. Therefore, rubrene was oxidized into rubrene radical cations by iodine in the first step. This was followed by the reduction of the radical cations using silver, which has a lower oxidation potential than the rubrene radical cations.375 Due to the lower solubility of rubrene than the radical cations and the absorption of the cations on the silver, the nucleation and the growth of the rubrene crystals occurred when rubrene became saturated.375
Usually, the chemical reaction methods used are solution-processing techniques and in order to realize simultaneous crystal growth concomitant with the generation of semiconductor molecules, the solubility of the semiconductor should be lower than that of the precursors. It is generally accepted that the quality and the growth of the crystals are highly dependent on the oxidation (or reduction) potential and the concentration of the semiconductor anions (or cations) and neutral molecules. However, it should be pointed out that in practice chemical reaction methods are not very extensively applied for the growth of organic semiconductor crystals. There are two probable reasons: (1) the anions or cations of most organic semiconductors are not stable enough to last the oxidation and reduction process; (2) too fast growth of the crystals hinders the formation of ordered molecular arrangements.
3.5 Patterning of organic semiconductor crystals
The patterning of crystals is an intriguing subject which makes it possible to investigate the fundamental physics of complex electronic devices. It also makes it possible to demonstrate the potential applications of organic semiconductor crystals in optoelectronics. However, of all the aforementioned methods, only dip-coating283,286 actually provides the possibility of achieving this target. Where other methods are concerned, they rarely produce crystals in ordered arrays. Three possible methods are expected to produce organic semiconductor crystal arrays: (1) top-down mode: patterning the crystals after their growth; (2) bottom-up mode: growing the crystals on the treated substrates with patterned seed crystals or other patterned inducers (e.g. SAM patterns) to form crystal arrays; (3) printing techniques.

3.5.1 Patterning of crystals after their growth. 
The patterning of organic semiconductor crystals is important for the fabrication of organic circuits such as inverters consisting of n-type and p-type crystals378,379 or only p-type crystals.55 For example, crystal arrays with moderate orientation can be produced using methods where the substrates are treated (rubbed) with pre-grown organic crystals (Fig. 17)38 or by drop-casting a suspension of the crystals in poor solvents onto the tilted substrate82 (the utilization of substrates pre-treated with hydrophobic and hydrophilic agents for selective patterning of the crystal suspension will be discussed in Section 3.5.2.2). The friction rubbing method needs crystals vertically grown on the substrates. It is found that crystals prepared by PVT techniques on a bare silicon substrate (untreated by hydrophobic SAMs) may meet the necessary requirements.
 Fig. 17  (A) Schematic diagram of the fabrication of organic crystal arrays by rubbing the substrate with a pre-deposited crystal sample and its (B) ideal result and (C) actual result. Adapted with permission from ref. 38. Copyright © 2010 The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
One efficient way to creating crystal arrays is to pattern the films consisting of plenty of crystals. The crystal network acts as a polycrystalline film. The crystals can be obtained using a range of techniques such as PVT methods, recrystallization, solvent-exchange methods and vapor-diffusion methods. The crystals are firstly dispersed in poor solvents and then thin films can be obtained by spin-coating or drop-casting.79,82 Bao and co-workers adopted a filtration-and-transfer (FAT) alignment method for patterning crystals which form crystalline film arrays.95 In short, they adopted a patterned polydimethylsiloxane (PDMS) mask with hollows as the filter so that the crystals could only pass through the holes in order to be deposited onto the porous AAO supporter. The PDMS mask and supporter were then removed to form the crystal arrays, which reflected the duplication of the hollow patterns of the PDMS (Fig. 18).
 Fig. 18  Schematic diagram of a filtration-and-transfer (FAT) alignment method for patterning crystal film arrays, MW: microwires. Reprinted with permission from ref. 95. Copyright © 2009 The National Academy of Sciences. 


3.5.2 Inducing techniques for the patterning of organic crystals. 
3.5.2.1 Crystal patterning using seed crystal patterns. 
One efficient method for inducing the crystal growth is to utilize the seed crystals. The seed crystals have been widely used for the growth of large crystals,380e.g. the aforementioned Czochralski method. This “bottom-up” method is free from pollution and damage to the crystals, both of which are usually associated with the handpicking process (crystals are handpicked out of the pre-grown crystals) of organic crystals. Moreover, as an “epitaxial” growth technique, it facilitates the generation of high quality organic crystals. In addition, it has the advantage of providing crystal patterns in situ on a chosen substrate at a specific location. This proves to be very helpful for forming crystal patterns in intimate contact with the selected substrates. For example, by dip-coating the substrate in a suspension of seed crystals and then epitaxially growing the seed crystals in the PVD system, it was possible to obtain patterns of CuPc crystals.381 The crystals grew epitaxially along the long axis of the seed crystals, i.e., the π–π stacking direction of the crystals. Therefore, it was possible to create crystal patterns such as parallel and crossed nanoribbon arrays by controlling the orientation of the seed crystals.
One important advantage of this method is its applicability for the growth of organic single crystalline heterojunctions. For example, F16CuPc crystals can grow epitaxially on CuPc crystals using PVD methods119 (Fig. 19). One necessary condition, however, is that this epitaxial growth requires that the two materials possess similar lattice parameters in order to decrease the lattice mismatch which might result in structural defects. Fortunately, the CuPc crystals grew along the [010] direction, which has a lattice parameter of 4.790 Å in this direction, and the F16CuPc molecules were stacked along the [100] direction, which has a lattice parameter of 4.796 Å in this direction. The mismatch between the lattice parameters of CuPc and F16CuPc is only about 0.13%. Hence, the single crystal of F16CuPc was successfully obtained on the CuPc crystals as shown in Fig. 19. Similar results382 were reported for the epitaxial growth of CuPc onto n-type 5,10,15,20-tetra(4-pyridyl)-porphyrin (H2TPyP) hollow structures. The interesting point of this result is that the two semiconductors were deposited in one step. The n-type semiconductor was first nucleated on the substrate and formed a hollow structure, and then the hollow structure provided the high surface energy areas for selective deposition of CuPc. The CuPc was partially wrapped by H2TPyP near the junctions, which formed core–shell structures and benefited the collection of excited carriers.
 Fig. 19  (A) Epitaxial growth of F16CuPc crystals onto CuPc crystals by PVT methods; (B) schematic top view of the (001) planes of CuPc and F16CuPc crystals, which pack along the [100] and [010] directions respectively (π–π stacking orientation). Reprinted with permission from ref. 119. Copyright © 2010 American Chemical Society. 
The SVA technique provides another method for patterning organic crystals by using seed crystals, where organic semiconductor thin films are used as the seeds. In simple terms, patterns of organic thin films are first prepared by selective deposition of organic semiconductors on substrates, and then the SVA process is carried out in order to generate patterned single crystals from the pre-deposited thin film patterns.383 The selection deposition can be obtained either by vapour sublimation through a shadow mask384 or by deposition on a pre-patterned substrate383 (see Section 3.5.2.2). The problem is that by using this method it is difficult to achieve patterned crystals with highly oriented arrays, especially over large areas. This is due to the uncontrollable pattern of seed crystals (epitaxial growth methods) and low orientation selectivity during the annealing process.
3.5.2.2 Crystal patterning using substrate pre-treatment with SAM patterns. 
Compared to the approach of using seed crystals for crystal growth, the modification of substrates with pre-treated patterns is easier and more powerful. Different techniques can be used to pattern the substrate for the subsequent crystal growth, such as micro-contact printing,101,385 shadow masking technique (e.g. Au patterns as a mask to form hydrophilic patterns on the substrate during the surface modification of substrate by SAMs386), plasma387 or vacuum ultraviolet (VUV) light388–390 etching (only some areas are etched to give SAM patterns) and different kinds of lithography techniques.363,390Fig. 20 shows an example of achieving crystal patterns using ODTS patterns. The SAM patterns on the substrates obtained by micro-contact printing101 were accomplished before crystal growth. Although this method could not guarantee that the crystals grown at all the lattice points of the SAM layer had the same orientation, it is still one of the most useful and efficient methods for producing high quality crystal patterns. Every desired site has crystals over a large area, compared with the previously discussed methods such as dip-coating techniques, patterning of crystals after crystal growth and seed crystal-inducing techniques.
 Fig. 20  Patterning of crystals on SAM patterns which were obtained by micro-contact printing techniques. The crystals were located on the rough SAM patterns. Adapted with permission from ref. 101. Copyright © 2006 Macmillan Publishers Limited. 
One method utilizing SAM patterns to obtain crystal arrays is using the rough edge of the SAM patterns as the crystal nucleation sites. For example, micro-contact printing usually provides rough SAM surfaces (e.g., r.m.s roughness: ∼150 Å), which results in a much higher possibility of nucleation than smooth surfaces (e.g., untreated area or vapour-deposited smooth ODTS monolayer surface with r.m.s roughness lower than 5 Å).101 When the substrate is kept at high temperature using vapour growth methods, the valley in the roughness area confines the movement of the vapour molecules. This in turn enhances the selectivity of the nucleation in this area. Selective growth is found, irrespective of whether the other surface of the substrate is untreated or treated with smooth monolayers. This suggests that the selectivity is due to the surface roughness of the patterns rather than the hydrophobic or hydrophilic properties.391 As shown in Fig. 20, pentacene, rubrene and C60 crystals were grown using PVT methods onto the patterned substrate at the precise locations of the SAM patterns. A similar mechanism is found by using Au patterns to grow crystal patterns through the PVT method.392 The crystals nucleated at the edge or at the top surface of the Au patterns and then grew two dimensionally on the smooth SiO2 surface. The problem is that the low quality of the rough patterns usually results in the formation of polycrystals.
The second method utilizing SAM arrays to pattern crystals is utilizing the interaction or the crystal lattice matching between the SAM patterns and the relevant organic semiconductor. For example, the crystallization of anthracene on a diphenylbenzene-modified substrate exhibited a higher nucleation density and more uniform facet angles of crystals than other SAMs (e.g. phenyl, or SAMs with terminal groups of –CH3 or –NH2, etc.) or unmodified substrates. Especially, the nucleation density was also higher than the other phenyl-terminated SAMs, suggesting the important role of the interaction and crystal lattice matching in the selective growth of crystals.385
The third approach using SAM arrays to pattern organic crystals is to utilize the surface energy to tune the wettability of the solutions, leading to nucleation at the desired sites. In other words, a lyophobic surface suppresses the wetting of the solution and prevents the preservation of the solution in this area. On the other hand, lyophilic surfaces facilitate the confinement of the solution.286,386,393 SAM-modified substrates are typically lyophobic to water (hydrophobic) and most organic solvents. Therefore, the substrate modification by SAM patterns is one of the most efficient ways for achieving lyophobic–lyophilic patterns. It should be noted that substrates with untreated patterns (irrespective of whether the crystals are growing onto the SAM areas or unmodified regions) are usually detrimental to the growth of high-quality crystals as well as the charge transport of the semiconductors. One way for producing patterned substrates is to treat the whole surface with a hydrophobic agent and then pattern lyophilic arrays on this substrate. The patterns and the rest of the areas of the substrate are both SAM modified. For example, the surface of the substrate can be first modified by HMDS and then patterned with a lyophilic SAM such as phenethyltrichlorosilane (PhTS) on the selected areas after removing HMDS in these regions.389 Similar results can also be found using other SAMs, as long as they possess phenyl terminal groups which are needed to form lyophilic patterns (e.g. phenyltrichlorosilane (PTS)).390 The solution-processing techniques for growing crystals such as the dip-coating method and drop-casting385 (theoretically, solvent-exchange and vapour-diffusion methods could also be used) can be adopted for growing crystal arrays by using SAM patterns. Of these, the dip-coating method is preferred due to its strength in providing crystals with an ordered orientation along the pulling direction. In order to localize the solution more efficiently to the lyophilic areas and to save the consumption of the organic semiconductors, Bao386et al. developed a method by injecting the semiconductor solution into the water and placing the patterned substrate with the lyophilic patterns (the background is the lyophobic areas) into the mixture. The stirring proved to be helpful in localizing the solution onto the lyophilic patterns and hindering the localization onto lyophobic areas. After removal of the mixture, droplets of the semiconductor solution were left onto the lyophilic patterns, which formed crystals after solvent evaporation. This is shown in Fig. 21. Another useful method for patterning organic semiconductor crystals is to utilize patterns with different surface energies to selectively deposit the suspension of the pre-deposited crystals. For example, CuPc crystals were patterned by drop-casting the suspension of CuPc crystals in water onto a substrate with patterned hydrophobic and hydrophilic patterns or through rinsing the crystals on the patterned substrate with hydrophobic or hydrophilic solvents.394
 Fig. 21  Patterning of crystals by utilizing the areas with different surface energies. Au pads: lyophilic area, and substrate: lyophobic area. Reprinted with permission from ref. 386. Copyright © 2008 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
A comparison of these methods reveals that the surface energy approach is much more popular due to its high efficiency (compared to the utilization of interaction and lattice match) and its ability to achieve high quality crystals (compared to the utilization of roughness). Similarly, polymers or other materials can also be used to act as templates (patterns) to provide the lyophobic–lyophilic patterns. For example, a fluoropolymer, Cytop-TM (lyophobic surface), is patterned onto a SiO2 substrate to form lyophobic–lyophilic patterns363 through a photolithography process. Photoresist is patterned to partially cover the substrate where Cytop-TM could not be deposited. Then, after removing photoresist patterns, the semiconductor C8-BTBT in a suitable polymer is deposited onto the wetting regions (without Cytop-TM patterns). This results in crystal arrays with the assistance of the SVA process. Based on patterns of large rectangular lyophilic regions (small rectangular lyophilic regions connected to the large rectangular region were patterned on its side) on the lyophobic substrate, Goto et al. obtained single crystal arrays through solution-processing techniques. These substrates successfully confined the solution in the lyophilic regions.395 According to the experimental results and computer simulations, they concluded that crystal nucleation occurred at the small rectangular lyophilic region due to the absence of surrounding solutions which in turn made the solution more saturated in this area. Then the crystal growth continued in the large rectangular lyophilic region. The anisotropic growth rates resulted in selective orientation. Zhang et al. reported another lithography-assisted method396 for patterning single crystals. BPEA solutions were directly spin-coated onto the substrate (modified by PTS after lithography) with ditch-like photoresist patterns. Such a self-assembly process produced a well-aligned single crystal nanowire array at the wafer-scale. The nanowire array possesses high quality, yielding a mobility of up to 3.2 cm2 (V s)−1, which is even much higher than that of the individual crystal obtained by a drop-casting method.30
3.5.2.3 Crystal patterning using substrate pre-treatment with other patterns. 
Theoretically, the most efficient way to guarantee the crystal growth at every induction site is by using chemical reaction methods. The much stronger covalent bonds between the acceptor (patterned inducers) and donor (precursors of the semiconductors) can provide a much higher possibility of crystal growth compared to any other methods. This method is particularly suitable for the growth of metal complex semiconductors, in which the metals can be facilely patterned onto the substrates through diverse means, such as shadow mask method or lithography techniques. For example, Cu-TCNQ or Ag-TCNQ patterns were successfully synthesized after chemical reaction of TCNQ with copper or silver arrays which had been first patterned onto the substrate.368,369,397
Takeya et al. developed a facile method which they named the edge-cast solution crystallization method185,398 for patterning crystals. This was achieved by sustaining the semiconductor solutions around the patterned pillars117,399 as shown in Fig. 22A. The pillars, which can be made from any insoluble material such as plastic, silicon wafer,117,120,400 or capillary tube,401 were found to be sufficiently efficient in sustaining the solutions around the pillars. Crystals formed around the pillars after evaporation of the solvents. The crystals grew in the direction from the edge of the droplet to the liquid-sustaining structure as shown in Fig. 22A. Similar results were also reported by Pyo109,401–403 and Bao120,404et al., who used a capillary tube or a small piece of silicon wafer as the pinners (or pillars). This is known as droplet-pinned crystallization. As shown in Fig. 23, the solution is sustained around the pins which creates crystal arrays as a consequence of solvent evaporation.120 By utilizing this method a mixture of two semiconductors (C8-BTBT and C60) was also developed which was capable of forming heterojunctions, in which the two semiconductors were separated according to the different crystallization rates. C60 crystallizes easily and grows faster and as a result the second semiconductor grew on it to form the p–n heterojunctions.405 There are some differences in the two approaches. In Takeya's method large crystalline films were obtained around every pillar, whereas in Pyo and Bao's method crystal arrays were observed around every pin. This might be due to the different size of the pillars, the use of different semiconductors (C8-BTBT for Takeya's method and C8-BTBT, C60, TCNQ, TIPS-PEN, PTCDI-C8, etc. for Pyo and Bao's method), different substrates or different growth conditions (e.g. solvent, concentration, etc.).109,117,120,401–406 Interestingly, in these methods, crystals grow along the direction of the solvent receding direction.109,117,120,401–404 In order to efficiently grow crystal patterns, two requirements need to be fulfilled:120 (1) the pillars or pinners should be lyophilic to make sure that the solution is sustained around them; (2) the solution should be saturated or close to saturation, so that the crystals can crystallize around the pillars or pinners to form crystal arrays. Otherwise, precipitation occurs only when a little solution is left, which leads to polycrystals on the pillars or pinners.
 Fig. 22  (A) Patterning of crystals by using pillar arrays to sustain the solutions around the pillars (edge casting); (B) patterning of crystals by using inclining plate arrays (or stamps with inclined surfaces at intended positions) to hold the solutions beneath the plate (gap casting). The arrows represent the direction of the crystal growth. Adapted with permission from ref. 185. Copyright © 2012 Elsevier B.V. 
 Fig. 23  Patterning of crystals by utilizing pillars or pinners to sustain the solutions around the pillars or pinners. Reprinted with permission from ref. 120. Copyright © 2012 American Chemical Society. 
Similar results are found by using patterned inclining plates to hold the solutions beneath the plate. This is known as the gap casting method and is illustrated in Fig. 22B.113,407 One end of the inclining plate is open to make sure that the evaporation of the solvents occurs from this end. This ensures that the crystals grow along the direction from this opened side to the closed edge, the same as the solvent receding direction. A thin wire can be adopted as support to provide the slight inclining angle.113 One important difference to the induction by pillars or pinners is that the inclining plates should be little lyophobic or at least more lyophobic than the substrate. Then the solutions will be more wettable on the substrate in order to form the crystal arrays. Using either a p-type semiconductor, C10-DNTT,407 or an n-type semiconductor, PDIF-CN2,113 this method resulted in crystalline films around every inclining plate. Similar results408,409 were also found by using a perfluoropolyether (PFPE) mould to confine the solutions. The PFPE possesses the advantage of repelling the organic solvents.
Bao and co-workers68,410 have also reported a solution-shearing method for aligning single crystals. Solution-shearing is a method which adopts a moving non-stick substrate to shear the solution and leave oriented crystalline films behind on the stationary substrate.411 This is somewhat similar to the aforementioned inclining plate used for holding the solutions beneath the plate, in which the plate was modified by hydrophobic SAMs. In order to increase the wetting of the substrate and decrease the adhesion of the solution on the moving substrate, the moving substrates are usually treated to be lyophobic. This can be achieved by using an ODTS modified moving substrate and an unmodified stationary substrate.412 Alternatively, by using moving and stationary substrates treated with ODTS and PTS respectively, similar results can be achieved. The reason is that the PTS modified substrate is more lyophilic than the ODTS modified one as mentioned above.68,410 Normally, the solution-shearing method is used to obtain films during the coating process. The imposed mass transport limitations during the coating process68 often result in void and dendritic structures rather than the desired arrays.413 Bao and co-workers410 improved the solution-shearing method by patterning the shearing blade with micropillar arrays to enhance the mass transport. This approach can facilitate the lateral mass transport; hence they termed it as the fluid-enhanced solution-shearing approach. By using this patterned blade, large crystal arrays with single crystalline domains as large as 1 cm long and 200–1000 mm wide were achieved for TIPS-PEN, which was quite unprecedented for crystal patterning.


3.5.3 Printing techniques for the patterning of organic crystals. 
Besides the printing techniques adopted for patterning inducers as discussed in Section 3.5.2, printing techniques can also be used for the direct patterning of organic crystals. Techniques such as micro-contact printing (μCP) and ink-jet printing have been reported for growing crystal patterns. Such printing techniques are promising for patterning crystals over a large area and make it possible to investigate the fundamental physics of complex electronic devices and even to apply the crystal arrays in large-area integrated circuits at low cost.
3.5.3.1 Micro-contact printing techniques. 
Micro-contact printing is a kind of soft lithography, which utilizes a patterned stamp usually made of commercial polymers such as elastomeric PDMS materials, to hold and release the objects. This can be achieved in several ways. The first is through a soft non-covalent contact (traditional micro-contact printing). Alternatively, in chemical lift-off lithography,414 covalent interactions can enhance the interaction between the stamp and the objects, resulting in precise replication of the stamp features for the purpose of transferring objects to the desired substrates. The stamp with a patterned relief can be obtained by using traditional photolithography techniques or electron beam lithography (EBL). The transferred objects415 can be solids or liquids, including SAM monolayers,101 semiconductors,416 electrodes417 or insulators.418 In the first step, the stamp is softly pressed onto the objects, which will selectively adhere onto the relief of the stamp according to the features of the stamp. Then the stamp with the objects is transferred onto the desired substrate. The objects can be released onto the substrate by removing the stamp through heating417,419 or peeling back420 or some other methods, leading to crystal arrays with topological patterns as on the PDMS stamp.
An interesting way to transfer organic semiconductor solutions onto substrates to generate crystal arrays is capillary force lithography (CFL).421 A great advantage of CFL is that high pressure is not required to adsorb the objects (solutions). For example, C8-BTBT solutions were successfully transferred by this method and resulted in crystalline lines or grids as a replicate of the PDMS mold.416 The problem is that, due to the fast nature of the process, this method may lead to crystalline patterns with several domains in each pattern rather than single crystals. A similar process was reported where a polyurethane acrylate (PUA) mold was used. The authors named the method liquid bridge-mediated nanotransfer molding (LB-nTM).422,423 The difference from the former reports is that in the LB-nTM method, the crystals are formed firstly through self-assembly in the recessed channels of the mold and then transferred to the substrate via a liquid bridge (anti-solvent) between the substrate and the mold. The self-assembly process makes it possible to achieve high quality single crystals. The objects can be small molecular semiconductors such as TIPS-PEN, C60 and 8,16-didodecyl-8,16-dihydrobenzo[a]benzo[6,7]indolo[2,3-h]-carbazole (C12-BBICZ), polymeric semiconductors P3HT and even electrode materials (PEDOT:PSS, Ag ink, etc.). A similar method was also reported by directly drop-casting the solution on the substrate along with CFL afterwards, forming crystals in the template channels.424
It is an attractive option to use pre-deposited crystals for printing. However, unlike organic thin films, large area organic crystals are not easily realized. Through an abrupt heating process (170 °C, 1 min), amorphous rubrene thin films were annealed into crystalline films with the width and length of domains as large as hundreds of micrometers.425 The crystalline films were then selectively removed from the substrate by pressing the PDMS mould onto the crystalline films at 170 °C. Keeping the substrate temperature higher than 160 °C is necessary to help the film to get rid of the substrate. The temperature also increases the adhesion between the stamp and rubrene film to form intimate contact.426 In another example, dodecane, a high temperature boiling solvent with low surface tension, was used in a mixed solvent system with toluene or dichlorobenzene and such mixed solvents made it possible to realize large flat single crystalline TIPS-PEN.427,428 Flat crystals of centimetre size were obtained by using a volume ratio of dodecane and toluene at 1:1. The crystals were then selectively transferred to other substrates by PDMS, as shown in Fig. 24. Similarly, when the temperature was higher than 100 °C, the etching of TIPS-PEN was improved. It was also demonstrated that the crystal arrays could be tailored by selectively transferring the crystals repeatedly (Fig. 24).
 Fig. 24  Crystal patterning by using the micro-contact printing technique by selectively transferring single crystals from a large crystal film as a replication of the stamp features. Reprinted with permission from ref. 428. Copyright © 2011 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Compared with other patterning methods, micro-contact printing is found to be the most efficient for transferring pre-deposited crystals to produce crystal arrays with the same orientation, because the crystal arrays are detached from the same crystal or crystal domain. Besides this technique, only the inducing techniques (substrate is pre-treated) with the assistance of the dip-coating method can provide crystal arrays with the same orientation. In addition, the micro-contact printing method can give rise to crystals at the desired locations with the desired shapes: with the same width, length and height at every lattice. Finally, it should be mentioned that in spite of its success this technique is still full of challenges where large crystal films are concerned.
3.5.3.2 Ink-jet printing techniques. 
Ink-jet printing is a technique which jets droplets onto substrates to form patterns or to produce crystal arrays over a large area. It has been found that hydrophobic or hydrophilic arrays pre-deposited on substrates are useful in assisting the alignment of the droplets.429–431 In order to form high quality crystals from ink-jetted droplets, it is essential to choose suitable solvents for the self-assembly.427,431 A fast printing process and inappropriate solvent will most likely result in low quality crystals and sometimes lead to the undesirable so-called “coffee-ring” effect430–432 where the thickness at the edge is thicker than at the centre. For example, it was found that when dodecane was used as the co-solvent it facilitated the growth of TIPS-PEN crystals (which has already been mentioned in Section 3.5.3.1). By ink-jet printing the TIPS-PEN solution onto hydrophilic patterns (untreated areas) of the ODTS-treated substrate, patterned crystals were obtained.431 It has also been found that the solvent-exchange method is helpful430 in assisting the ink-jet printing process: (1) ink-jetting the poor solvent (or anti-solvent) onto the hydrophilic patterns (untreated patterns of the HMDS treated substrate); (2) ink-jetting the semiconductor solution onto the poor solvent patterns; (3) letting the poor solvent and the solution mix slowly to form crystals (as shown in Fig. 25). In order to retard the mixing of the poor solvent with the solution, the solution is jetted at one side of the patterns. The two sides are connected by a narrower region, a bit like a bottleneck as shown in Fig. 25. The role of this protuberance is similar to the inducing technique example395 mentioned in Section 3.5.2.2, in which a small rectangular hydrophilic region is connected to a larger rectangular hydrophilic region. Such bottleneck is not only helpful in retarding the mixing for achieving high quality crystals but it also facilitates the formation of crystals with the same orientation. Using this ink-jet printing technique, crystal arrays of C8-BTBT were achieved with a mobility as high as 31.3 cm2 (V s)−1, which is one of the highest values reported for solution-processed devices.
 Fig. 25  Schematic diagram of an ink-jet printing technique for growing crystal arrays with the assistance of a solvent-exchange method: (1) ink-jetting of poor solvent (or anti-solvent); (2) ink-jetting of semiconductor solution; (3) crystal growth with evaporation of mixed solvents; (4) complete evaporation of solvents to form crystals. Reprinted with permission from ref. 430. Copyright © 2011 Macmillan Publishers Limited. 
A combination of ink-jet printing with CFL was reported by Sung and co-workers.433,434 The solution droplets of organic semiconductors are jetted into the universal mold by ink-jet printing and then form nanowire crystals through capillary-driven flow and drying. The mold and the crystals are transferred onto the substrate that is covered by a thin polar liquid layer, forming a good conformal contact between the nanowires and the substrate. The drying of the liquid layer leads to the pattern of nanowire crystals on the selected locations on the substrate.

3.6 Ultrathin or large crystals: guidelines for controlling crystallization
As discussed above, organic crystals have many advantages, including their perfect molecular arrangements, the elimination of grain boundaries and minimal defects. All of these make it possible to reveal the intrinsic properties of the semiconductors, study the structure–property relationship, investigate the factors for high performance devices, and unveil the charge transport mechanisms. For example, ultrathin organic semiconductor crystals are helpful for studying the thickness-dependence of conducting channels in organic transistors. It is useful to reveal the minimal thickness of the semiconducting layer for efficient charge transport. It is generally believed that the charge carrier transport is located at the interface region and consists of one to several molecular layers.435 However, organic thin films face challenges in revealing such information due to the presence of disordered molecular arrangements and huge grain boundaries and defects, although a few reports have used LB films for investigating this thickness-dependence.436 In contrast, crystals are free from these disadvantages and the experimental results suggest that a semiconducting monolayer is sufficient for achieving charge transport. Secondly, as mentioned in Section 3.5.3.1, large flat thin crystal films are a prerequisite for the patterning of crystals by micro-contact printing techniques. This is quite meaningful for achieving large crystal arrays with the same size (height, length and width) and the same orientation. Although some large flat crystals with thicknesses of only tens of nanometres have been produced by using methods such as PVT and drop-casting methods,38,67,92 controllable methods are rarely reported. Inspired by the mechanical exfoliation of graphene,437 Kloc and co-workers438 reported an “organic crystal cleavage” method (Fig. 26439), which has proven to be efficient for obtaining ultrathin crystals. In place of graphite, F16CuPc or pentacene was used. The authors438 thinned the crystals by repeatedly peeling the organic crystals by using adhesive tape. After several cleavages, thin crystals were gently moved to the substrate by pressing the tape onto the substrate. This method can efficiently decrease the thickness of the crystals from micrometres to tens of nanometres.
