There is no doubt that crystals with a layer-by-layer structure, such as the case in graphite,439 will be easily exfoliated, especially those with strong in-plane but weak out-of-plane intermolecular interactions. Hence, similar results439 were obtained with even thinner crystal sheets (3–15 nm) over a large area (100–200 mm) based on crystals of B-phase (2Z,2′Z)-3,3′-(1,4-phenylene) bis(2-(3,5-bis-(trifluoromethyl)phenyl)acrylonitrile) (CN-TFPA) (Fig. 26A and B).
 Fig. 26  Exfoliated crystals on (A) the scotch tape (scale bar: 30 μm) and (B) the SiO2 substrate after the transfer process (scale bar: 20 μm); (C) schematic diagram of an “organic crystal cleavage” method. Reprinted with permission from ref. 439. Copyright © 2012 American Chemical Society. 
Ultrathin organic crystals also can be produced by self-assembly. According to the aforementioned assumptions as discussed in Section 2.2, if one material possesses strong in-plane but weak out-of-plane intermolecular interactions, it is possible to self-assemble the molecules into large crystal films due to the strong dominating intermolecular interactions. Ultra-thin crystals of HTEB,105 as large as millimetres, were obtained by a drop-casting method from its chlorobenzene solution. The HTEB molecules in the ultrathin crystal films grew almost vertically on the substrate in a layer-by-layer growth mode. These results confirm the importance of strong in-plane intermolecular interactions for the self-assembly of large 2D crystal films. Similar results428 can also be found in TIPS-PEN crystals by drop-casting solutions of TIPS-PEN in dodecane–toluene co-solvents.
On the other hand, large crystals are essential for the analysis of the molecular arrangements in order to obtain the molecular structure, intermolecular interactions, crystal lattice parameters and so forth. They are also one of the prerequisites for studying the fundamental physics, especially for TOF, Hall effect measurements and top-gate OFETs. In addition, the in-depth study of large crystals can benefit the large crystal domains for potential large area applications, such as patterned OFET device arrays and applications involving OLED and OPV for achieving large areas of illumination and p–n junctions.
Among the aforementioned methods, melting methods are the most possible methods for achieving large crystals. The crystal size is only confined by the size of the ampoule.8,15 The disadvantage of the melting method is that organic semiconductors may not be stable at high temperature, which will lead to oxidation, decomposition or polymerization among others. Therefore, only some semiconductors have been explored using the melting method, e.g. naphthalene, anthracene, phenanthrene, pyrene, perylene, etc. The vapour-Bridgmann method144 reduces the impact of high temperature on organic semiconductors by using sublimation, which normally can be achieved at temperature lower than the melting point. At the same time, the vapour-Bridgmann method maintains the possibility of large crystal growth, again only confined by the size of the ampoule.
Low pressure can reduce the sublimation point. Hence, the PVT method is widely adopted for the growth of organic semiconductor crystals, where the pressure is usually kept lower than 1 atm. Simultaneously, due to the different vapour pressure, the crystallization regions of the impurities are different from that of the desired material. Hence, this method also facilitates high quality crystals by expelling the impurities. In order to achieve large crystals, the crystallization process should be as slow as possible. To achieve this purpose, there are several factors that need to be considered, including the source temperature which should be approximately equal to the sublimation point, a higher crystallization area which can be controlled by the gas flow and the temperature gradient, steady and slow gas flow, steady and low temperature gradient, and lower pressure. All of these have been discussed above. Fig. 27 shows several examples of large crystals obtained by using PVT methods.
 Fig. 27  Large crystals obtained by using PVT methods. Reprinted with permission from ref. 15. Copyright © 2013 Materials Research Society. 
In order to produce large crystals by solution-processing techniques, it is necessary to decrease the crystallization speed so that a self-assembly process can be achieved. Traditional recrystallization, solvent-exchange and solvent-vapour diffusion methods are the most common methods for growing large crystals. There are three main approaches for reducing the speed of precipitation: reducing the cooling speed, decreasing the evaporation rate of the solvents and reducing the infiltration speed of the poor or anti solvent into the solutions. Details have been discussed in Section 3.3.15 For the semiconductors which exhibit significant changes in solubility at different temperatures, the crystals can be obtained by reducing the solubility by cooling the solutions. There is no doubt that decreasing the cooling speed is a very efficient route to forming large crystals.276 In order to decrease the evaporation rate277 of the solvent, there are a range of means available. These include decreasing the temperature during the evaporation process, using a solvent with low volatility, keeping the solution in a sealed system,440 placing the solution in the solvent atmosphere (a kind of solvent-vapour diffusion method) or reducing the concentration. In order to decrease the infiltration speed of the poor or anti solvent into the solutions, there are two approaches: the solvent-exchange and solvent-vapour diffusion methods. Where the solvent-exchange method is concerned, ideally the solutions and the poor solvent should have different densities. In addition, the low density solvent should be poured onto the one with higher density very slowly to prevent perturbation and mixing. Sometimes, in order to further reduce the infiltration speed, a buffer layer material, a solvent with a medium density and medium solubility of the semiconductor, is first poured onto the solution. The solvent-vapour diffusion method can significantly reduce the speed of infiltration of the poor solvent into the solution. Other experimental factors can also be optimized. For example, the speed can be controlled by adjusting the temperature to change the volatilization of the solvents, by tuning the concentration and the volume ratio of the solution and the poor solvent. Details of the growth control can be found in the relative sections.
4 Device physics of organic semiconductor crystals in optoelectronics
Single crystals of organic semiconductors are free of grain boundaries and have long-range periodic order as well as minimal traps and defects. Hence, single crystal devices are particularly beneficial for revealing the intrinsic properties of organic semiconductors, hence providing a powerful tool not only for examining structure–property relationships but also for studying fundamental physics in organic semiconductors. This in turn further guides molecular design and synthesis to achieve practical applications of organic semiconductors. In this part, we will focus on the device physics of organic semiconductor crystals in optoelectronics, discuss the factors which influence the performance, investigate the structure–property relationships for achieving high performance devices and examine the charge generation, injection, transport and combination mechanisms. An in-depth study of the device physics will benefit further understanding and the future application of organic semiconductors.
4.1 Device physics in organic single crystal field-effect transistors
Field-effect transistors are a type of devices that have three terminals where the current in the channel can be controlled by an external electric field (gate voltage). Compared with diodes, field-effect transistors are superior with high input resistance, low energy consumption and high signal-to-noise ratio. They are commonly used as signal-amplifiers, oscillators and logic switchers in integrated circuits. Organic semiconductors have attracted much attention in field-effect transistors due to their intriguing advantages such as flexibility, light-weight, ease of functionalization, and the possibility of being fabricated over a large area at low cost. Since the first manufacture of organic field-effect transistors (OFETs) at the end of the 1980s, progress in this field has been particularly rapid. For example, mobility of OFETs has been improved from 10−5 cm2 (V s)−1 (the first OFET, based on polythiophene441) to over 10 cm2 (V s)−1135,442–445 for thin films and from 0.075 cm2 (V s)−1 (first single crystal OFET based on α-sexithiophene in 1996446) to 40 cm2 (V s)−136,430,447 for single crystals. In addition, the studies of organic single crystals with mechanical flexibility make them more promising.309,448 Particularly, organic single crystal field-effect transistors are of great significance for studying the intrinsic charge transport properties, the structure–property relationship and the charge transport mechanism.

4.1.1 Factors which influence the charge transport. 
4.1.1.1 Device configuration. 
A typical organic field-effect transistor is constituted of a semiconducting layer, an insulating layer (gate dielectric) and three terminal electrodes (the gate, drain and source). According to the different position of the layers, organic field-effect transistors can be classified into four different types, as shown in Fig. 28: (i) the bottom gate-bottom contact (BG-BC), (ii) the bottom gate-top contact (BG-TC), (iii) the top gate-bottom contact (TG-BC) and (iv) the top gate-top contact (TG-TC).
 Fig. 28  Four types of configurations for OFETs based on organic semiconductor crystals. 
Four aspects need to be considered during the fabrication of transistors. The first of these is the size of the organic crystals. Usually, it is difficult to fabricate top gate devices for small organic crystals. On the other hand, the top gate-bottom contact mode (TG-BC) and bottom gate-top contact mode (BG-TC) are unsuitable for thick crystals because the charge carriers are inaccessible to the interface between the semiconductor and the dielectric layer.38,449–451 Secondly, the method of preparing the crystals has to be carefully chosen. There is no doubt that the substrate surface plays an important role in the crystal growth if the crystals grow directly on the substrate. Therefore, a suitable substrate should have a smooth surface, a low nucleation density and a low defect density. In addition, the substrate should not be affected by the crystal growth conditions. From this point of view, the top contact mode is probably more useful for obtaining large high quality crystals than the bottom contact configuration because the pre-deposited electrodes in the latter can act as nucleation sites. The third factor needed to be considered is the deposition method for the insulator and electrode layer of the transistors. Normally, because organic crystals are sensitive to both thermal irradiation and solvents, the BG-BC configuration is preferred where the organic crystals are deposited in the last step and the insulator or electrode layers are pre-deposited. The final factor is the injection and outflow of the charge carriers. The performance of transistors based on the top contact mode is superior to the bottom contact one because it usually results in better contact quality between the crystal and the drain/source electrodes. Flexible and thinner crystals (or substrates) also facilitate the formation of good contact.
4.1.1.2 Crystal phase and molecular packing. 
Normally, it is accepted that the intrinsic electronic properties of organic semiconductors are determined by two parameters: the transfer integral and the reorganization energy. They are highly dependent on the chemical structure and the molecular arrangements. There are a lot of literature reports about their definitions, their calculations and how they determine the charge transport.6,49,155,452 Hence, chemical compositions and chemical structures6 from the point of view of their influence on the molecular arrangements and in turn on the transfer integral and reorganization energy will not be discussed here.
With respect to a specified material with a definite chemical structure, the major factor that affects the transfer integral and reorganization energy is its molecular arrangement. Therefore, it is important to find ways to improve the molecular arrangements for optimized charge transport by using different crystal growth techniques. Theoretically, a slow self-assembly process will favour the packing relying on the nature of the material itself. It will result in crystal growth along the direction with the strongest intermolecular interactions. In other words, a thermodynamically stable phase with a lower enthalpy can be achieved, because this process can avoid the external impacts to the greatest extent. The thermodynamically stable phase usually means the phase with the highest performance. For example, PVT is one method which makes it possible to almost eliminate undesirable external effects. Thereby crystals with a thermodynamically stable phase can be obtained, by controlling the temperature of the sublimation and crystallization regions, the temperature gradient and the flow velocity of the carrier gas. By controlling the temperature of the crystallization region, α and β phase CuPc crystals can be achieved at temperatures lower than 192 °C and higher than 224 °C, respectively. This indicates that the α phase is a metastable phase and the β phase is a more thermodynamically stable phase, because a high temperature of crystallization is conducive for a slow deposition of the molecules.63 The crystals of the α phase63 (with the highest mobility of 0.4 cm2 (V s)−1) exhibited a somewhat lower performance than crystals of the β phase62,256,379,381,453 (with the highest mobility of 1.0 cm2 (V s)−1). Another example is that the rubrene crystals with a more thermodynamically stable state belong to the orthorhombic crystal system. These crystals can be obtained by controlling the PVT conditions, and mobility as high as 20 cm2 (V s)−137,38,70,182 was reported. However, fast precipitation was found to favour the kinetically stable state with monoclinic or triclinic phases.347 Drop-casting methods are regarded as another kind of self-assembly process. However, if the solvent is volatile and a high temperature is adopted, the evaporation of the solvent may be a fast process, which will also lead to a kinetically stable phase. An example is BPEA crystals in which either the thermodynamically or kinetically favoured phase can be obtained by a drop-casting process using chlorobenzene and dichloromethane as the solvents respectively.30,67 The fast evaporation of the dichloromethane solution resulted in a kinetically stable phase, which showed a mobility much lower than that of the crystals obtained from the chlorobenzene solution.
In addition to pursuing preferred molecular arrangements, it could be expected that high performance could also be achieved by decreasing the intermolecular distance. Fig. 29 presents an example where the intermolecular interaction was intentionally increased by bending the CuPc crystals. The bent crystals showed a higher average mobility than the straight ones. These results are in good agreement with the theoretical calculations.454 Similar results68 were found by using different solution-shearing speed to reduce the intermolecular distance of TIPS-PEN. This results in a decrease of the π–π stacking distance from 3.33 to 3.08 Å for evaporated unstrained thin films and solution-sheared strained films (at 8 mm s−1) respectively. Theoretical calculations26,43 demonstrated that decreasing the intermolecular distance can exponentially increase the splitting of the HOMO and LUMO energy levels as shown in Fig. 30A. A higher splitting of the energy orbital means a higher transfer integral and this in turn suggests a higher mobility. On the other hand, the splitting does not increase with the lateral increase of the π–π overlap but rather exhibits oscillations in the value. This indicates that the transfer integral is highly dependent on the relevant molecular orbital overlap rather than simply on the spatial overlap between adjacent molecules (Fig. 30B).
 Fig. 29  Schematic and corresponding representative SEM images of (A) straight and (B) bent CuPc crystals, the grids display the crystal lattice in the crystals and the red and green grids represent the distortion of the crystal lattice after compression and stretching respectively. (C) Schematic illustration of molecular packing of bent crystals, with denser molecular packing inside. Reprinted with permission from ref. 454. Copyright © 2011 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
 Fig. 30  Evolution of the electronic splitting of the HOMO and LUMO as a function of the (A) intermolecular distance and (B) the lateral shift distance. Adapted with permission from ref. 43. Copyright © 2002 by the National Academy of Sciences. 
From this analysis, it is clear that the intrinsic property of a particular material is highly dependent on the intermolecular interactions and these can be tuned by different kinds of crystal growth techniques. It is noteworthy that the properties of the devices are affected by the interfaces between the semiconductor and the drain/source electrodes as well as the interface between the semiconductor and the insulator.455 In addition, the charge transport is also dependent on the testing conditions.456 For example, the temperature can affect the drift speed of the charge carriers and the volume of the crystals. The hydrostatic-pressure,457,458 the absorbed species,459 or the traps in the transporting interface all can affect the charge transport properties. In addition, the performance of the transistors is also dependent on the configuration of devices (Section 4.1.1.1), the thickness of crystals as well as the length and width of the conducting channels.
4.1.1.3 Electrodes, dielectrics and interfaces. 
Electrodes, dielectrics and interfaces can also affect the performance of the crystal devices.460–462 The interface between the semiconductor and electrodes is the region where the charge carriers inject into and flow out of the semiconductors. The interface between the semiconductor and the insulator is the region where the charge carriers transport. Meanwhile, the surface of the pre-deposited layers (the insulator layer in the bottom-gate devices and the drain/source electrodes in the bottom-contact devices) can also affect the growth and molecular arrangement of the crystals. These in turn affect the performance of devices.
4.1.1.3.1 Electrodes and semiconductor/electrode interfaces. 
For electrodes, including the gate and drain/source electrodes, a prerequisite is that the material should be highly conductive and should be able to form a uniform electric field. For gate electrodes, different kinds of materials such as highly doped silicon, metals, indium-tin oxide (ITO),463,464 conductive polymers, carbon nanotubes (CNTs)465 and graphene466 can all be used as electrodes (Table 1). In addition, the procedure used in the device fabrication, thickness, transmittance and flexibility must be considered for electrodes in specific applications.467 The drain and source electrodes are directly connected to the active semiconductor layers and determine the injection and the outflow of charge carriers. There are three main factors that influence the interface between the drain/source electrodes and the semiconductor crystals: (1) the tightness of the physical contact; (2) the energy level alignment and (3) the interface compatibility.

With respect to the first issue, the methods where the crystals37,468,469 are stamped onto the pre-deposited electrodes to form devices with bottom contact mode are not conducive to the charge transport. The reason can be ascribed to the rigid nature of the crystals. On the other hand, the stamping technique for placing gold or silver films92,453 onto the crystals to form devices with top contact mode suffers negligible restriction. The reason is that the metal film is flexible enough to form an intimate contact with the crystals. From this point of view, the deposition of electrodes using thermal evaporation or electron beam deposition should form good contact with the crystals. However, the experimental results are not as good as expected. The reason is that the diffusion of metal atoms into the crystals results in some damage of the crystals.470 This can be solved by judicious selection of organic materials as the electrodes (e.g. TTF:TCNQ complex) that can be deposited at low temperature.
Where the second issue is concerned, it is well acknowledged that the work function (WF) of the drain/source electrodes should well match with the HOMO or LUMO energy levels of the semiconductors in order to facilitate charge injection and collection.477–479 There are two methods for approaching this target: (i) selecting suitable electrode materials or modifying the electrodes to fit the Fermi level of the electrode to the HOMO/LUMO energy levels of the semiconductors; (ii) functionalizing the semiconductors to tune their HOMO/LUMO energy levels. For p-type semiconductors, the charge carriers are holes and they should be injected from the Fermi levels of the source electrodes into the HOMO energy levels of the semiconductors.480 Most organic semiconductors possess HOMO energy levels lower than −5.0 eV, which match well with metallic materials having work functions around or higher than 5.1 eV.452 Some examples are gold (5.1 eV),38 platinum (5.6 eV),481 nickel (5.0 eV)482 and carbon (5.0 eV)256 (Table 1). Research has suggested that most of the organic semiconductors with high performance have HOMO energy levels around −5.1 ± 0.3 eV. They are close to the work function of Au (5.1 eV),6 which may be one of the reasons that gold is the most widely used electrode material in p-type field-effect transistors. For n-type semiconductors, the charge carriers are electrons and they should be injected from the Fermi levels of the source electrodes into the LUMO energy levels of the semiconductors. Due to the fact that the LUMO energy levels of most semiconductors are higher than −4.0 eV, the low WF metals such as Ca (2.9 eV),451,483–,485 Mg (3.66 eV),486 Ag (4.26 eV)90,92,483,487 and Al (4.2 eV)488 were preferable for achieving electron transport. Therefore, by judicious selection of materials as the drain/source electrodes that are capable of matching the HOMO/LUMO energy levels of the semiconductors, higher performance devices can be expected. Moreover, p- or n-type semiconductors would be expected to exhibit reverse (electron or hole respectively) transporting characteristics. In other words, through tailoring the drain/source electrode materials, ambipolar properties can be obtained. For example, for TIPS-TAP, if a lower WF metal (Ag) is adopted for the drain/source electrodes, the electron mobility increases several times compared to the devices fabricated using higher WF metal (Au) as the electrodes.92 In addition, if using metals with a low work function, such as Ca,451,484,489,490 Ag487,490 or Mg–Ag alloy,486 most typical p-type semiconductors can exhibit electron mobility or ambipolar properties.451,484,487 The problem is that although the metals with low WF facilitate the electron injection, the hole injection is suppressed sequentially due to the mismatch of the work function with the HOMO energy levels.490 Therefore, a more intriguing and efficient method for achieving ambipolar properties is to utilize an asymmetry electrode configuration. The high WF materials used as one electrode are designed to facilitate the hole injection whereas the low WF materials used as the other electrode are for enhancing the electron injection. Such asymmetrical device configuration was also applied in devices with a view to forming stepwise energy levels among the work function of the drain electrode, the HOMO/LUMO energy levels of the semiconductor and the work function of the source electrode. This stepwise energy levels are helpful for the injection and outflow of the charge carriers.90,380,481 Another method for adjusting the work function of the electrodes is to modify the drain/source electrode.491,492 The work function of the electrodes can be changed by more than 1 eV by using SAMs to modify the electrodes. For example, SAMs with electron-withdrawing groups can increase the work function of the metal electrodes, whereas those with electron-rich groups can negatively shift the work function of the metals. Presently, the most commonly used SAMs are mercaptans, which can form a monolayer on the metals (e.g. Au) anchored through the thiol-group. The thiophenol-modified Au (TP/Au) electrodes493 can enhance the hole mobility from 0.7 to 3.2 cm2 (V s)−1 for BPEA crystals. Another method for modifying the electrodes is by using a thin layer of another material with a work function close to that of the semiconductors. For example, annealing the copper electrodes to form copper oxide (CuxO) can facilitate the hole injection. Compared with the Fermi level of Cu, the CuxO has a similar valance band to the HOMO energy level of the organic semiconductors. Similar results can be obtained by inserting a thin layer of molybdenum oxide (MoOx) between the electrodes and the semiconductor layer. Here, the MoOx has a high work function (5.6 eV) and is supportive to the hole injection into the semiconductors with low HOMO energy levels.208,494 In addition to the adjustment of the work function of the drain/source electrodes, the functionalization of the semiconductor itself is another method which can be used for achieving energy alignment between the work function of the electrodes and the HOMO/LUMO energy levels of the semiconductors. Similarly, electron-withdrawing groups are found to significantly lower the LUMO energy levels of the semiconductors. This is helpful for the good match with the high WF electrodes and makes it possible to avoid using low WF metals due to their low stability.6
Thirdly, in order to improve the interface compatibility between the semiconductor and the drain/source electrodes, there are two approaches: (i) the modification of the traditional metal electrodes by using organic materials such as SAMs; (ii) the direct use of carbon materials or organic conducting materials as the drain/source electrodes, such as graphite, graphene, CNTs, organic charge transfer complexes or conductive polymers. These methods are extremely important for creating devices with crystals grown after the deposition of the drain/source electrodes, because good interface compatibility is helpful for forming a lower density of nucleation sites and better molecular arrangements. The modification of metal electrodes by using organic SAMs is extremely helpful in the case of bottom contact devices.431,495 Sometimes, a thin layer of materials such as insulator sulphur is deposited between the semiconductor and electrodes. This can also improve the contact between the semiconductor and the electrodes and result in higher performance.496 It should be mentioned that the use of carbon materials and organic conductive polymers as electrodes is more efficient for achieving perfect contact between the semiconductors and the electrodes because of their good interface compatibility with organic semiconductors. Carbon materials, such as graphite,497,498 graphene467 and carbon nanotubes (usually adopted as a composite with polymers499), also have aromatic rings as structural units (the same with the organic semiconductors) and therefore are attractive as electrode materials. Graphite was widely adopted for the fabrication of single crystal devices. For instance, graphite ink can be used via painting onto the crystals directly.256 Similarly, carbon nanotubes can be dispersed in organic solvents with different kinds of additives (e.g. polymers to form continuous and homogeneous films for achieving a uniform electric field) and utilized as electrodes. Graphene is ultrathin and flexible, and thereby can lower the device size and reduce the edge effect of the electrodes.500 This suggests a bright future for practical applications in the next generation of transparent and flexible electrodes. Besides carbon materials, organic conductive polymers and charge-transfer complexes can also be used as electrodes. Moreover, polymers, such as polymer blend poly(3,4-ethylenedioxythiophene):poly(styrenesulfonate) (PEDOT:PSS),429,501,502 can be deposited through various solution-processing techniques such as spin-coating and printing techniques. Organic charge-transfer complexes are usually constituted of two parts: an electron-rich system as the donor and an electron-poor system as the acceptor. For example, tetrathiafulvalene:tetracyanoquinodimethane (TTF:TCNQ)475,503,504 is a well-known conductive charge-transfer complex, which can be formed even by two solids of TTF and TCNQ.45,505 These organic electrodes possess similar advantages to those of conducting polymers. Meanwhile, they can be deposited at lower temperature than metals which is essential for crystals in order to improve the contact between the organic semiconductor and the electrodes. For example, by using TTF:TCNQ (−4.64 to 4.78 eV) as the electrode, a mobility as high as 10–11 cm2 (V s)−1 can be obtained for HMTTF (−4.61 eV)506 crystals, while the mobility values based on devices using Ag (4.2 eV) and Au (5.1 eV) were only 0.12 and 0.02 cm2 (V s)−1, respectively.503 This could be attributed to (1) the better match between the work function of TTF:TCNQ and the HOMO energy level of HMTTF; (2) the lower deposition temperature of TTF:TCNQ which ensures smaller damage to the crystals, and (3) the better interface compatibility in the organic–organic materials. Similarly, TCNQ can also form charge transfer complexes with the electron-donor HMTTF and the film of HMTTF:TCNQ possesses smoother morphology than TTF:TCNQ films. This facilitates the formation of a homogeneous electric field and results in higher device performance.506
4.1.1.3.2 Dielectric and semiconductor/dielectric interface. 
In order to discuss the effect of the dielectric layer on the performance of single crystal field-effect transistors, it is necessary to briefly describe the structure and working mechanism of OFETs. When a gate voltage is applied to an OFET, an electric field will be generated in the dielectric layer and then the charge carriers can be induced at the interface between the semiconductor and the insulator through injection from the source electrodes. An OFET can be regarded as a capacitor, in which the gate electrode and the semiconducting layer act as two electrodes and the insulator acts as a dielectric layer. According to the equation Ci = ε0k/d (where Ci is the density of capacitance, ε0 is the dielectric constant in vacuum, k represents the relative dielectric constant and d is the thickness of the dielectric layer), there are two methods for increasing the capacitance of the dielectric layer: (i) increase the dielectric constant by choosing high k materials, and (ii) decrease the thickness of the dielectric layer. Therefore, the insulator can affect the performance of OFETs in many ways: (i) the dielectric constant (ε), which can affect the electric field intensity and the accumulation of the charges; (ii) the compactness, which affects the leakage current (the insulating property) and further influences the power consumption and ultimately the device performance; (iii) the morphology, the surface energy and the compatibility with the semiconductors. These have an enormous effect not only on the charge transport but also on the crystal growth if the crystals grow directly onto the dielectric layer; (iv) the thickness of the insulator, which affects the dielectric field intensity and the leakage current; (v) the trap density on the surface as well as in the bulk of the insulator, which can dramatically change the conductive performance of the device. The dielectric constants of different insulators are summarized in Table 2. It should be noted that the dielectric constant of the same insulator can vary when using different deposition techniques.

A high dielectric constant means a strong ability to induce polarization and a strong capacity to store the charges. Hence, generally, a high k insulator is preferred because a high k dielectric can induce the same electric field at a low drive voltage, and this is expected to exhibit high mobility at low energy consumption.507 On the other hand, if a low-k insulator such as SiO2 is selected, an ultrathin dielectric layer should be adopted in order to increase the capacitance. This will result in a high leakage current and further decrease the device performance. By using high-k insulators as the dielectric layer, a low-voltage operation can be achieved with an operation voltage as low as 1 V. However, for devices with a low-k (e.g. SiO2, or a polymer insulator) dielectric layer, voltages as high as several tens of volts should be applied. Typical high-k insulators507 include Ta2O5, ZrO2, HfO2, TiO2, etc., which can be obtained by anodization, sputtering, e-beam deposition, atomic layer deposition (ALD) or sol–gel methods. Theoretically, a high dielectric constant facilitates the polarization of the dielectric layers when the gate voltage is applied, and this increases the capacitance. A large capacitance is helpful for accumulating more charge carriers at the interface between the semiconductor and the insulator layer in order to achieve high performance. However, the experimental results for rubrene single crystals are quite different.166 The authors concluded that the charge transport in the organic single crystals may be polaron transport. The movement of the polaron will be more hindered by a dielectric layer with a higher dielectric constant. In this situation, lower device mobility will be obtained.165,166,508–510 These have been discussed in Section 2.4. From this point of view, air or vacuum should be good candidates for dielectric layers, because they possess very low dielectric constants (ε = 1), which will result in high mobility. In addition, the defects on the surface of the dielectric layer can be eliminated, which will in turn minimize the charge traps.38,166,182,187,188,511 Similar results179 can also be found for n-type semiconductor PDIF-CN2 crystals. The maximum mobility decreased from 5.1 cm2 (V s)−1 for vacuum dielectrics (ε = 1) to 3.1 cm2 (V s)−1 for Cytop (ε = 2.1) and 2.3 cm2 (V s)−1 for PMMA (ε = 3.8) gate dielectrics.
Polymer insulators are promising for effectively reducing the cost because they are usually obtained by solution-processing techniques. However, thick films (500 nm–1 μm) are desirable in order to eliminate the unavoidable formation of pinholes.507,512 Parylene has attracted much attention, because it is deposited by polymerization and can form compact structures even as thin as 100–200 nm with the absence of pinholes and defects. The fabrication of parylene is similar to the PVT method used to grow single crystals. To summarize, the dimer source of parylene is put into a low temperature zone (∼100 °C), and its vapour is then transported to a high temperature zone (∼700 °C) and pyrolyzed into monomers. Then the monomer is transported to the deposition zone and polymerized into high quality films.9,257,513 The dielectric properties can be adjusted by facile functionalization of the dimer source to form parylene N, parylene C, parylene D and parylene HT with unsubstituted groups, chloro, dichloro and tetrafluoro groups, respectively.51,430,514,515 Due to the absence of solvent and the advantage of the low temperature deposition region, the polymers are widely used in single crystal devices with the top gate mode. For other polymers, one possible method is to introduce a cross-linking agent512,516 to solidify the insulating layer. This makes it possible to adopt a thin dielectric layer with high break-down voltage. For example, 50 nm Cytop (even thinner cytop is achievable by choosing a special device configuration517) exhibits a leakage current 1000 times lower than the drain current after the addition of the cross-linking agent.512 SAMs have also been reported as dielectric layers, because these monolayers usually form compact structures with a smooth surface. The compact structures can result in low leakage current. Typically, the thicknesses of the SAMs are only several nanometers (e.g. 2 nm) which can significantly reduce the driving voltage and power consumption. Normally, the SAMs are anchored onto the substrate with either an inherent or an introduced oxidized layer.518,519 Although polymer insulators have suitable surfaces for growing crystals (smooth surfaces, good compatibility with organic semiconductors, similar surface energies), they always suffer the disadvantages of low thermal stability and high solubility in common solvents. The low thermal stability makes the insulators inappropriate for adoption in melting or PVT crystal growing techniques. It should be noted that polyimide (PI) is a good candidate for use as a thermally stable insulator.520 Where the high solubility is concerned, it faces the challenge to seek suitable orthogonal solvents for growing crystals directly on the insulators or depositing the polymers onto the crystals.
In order to acquire high quality crystals, crystal substrates such as KCl crystals521 have been used for inducing an epitaxial growth. These results suggest the important role of the substrate surface in crystal growth and hence ultimately in the device performance. It is generally accepted that charge carriers transport in the conducting channels with a thickness of one to several molecular layers near the interface between the semiconductor and insulator layers.105,436,461 Hence, the interface also plays a very important role in device performance. This is the reason that the metal oxide dielectric layers are commonly modified by SAMs in order to achieve high device performance. SAMs can decrease or eliminate the defect density at the interface (e.g., hydroxyl groups on metal oxide trap charge carriers) and this modification is an efficient way for improving the device performance.462,522 Meanwhile, the traps also lead to the instability of the device. They can shift the threshold voltage, resulting in hysteresis of the curves. Parylene and cytop are good choices for avoiding this kind of hysteresis,523 because of the trap-free properties. SAMs, especially those with fluoro groups, can also significantly affect the charge distribution at the semiconductor/insulator interface, and thereby can significantly shift the threshold voltage. By suitably adjusting the modification of SAMs, the threshold voltage can be expected to shift to around zero, and thus decrease the energy consumption.255,519,524,525 The decrease or elimination of the traps in the semiconductor/insulator interface is also essential for enhancing the electron transport and producing ambipolar characteristics.526,527 SAM modifications or polymer substrates are encouraging strategies for decreasing the traps in the interface between the gate insulator and the organic crystals. It is worth noting that normally both requirements, including low WF electrodes and the passivation of the dielectric layer, are necessary to achieve ambipolar transport.
In summary, a favourable dielectric layer should possess the following characteristics: (i) a uniform, dense and compact structure in order to decrease the leakage current, (ii) thin enough to achieve low energy consumption and make it possible to scale down the device, (iii) a smooth surface to decrease the density of nucleation sites, (iv) a defect-free surface to reduce the charge trap; (v) a compatible surface with the organic semiconductor, (vi) a suitable dielectric constant to achieve high performance and low-voltage operation for low energy consumption, and (vii) low cost and a reasonable efficient process in order to fabricate the device over a large area. Sometimes, in order to fulfil most of or all these requirements, two or more dielectric layers are adopted to combine their best merits. For example, a double layer of metal oxide dielectrics (e.g. TiO2/Al2O3) can combine the merit of one high k material which helps to achieve low energy consumption and one large band gap material which helps to reduce the leakage current.528,529 The combination of metal oxides and polymers (such as PMMA/SiO2)208,507 can combine the merit of the high k metal oxide to achieve low voltage operation and the advantage of the polymeric compatible surface to form high quality crystals and low defect density.
4.1.1.4 Contact resistance. 
There are many factors which can affect or even determine the final performance of organic transistors. For example, the temperature can change the mobility166,182,530 as discussed in Section 2.4. Moreover, the dielectric materials, the electrode materials, the thickness of the dielectric layers, and the channel dimensions478,531 all can affect the device performance as mentioned above. In this section, we will focus on the influence of contact resistance on the performance of single crystal organic field-effect transistors.
The contact between crystals and electrodes can be observed by using scanning Kelvin probe microscopy (SKPM).532 A four-probe configuration is often useful for examining the contact resistance. Two additional electrodes are deposited between the drain and source electrodes along the conducting channel, and act as voltage probes (VXX in Fig. 31A).19,99,179,182,252,257,447,515,533–535 By using this method, the contact resistance between the crystal and drain/source electrodes can be calculated by using the whole resistance minus the channel resistance (proportional to the channel length). The mobility can be defined by using the distance of the two additional electrodes (as the channel length) and the voltage drop between these two electrodes. For example, under the same conditions, the mobility of rubrene crystals was calculated at around 18 cm2 (V s)−1 by the two-probe methods (the traditional device with only two electrodes, i.e. the drain and source electrodes). On the other hand, the mobility estimated using four-probe methods was as high as 40 cm2 (V s)−1 for the same crystal (Fig. 31B).447 These results suggest that the contact resistance plays an important role in the device performance. A precise estimation of the device performance is crucial for investigating the intrinsic properties of the crystals, the structure–property relationship and the charge transport mechanism.
 Fig. 31  (A) Schematic diagram of a device which can estimate the intrinsic mobility by removing the contact resistance. The voltage measured along the channel (VXX) is used for the four-probe method and the voltage measured perpendicular to the channel (VXY) is for investigation of the Hall-effect. Reprinted with permission from ref. 179. Copyright © 2012 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim; (B) the transfer curve (ID as a function of VG) obtained from the traditional two-probe method and the channel conductivity (σ = ID/ΔV) as a function of VG obtained using the four-probe method. Reprinted with permission from ref. 447. Copyright © 2007 American Institute of Physics; (C) the relationship between the transversal Hall voltage and the magnetic field B. Reprinted with permission from ref. 179. Copyright © 2012 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
The Hall effect can be used for exploring the intrinsic performance of the crystals, because one needs to examine only the voltage as it is not dependent on the contact resistance.179,183,184,275,536 It describes the characteristics of the carriers in the semiconductor when an electric and a magnetic field are applied. When a magnetic field is applied to the field-effect transistor perpendicular to the channel, the charge carriers in the conducting channel will drift to one side of the channel due to the Lorentz force and result in electric potential difference (VXY in Fig. 31A). What is different from the four-probe method is that the two electrodes for the Hall voltage need to be fabricated perpendicular to the conducting channel, and the Hall voltage is obtained from the transversal voltage. Due to the weak dependence on the contact resistance, the experimentally obtained Hall mobility is usually higher than that from the two-probe method. An example of the Hall effect obtained from PDIF-CN2 crystals is shown in Fig. 31C. By using surface functionalization of the organic crystals with PFPE,149 Podzorov and co-workers claimed that the intrinsic mobility can be obtained due to the decrease of trap densities. Moreover, the trap-healing effect makes the observation of an extremely low-noise Hall effect possible, permitting Hall effect measurements in low magnetic fields (<1 T) and at small excitation drain currents (∼1 nA).
Assuming that the contact resistance values at every electrode are the same, the resistance of a transistor will be proportional to the channel length. A method for deducting the contact effect is to fabricate many devices with different channel lengths on the same single crystal,537 which is called the transfer line method (TLM). By using this method, the contact resistance can be removed and the “intrinsic” mobility can then be estimated. Likewise, increasing the channel length is favorable for achieving the intrinsic mobility, in which the proportion of contact resistance within the whole resistance can be reduced and thereby the short-channel effect is minimized.165


4.1.2 Manipulating the factors which influence the charge transport. 
4.1.2.1 Manipulating organic crystals. 
Usually, organic semiconductor crystals are very sensitive to heat and electron beams; therefore, the process for the deposition of insulators or electrodes on crystals is extremely important for device performance. Generally, the bottom gate bottom contact (BG-BC) configuration is most suitable for excluding external influences on the crystals. In this configuration, the crystals are deposited lastly. Hence, all the means such as photolithography, EBL, electron-beam evaporation, thermal evaporation, or solution-processing techniques (spin-coating, drop-casting, dip-coating, etc.) can be adopted188,189,495,538 for the deposition of the electrodes and the dielectric layer. For example, EBL539 or AFM nanolithography540 can be used to fabricate devices with the desired channel dimensions.
Methods such as PVT,101 drop-casting,44,79,87,301,538 dip-coating385 or ink-jet printing431 can all be utilized to grow single crystals on the pre-deposited channels for producing devices with BG-BC mode. It should be emphasized that damage on pre-deposited layers (the insulators and the electrodes) should be avoided during the crystal growth. For example, if a polymer is used as the dielectric layer, high temperature is not suitable for the crystal growth in order to exclude the deterioration of the dielectric properties such as insulating properties, smooth surface and compact structure. For solution-processing techniques, solvents that dissolve the polymer are inappropriate. In order to obtain high quality crystals and precisely control the location of the crystals, modifications of electrodes and insulators are usually employed to decrease the nucleation density of the crystals. This selective modification has been discussed in the section on crystal growth.386
The electrostatic-bonding method37 can be used for flipping the pre-grown crystals onto the channel directly,495 by mechanically placing the pre-grown crystals or dropping a suspension of the pre-grown crystals82 onto the channel. The crystals can be transferred by shaking off, mechanically transferring by forceps or probes, rubbing the substrate with pre-grown crystals, by using PDMS stamps, or floating the crystals from their suspensions.32,38,62,99,180,428,541–544 For this technique, there are almost no special requirements for the dielectric layer and drain/source electrodes. For instance, air gap or vacuum dielectrics38,99,166,182,187,188,511,545 can also be realized. Similarly, ionic liquids546–549 and even organic semiconductor crystals550 (a Schottky junction is used to reduce the leakage current by using a rubrene crystal as the dielectric layer and Ca as the gate electrode) have also been reported as dielectric layers by using this technique. A typical process for the fabrication of BG-BC devices is shown in Fig. 32. All the other layers are fabricated before crystal location. Another merit of this method is that the crystals can be moved away and placed again so that the anisotropic property of the same crystal can be investigated.
 Fig. 32  Schematic diagrams of the electrostatic bonding technique for achieving crystal devices, in which other layers are fabricated before the crystal location (bottom gate-bottom contact). Reprinted with permission from ref. 37. Copyright © 2004 the American Association for the Advancement of Science. 
As mentioned in the sections on interfacial effects between the semiconductor and drain/source electrodes, this method used for fabricating crystal devices usually results in large contact resistance.37,469 One reason could be ascribed to the rigid nature of the crystals. Small crystals with flexibility can adhere onto substrates more tightly.189 The modification of drain/source electrodes is also helpful for lowering the contact resistance between the electrodes and the crystals.250,495,535 Another efficient method for getting intimate contact is to utilize a flexible substrate and insulator (e.g. PDMS).37,188,468,551 For example, rubrene crystals on a non-flexible substrate (SiO2) showed a mobility of about 2.6 cm2 (V s)−1. However, when a flexible substrate was used, such as PVP as the dielectric layer and kapton or PET as the substrate, its mobility reached as high as 4.6 cm2 (V s)−1 with a lower threshold voltage and a higher on/off ratio.468
4.1.2.2 Manipulating the dielectric layer and the semiconductor/dielectric interface. 
The utilization of traditional top gate mode (as in organic transistors) meets a big challenge for organic semiconductors. The most critical reason is that organic semiconductors are sensitive to heat, electron beams, solvents and so forth. Hence, the deposition of a dielectric layer on organic semiconductors will affect the device performance, especially when high temperature or solution-processes are utilized. As for organic semiconductor crystals, there is another challenge, i.e. the difficulty in achieving large organic semiconductor crystals.552 Devices using air as the dielectric in the top gate mode can be achieved by directly placing the needle probe above the channel as the gate electrode.553 This method is free from the deposition of the dielectric layer on the crystals; however, it faces its own drawbacks.
The deposition of a dielectric layer on crystals should be approached at low temperature to avoid thermal irradiation to the crystals. When solution-processing techniques are used, the dielectric layer should be obtained from an orthogonal solvent. One strategy is to utilize vapour deposition to deposit the polymer insulators, such as parylene, which can be deposited at room temperature using chemical vapour deposition.152,252,256,257,430,497 This room-temperature and solvent-free deposition eliminates the potential thermal damage and solvent dissolution of the crystals. In addition, the compact structure of the parylene film is helpful for obtaining low current leakage with a thin dielectric layer. Additionally, the dielectric constant of parylene is very low, which facilitates a high mobility. For instance, a mobility as high as 31.3 cm2 (V s)−1 was obtained from a top gate-top contact device (TG-TC) by using parylene as the dielectric layer and C8-BTBT as the semiconducting layer.430 The highest performance based on CuPc single crystals was also obtained by using this kind of device configuration with parylene as the dielectric layer.256 Vapour deposition is not only effective for polymers like parylene, but it also works effectively for small molecules such as 6,13-pentacenequinone (PQ).36 Slow vacuum evaporation can lead to compact PQ films at low thickness (∼200 nm) with low leakage current. Based on this PQ dielectric layer, pentacene crystal transistors exhibit a mobility of up to 40 cm2 (V s)−1.
Some mechanical ways, such as stamping or micro-contact printing techniques, have been developed for transferring insulator films onto crystals. These can eliminate the thermal and solvent damage on the crystals. For example, a 9,10-diphenylanthracene (DPA) single crystal534 was attached onto the surface of the rubrene crystals and it acted as the gate dielectric layer. The traps in the interface between the rubrene crystals and the dielectric layer of the DPA crystals are predicted to be negligible. Podzorov and co-workers554 reported the fabrication of a device by laminating the insulating polymer membrane onto the crystals as the dielectric layer. It was achieved by suspending the membrane above the crystal and then pulling the membrane tightly onto the crystal to form an intimate contact. The schematic diagram of this vacuum lamination approach is presented in Fig. 33. This method is free from high temperature processes, does not need the solvent and can be applied onto any surface. It is particularly useful for crystal surfaces which may affect the growth of the insulator and result in loose structures and high leakage current.128,554 In addition, due to the softness and flexibility of the polymer membrane, the process can be repeated many times onto the crystals without destroying them.
 Fig. 33  Schematic diagrams of the vacuum lamination approach for achieving crystal devices with top gate-top contact mode. The polymer membrane (Mylar®) is laminated onto the crystal as the dielectric layer by vacuum pulling. Reprinted with permission from ref. 554. Copyright © 2011 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Another approach for obtaining polymer dielectrics is to use an insulating solution, in which the solvent is orthogonal to the semiconductor.555 For example, a polymer electrolyte556 deposited by drop-casting its acetonitrile solution has been reported as the gate dielectric layer. Acetonitrile is chosen because it is an anti-solvent for the semiconductor. In order to further reduce the influence of the solution on the crystal, a buffer layer can be pre-deposited onto the crystal. Similarly, a droplet of insulator solution onto large crystals to form a dielectric layer was also reported. The leakage current can be reduced by minimizing the overlap between the dielectric layer and the drain/source electrodes. For instance, a polymer gel electrolyte557 in acetonitrile was drop cast on rubrene crystals to form 100–300 μm droplets as the dielectric layer. A gold wire was placed onto the electrolyte to act as the gate electrode. Ionic liquids558 can also be adopted as the dielectric layer by placing a droplet onto the crystals. It has also been reported that pure water can be utilized as the gate dielectric.559 Water is incapable of dissolving typical organic semiconductors and can be dropped onto the crystals to just cover the conducting channel. Similarly, aqueous solutions560–562 containing media such as NaCl have also been adopted. These devices can be used as PH sensors or biosensors. Lee and co-workers563 reported devices based on P3HT nanofibers (the fabrication procedure is shown in Fig. 34). Electrospinning techniques were used to achieve long and continuous fibres. Ionic liquids and poly(ethyleneglycol) diacrylate (PEG-DA) are exploited as the dielectric layer in order to decrease the influence of the deposition procedure on the crystals. The polymer is polymerized after the deposition of the monomer and the UV cross-linking initiator.
 Fig. 34  Schematic diagrams of a fabrication procedure for a top gate-bottom contact device based on electrospinning of P3HT fibers. Reprinted with permission from ref. 563. Copyright © 2010 American Chemical Society. 
4.1.2.3 Manipulating the source/drain electrodes and the semiconductor/electrode interface. 
In earlier work, drain/source electrodes were fabricated by manually painting onto the crystals directly. The painted electrodes can be Ag,102,515 Au564 or graphite paste,256,257,497 Ag-epoxy,36,57 or even metal nanoparticles with polymers. Thermal evaporation has been widely reported for the deposition of drain/source electrodes,252,257 although this method may result in thermal irradiation of the crystals and metal atoms may diffuse into the crystals, leading to deterioration of the device performance. However, it exhibits some advantages such as (i) the formation of a smooth and continuous film, (ii) the absence of solvents which avoids any potential dissolution of the crystal and (iii) free from damage induced by techniques such as EBL and photolithography. In addition, it can eliminate the mechanical damage occurring in the painting methods. It should be noted that these evaporated electrodes430,503,565,566 can be metals or organic charge transfer salts. Of these materials, the TTF:TCNQ complex is one of the most promising materials because of its low evaporation temperature and possibility of forming intimate contact with organic semiconductors. Micro-contact printing and ink-jet printing (e.g. PEDOT:PSS complex502) can also be used to deposit the drain/source electrodes. This is a superior pathway for achieving electrode arrays.
For small crystals, the manipulation of the source and drain electrodes precisely onto the crystals is one of the toughest issues. The first report of deposition of source/drain electrodes onto individual micro/nanometer sized crystals utilizes a gold wire as the shadow mask.62 The gold wire produces a channel length of 20–25 μm, similar to its diameter. By slightly and parallelly moving the gold wire to deposit the electrodes twice or thrice, the channel length can be even narrower, e.g. ∼5 μm,11,16,62,381 which leads to successful device fabrication based on a crystal with a size over ∼5 μm. Similarly, polymeric fibres (normally, ∼20 μm) can also be used as shadow masks.273,300,340,355 If the electrode materials are changed during the second deposition, asymmetric electrodes can be obtained.90 The process is presented in Fig. 35. The asymmetric drain/source electrodes are helpful for forming stepwise energy levels among the drain electrode, the semiconductor crystal and the source electrode. These stepwise energy levels facilitate the charge transport in the transistors.90 Although the painting technique can also give asymmetric electrodes,102 the multi-deposition through movement of the shadow mask is superior in forming smooth and regular electrodes on micro/nanometer sized crystals and with the absence of mechanical damage.
 Fig. 35  Schematic diagrams of a fabrication procedure for deposition of drain/source electrodes by using the shadow mask technique (copper grid or micro/nanometer sized wires/ribbons as the mask). Reprinted with permission from ref. 11. Copyright © 2008 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Another pioneering work used a copper grid as the shadow mask.96 Compared with the painting technique (which normally has channel lengths around or even longer than 1 mm252,257), the copper grid mask provides an efficient way for fabricating devices with small dimensions. A shorter channel length can be obtained by using grids with narrow bars and channel lengths as short as 5 μm have also been reported.567 The channel length can also be decreased by using the shadow effect. By simply changing the relative position between the substrate and the material source, e.g. by rotating the substrate, an oblique incidence can be achieved leading to deposition of the electrodes beneath the mask near its edge.481 This oblique incidence also provides an approach for fabricating asymmetric electrodes, by changing the source material during the second deposition. Theoretically, different types of shadow masks can be used to provide the shadow effect, such as copper grids,438,481 tungsten wires568 or organic crystal ribbons.382
A further decrease in the channel dimension was designed by using organic micro/nanometer sized wires/ribbons as shadow masks.16,54,55,63,67,85,97,302,382,569 The channel length was dependent on the diameter/width of the mask, so that it could be decreased to nanometers. If thinner or “edge-on” crystals are employed, even narrower channels can be obtained.570 In addition, the small mask can be electrostatically bonded onto the probe tip, which makes it feasible to transfer the wire/ribbon mask to any desired place for electrode deposition. Moreover, due to the flexibility of the organic wires/ribbons and their electrostatic bonding effect with the probe tip, this technique can eliminate the mechanical damage which can happen in the other shadow mask techniques. The wire/ribbon mask can also be replaced by other films such as gold films.348,571 It is noteworthy that because of the small size and flexibility of these shadow masks, it is convenient to fabricate devices by crosswise or parallelly placing two or more wires/ribbons as shadow masks on the same crystal. This means it is possible to form 4 or 6 electrodes on an individual crystal which has dimensions of only several micrometers. Lee and co-workers572 reported an electrospinning method for fabricating electrode arrays. Well-aligned polymeric nanowire arrays were fabricated onto the semiconductors using electrospinning and used as shadow masks to deposit metal films. Using this method, electrode arrays with channel lengths scaled down to 100 nm were achieved after removal of the shadow mask.
Even though the shadow mask techniques make it possible to fabricate drain/source electrodes on small crystals, the thermal irradiation onto the crystals is inevitable during the vapour evaporation process. Although some efforts have been made to eliminate the diffusion of the metal atoms into the crystal lattice, such as by increasing the distance between the material source and the crystals, or lowering the temperature of the crystals,257 it is difficult to avoid the damage of the ordered crystal structure. In a pioneering work,449 Bao and colleagues first fabricated divided, round electrode arrays onto the substrate followed by placing a semiconductor crystal into the center of these round electrode arrays using an electrostatic-bonding method. Then a pre-deposited drain/source pair on the PDMS substrate was flipped and pressed onto the crystal. Hence, by moving the top contacted drain/source electrode pair, this method can be used to investigate the performance along the different directions without repeatedly moving the fragile crystals. In order to eliminate thermal irradiation and decrease the mechanical damage, Hu and co-workers explored a “stamping” technique by mechanically transferring the pre-deposited metallic films30,38,80,92,94,105,119,188,342,378,453,454,573 or conducting nanowires379 onto the crystals as electrodes. This process is presented in Fig. 36. A small piece of an electrode film is transferred from a pre-deposited film by using the micromanipulator probe of a probe station. Then the small piece of the electrode film is placed on the crystals directly to form the drain/source electrode. The thickness of the electrode film is about 100 nm, which is dependent on the scalability and ductility of the materials.92 It should be mentioned that channel lengths as narrow as several micrometers can also be obtained using this method, which, therefore, can meet the challenge of fabricating devices onto small crystals and can decrease or eliminate the mechanical, thermal or electron beam damage resulting from other techniques. Additionally, this method can also provide asymmetric drain/source electrodes by stamping two different electrode strips (e.g. a gold and a silver film) as the drain and source electrodes respectively (Fig. 36B). Compared with the aforementioned techniques for fabricating asymmetric drain/source electrodes, this method is extremely superior due to its facile operation and negligible damage to the crystals.
 Fig. 36  Schematic diagrams of a fabrication procedure for deposition of (A) symmetric and (B) asymmetric drain/source electrodes through a “stamping” technique by transferring the pre-deposited metal films onto the crystals. Adapted with permission from ref. 92. Copyright © 2011 The Royal Society of Chemistry. 


4.1.3 Highlights of important factors for high performance organic field-effect transistors. 
It is believed that organic semiconductors with mobilities greater than or equal to 1.0 cm2 (V s)−1 are necessary for practical applications, because this value is comparable with the mobility of amorphous silicon. In this section, we will summarize the organic semiconductors with reported crystal mobilities greater than or equal to this value in Table 3. Their chemical structures, molecular arrangements and crystal growth methods are also shown for reference. Based on these comparisons and analyses, some rules for high performance devices are expected to be drawn, which are helpful for revealing the intrinsic properties of the crystals, investigating the device physics, studying the structure–property relationship and unveiling the charge injection and transport mechanism. For some materials, such as rubrene and pentacene, because there are many publications referring to their crystal devices, only some representative results are presented here.
