Through careful analyses of the data presented in Table 3, some useful conclusions can be drawn and will be highlighted here, for further understanding of the fundamental device physics and the charge injection and transport mechanisms, and consequently promoting the practical applications of organic semiconductors:
(i) The most important factor dominating the device performance is the nature of the material itself. The performance presented here should be quite close to the intrinsic properties of these materials, due to the absence of grain boundaries, the minimal defects and the perfect order of the crystals. Since the relationship between the chemical structure and the property is not within the scope of this review, the universal rules for the design and synthesis of organic semiconductors are not discussed here. Issues such as the importance of a planar chemical structure, extended π-systems, C2 symmetry and the π-stacking mode have been well discussed elsewhere.6,452
(ii) The anisotropic properties of the charge transport in the crystals are comprehensible, which can be ascribed to the structural anisotropy. Simply speaking, during the movement of the charge carriers, the micro-environment (different atoms, chemical bonds, intermolecular interactions, intermolecular distance, etc.) along different directions is different, hence leading to different transport characteristics. In other words, the charge transport is highly dependent on the electronic coupling and electron–phonon coupling, and they are affected by the surrounding atmosphere. From Table 3, it is clear that the 5 examples, such as DBTDT, DPV-ant, rubrene and 4,4′-bis((E)-2-(naphthalen-2-yl)vinyl)-1,1′-biphenyl (BNVBP), show strong anisotropic properties. Details of the charge transport mechanism and the internal reasons for the anisotropy are discussed in Section 2.
(iii) It is generally believed that the mobility of an organic crystal depends on its molecular arrangement. The arrangement affects both the integral transfer and the reorganization energy. Hence, for future applications, it is of great significance to ensure that the conducting channel is along the preferred orientation of the charge transport during the device fabrication. As shown in Table 3, besides the examples which show anisotropic mobility, the highest device performances of the other materials are usually obtained when the preferred molecular orientation, i.e., the strongest intermolecular interactions such as π–π stacking directions (e.g. rubrene37), is along the conducting channel. For a herringbone packing mode, similar results can be found and indicate that closer C–H⋯π interactions lead to higher performance (e.g. DPV-Ant,55 DBTDT54). For polymers, the intramolecular interactions are the strongest relevant interactions. Therefore, the highest performance obtained for polymer crystals is along the direction which is parallel to the polymer chains. More details on the structure–property relationship can be found in Sections 2 and 4.1.1.2.
(iv) The energy alignment between the HOMO/LUMO energy levels of organic crystals and the Fermi levels of the source/drain electrodes is very essential for the device fabrication, in order to facilitate charge injection from the source electrode into the HOMO/LUMO energy levels as well as the subsequent outflow from the semiconductor into the drain electrode. The HOMO energy levels of most p-type semiconductors lie around −5.1 ± 0.3 eV. Hence, Au, Pt or carbon are excellent candidates for the drain/source electrodes. The LUMO energy levels of most n-type semiconductors are higher than −4.0 eV, and therefore Ag or Mg–Ag alloys (with even lower Fermi levels) are better choices than Au. It is found that cautious selection of the electrodes to ensure stepwise energy levels can facilitate charge transport for high performance devices.90,481 These asymmetric electrodes are also capable of achieving ambipolar characteristics. Such energy alignments have been discussed in Section 4.1.1.3.1 and the techniques for realizing stepwise energy levels can be found in Section 4.1.2. In addition, suitable modification of the electrodes can change the WF of the electrodes and thereby facilitate the charge injection, leading to enhanced charge transport properties.
(v) The dielectric layer plays a key role in the performance of organic transistors (for details see Sections 2.4 and 4.1.1.3.2). For example, it is believed that the dielectric constant affects the capacitance of the transistors and the polarization of the charge carriers. According to the polaron theory, a low dielectric constant will result in high mobility. As shown from the data in Table 3, it is interesting that high performance devices are always based on insulators with low dielectric constants, such as SiO2, parylene, cytop, PI, PDMS, PVP, etc., and devices with air or vacuum as the dielectric layers can exhibit even higher performance37,38 (for the dielectric constants of these materials, please refer to Table 2).
(vi) The interfacial compatibility also plays an important role in device performance (for details see Sections 4.1.1 and 4.1.2). This will not only affect the crystal growth on the substrate, but also the density of traps and defects. That is the reason why most of the dielectric layers or drain/source electrodes are modified by SAMs or other interfacial layers, with a view to inducing suitable molecular arrangements, reducing the trap density and decreasing the damage of the latter-layer deposition onto the organic crystals. From this point of view, organic and flexible insulators are beneficial for forming intimate contact with the crystals. Air or vacuum dielectrics can eliminate defects between the dielectrics and the organic crystals, which is helpful for achieving high performance devices. The decrease in trap density is useful for creating high performance devices and ambipolar properties.
(vii) The crystal growth conditions can also significantly affect the device performance (see Section 3). For example, crystals obtained by the PVT method usually result in higher performance due to the absence of any contamination. Similarly, drop-casting, slow solvent-exchange and solvent-vapour diffusion techniques are beneficial for accessing high quality crystals. This can be attributed to the slow self-assembly process. Thermal or solvent annealing processes are expected to further optimize the molecular arrangements and examples can be found for TIPS-PEN crystals.
In addition to the factors discussed, the device configuration, the method of device fabrication (e.g. painting, vacuum evaporation or “stamping” method for fabricating drain/source electrodes), the channel dimension (channel length,531 width and thickness of the crystals) all can affect the device performance. For example, it has been found that the increase of channel length will shift the threshold voltage to more positive values for rubrene crystals.531 This behaviour is ascribed to the change of space charge transferred from the contacts into the semiconductor. Also, the increase in the growth temperature will increase the activity of the molecules, and thereby increase the intermolecular distance. This can be inferred from the lattice parameters of rubrene crystals obtained at different temperatures.70 The imposed external forces will also affect the molecular arrangement (e.g. the solution-shearing method for growing crystals68,410 or intentional bending of the crystals454). These of course lead to a subsequent variation of the properties. In addition, other factors such as the conditions used for device measurements (environment, pressure, temperature,630etc.) will also influence the device performance. For example, the mobility of rubrene exhibited positive and negative pressure coefficients depending on whether it was measured below or above 600 MPa respectively. This effect is reversible when sweeps were repeated back and forth, which suggests that the pressure not only changes the intermolecular distance but also causes additional molecular displacement.186 Also, it was observed that the switch-on of high-vacuum gauges also causes a decrease of the surface mobility and an increase of the threshold voltage in vacuum-gap transistors, probably because the gauges can generate electrically neutral free radicals on the surfaces of semiconductors that act as surface traps.459


4.1.4 Devices based on organic single crystal transistors. 
Because of the absence of grain boundaries, the long-range periodic order as well as the minimized traps and defects, the devices based on single crystals are ideal candidates for ascertaining the factors which influence the device performance, examining the charge generation, injection, transport and combination mechanisms, investigating the structure–property relationships and guiding the future application of organic semiconductors.
4.1.4.1 Inverters and integrated circuits. 
Inverters, one of the basic logic circuits, can be constructed by using one or two transistors. One of the gate electrodes acts as the input and the shared drain/source electrode is regarded as the output. Logically the output is opposite to the input. The truth table is shown in Fig. 37A. Inverters can be fabricated by a wide range of means.631 One simple method is to use an n-type or p-type transistor coupled with a resistor (Fig. 37B). However, there are several disadvantages. First, when the transistor is on, in other words, the input voltage value is higher than the threshold voltage (high input), there is a current flowing through the device. This current will lead to high power consumption, low processing speed and an output voltage lying in the range between VDD and zero rather than an output voltage of 0 (low output). Albeit useful, increasing the load resistance is inadequate for shifting the output voltage to 0 and sharpening the transition region between the high output (VDD) and the low output (0). Secondly, the size of the resistor is usually much larger than that of the transistor. This will obstruct the scaling down of the circuits. Organic inverters consisting of two transistors with the same type (p- or n-type) can also be exploited (Fig. 37C and D). They have the advantage of facile fabrication because only one kind of semiconductor and electrode are adopted. Moreover, both of them occupy a smaller size and have a better overall performance compared with the resistor loaded invertor (Fig. 37B). The problem is that the leakage current still exists when the driver is on, because the load is always active. It is well recognized that the best configuration is the complementary inverter, which contains one n-type and one p-type transistor, as shown in Fig. 37E.
 Fig. 37  Schematic diagrams of inverters. (A) Truth table; (B) p-type inverter with resistor load; (C) p-type inverter with enhancement load (saturated); (D) p-type inverter with depletion load; (E) complementary inverter. 
The power consumption of a complementary inverter is negligible because one of the transistors is always off. And the voltage transfer characteristics exhibit a full output voltage swing between 0 V and VDD with a sharp transition. In addition, the complementary inverter also operates robustly and has a better noise margin. However, the problem for organic semiconductors is that there are few n-type or ambipolar semiconductors with high mobility and high stability, as mentioned in the above sections. This creates a mismatch between the n-type and p-type mobilities.632 In addition, it is difficult to align the HOMO (for the p-type semiconductor) and LUMO energy levels (for the n-type semiconductor) of two semiconductors to facilitate hole and electron injection simultaneously. Nevertheless, complementary inverters (Fig. 37E) based on organic semiconductor crystals have been reported by several groups. For example, tetramethylpentacene (TMPC) and N,N′-di[2,4-difluorophenyl]-3,4,9,10-perylenetetracarboxylic diimide (PTCDI)577 are adopted in complementary inverters as the p-type and n-type semiconductor crystals respectively. Here the p-type TMPC acts as the load and the n-type PTCDI acts as the driver. A gain of 4.2 is obtained from this inverter. A similar example379 is obtained by using CuPc and F16CuPc as the p-type and n-type semiconductor crystals respectively. Here F16CuPc and CuPc act as the load and the driver respectively. As shown in Fig. 38A–C, SnO2:Sb nanowires are adopted as the electrodes. The voltage transfer characteristics are obtained by applying the input voltage (Vin) onto gate A and the power source (VDD) onto the source electrode (No. 1) of the n-type semiconductor with the p-channel source electrode (No. 5) grounded. Sharp switching character is observed with a gain of up to 7 (Fig. 38D). In another example,79 inverters consisting of n- and p-type crystal arrays are achieved through drop-casting the suspension of the pre-grown crystals onto the patterned electrodes. The inverters exhibit nearly symmetrical voltage transfer characteristics and gate switching, which could be attributed to the good match of the device performance and similar threshold voltage. It should be noted that the match between the electric performance of n- and p-type channels can also be obtained by adjusting the channel dimensions and geometries.577 By using C8-BTBT and PDIF-CN2 as p- and n-type semiconductors respectively, Soeda and co-workers113 reported a complementary inverter which exhibited a maximum gain of 120. They ascribed the high performance to the high mobility of the two materials, the minimized shallow-trap density, the high crystallinity of the two semiconductors and the absence of grain boundaries. Bao and co-workers404 fabricated a complementary inverter based on the crystals which were grown by using droplet-pinned crystallization. In order to realize the p-type and n-type crystals on the same substrate, the substrate surface area between the two droplets was treated by UV-ozone to prevent the inter-diffusion of the two solutions. Due to the high performance of both crystals, inverters with gains as high as 155 were observed. Ambipolar semiconductors are favorable for simplifying the device fabrication process because the transistors based on ambipolar semiconductors can be used as either the load or the driver. An attempt was reported by Zhu and co-workers297 based on co-crystals by using a D–A system where DPTTA was the donor and TCNQ was the acceptor. Inverters based on these co-crystals exhibited a maximum gain of 10 with a simple device configuration where Au was used as the electrodes.
 Fig. 38  (A and B) Schematic diagrams and (C) SEM images of an organic circuit containing an n-type F16CuPc nanowire, a p-type CuPc nanowire and six SnO2:Sb nanowires as electrodes, (D) an example inverter by applying the input voltage onto gate A and the power source onto the source electrode (No. 1) of the n-type semiconductor with the p-channel source electrode (No. 5) grounded, (E) various potential device applications can be achieved by using different connections. Reprinted with permission from ref. 379. Copyright © 2009 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
The lack of n-type or ambipolar organic semiconductors with high mobility and stability makes it difficult to fabricate high performance complementary inverters. To get around this problem, a hybrid structure is a good choice. This can be achieved by using high performance p-type organic semiconductors and high performance n-type inorganic semiconductors for the inverters. For example, CuPc and ZnO nanowires were integrated into complementary inverters,378 with a maximum gain of 18 obtained by using ZnO as the load and CuPc as the driver. A maximum gain of 29 was achieved by reversing the roles of the two crystals. Judicious choice of different electrode materials to match the HOMO and LUMO energy levels of the crystal is another way to make high performance inverters. Suitable electrode materials and configurations can facilitate hole and electron injection simultaneously. Efforts484 using Au and Ca as electrodes for rubrene inverters were reported in the literature. In order to enhance electron transport, the electron conducting channel was designed short but wide. The inverter gain was as high as 140, which could be ascribed to the high switching performance in both the p- and n-channel transistors. Finally, it should be mentioned that other inverter configurations are also applicable for organic semiconductors. For example, two DPV-Ant single crystals,55 which exhibit a high mobility of up to 4.3 cm2 (V s)−1, were utilized in a p-channel inverter with depletion load. The crystals were transferred from pre-grown substrates (using the PVT method) by using a mechanical probe. The inverter exhibited ideal transfer curves with gain as high as 80.
Complex logic circuits are crucial for the potential application of organic semiconductors. Hu and co-workers379 demonstrated the fabrication of logic gates of NOR and NAND by using nanowire crystals of CuPc as the p-type semiconductor, F16CuPc as the n-type semiconductor and SnO2:Sb as the electrodes, respectively. The circuits exhibited low operation voltage (<10 V), low current (<4 pA) and low power dissipation (<40 pW). Such performance was ascribed to the high quality of the semiconductors and electrodes and the energy level matching between them.
In a short summary, because of its negligible power consumption, a complementary inverter consisting of an n-type transistor and a p-type transistor is preferable. There is no doubt that high and comparable performances of the two transistors are favourable to realize high performance inverters. In order to facilitate charge transport, cautious selection of the electrode materials and design of the device configuration are indispensable. For example, the material of the shared electrode should be suitable for matching both the HOMO of the p-type semiconductor and the LUMO of the n-type semiconductor.
4.1.4.2 Photoswitches and phototransistors. 
One important property of semiconductors is that they have appropriate energy gaps, which can absorb light with suitable energy. Where organic semiconductors are concerned, when the electron on the HOMO energy levels absorbs a photon, it can be excited to the LUMO energy levels, thus leading to the positively charged hole. The electron and hole are attracted to each other by electrostatic Coulomb forces and the electron–hole pair is defined as an exciton. The electron–hole pair can be separated into free charge carriers by, for example, a suitable built-in potential in the p–n junction for the photovoltaic effect or by an external potential to form electricity. This light dependence of the electric signal has attracted much attention633,634 due to its potential applications in light detection and energy conversion. These photoresponse phenomena have been used as organic photovoltaic cells (Section 4.3), photoconductors, photodetectors,635 light sensors (Section 4.1.4.4), optoelectronic memories (Section 4.1.4.3), signal amplifiers and circuits.
When an external bias voltage is applied onto the two-terminal devices to dissociate the excitons, the light can be transferred into an electric signal. In this situation, the conductivity of the channel can be tuned or even switched between ON and OFF by controlling the light (photoswitch), similar to the role of the gate voltage in the field-effect transistors.502 Because most organic semiconductors have low conductivity, the devices are OFF under normal states. When they are illuminated, the photoexcited charge carriers change the semiconductor to be more conductive (the photoconductive effect567,636), and the photoswitch is switched on. Research suggests that the photo-generated excitons in crystals have a much longer lifetime317 and longer diffusion lengths (micron magnitude)48,212,213 as long-lived triplet excitons by fission of singlets. The generated singlet excitons215 have a short lifetime of less than 10 ns and most of the singlet excitons can fissure into triplet excitons with a lifetime of ∼100 μs. It has been found that the switch on of the vacuum gauge (generating chemicals that land at the surface of crystals and form surface traps)213 can significantly decrease the photocurrent and a thin protective layer can inhibit the gauge effect. These results imply that the photoconductivity is predominantly a surface effect (Fig. 39A). A possible mechanism is that the long-lived triplet excitons are capable of reaching the surface of the crystals via long-range diffusion, where they dissociate due to the interactions of triplets with surface defects (e.g. lattice discontinuity) and thereby generate a surface photocurrent. The photoconductivity follows a power law with three distinct regimes as the excitation power increases (Fig. 39B), rather than a linear photo-response.213 Moreover, the surface photoconductivity shows different power exponents compared to the bulk photoconductivity, due to the different dominating terms of exciton fission, fusion and diffusion, as well as triplet-charge quenching. The localized photoexcitation and spatially resolved detection of excitonic luminescence visually showed that the triplet exciton is strongly anisotropic with long-range diffusion along the direction of π–π stacking (Section 2.5).48 The competition637 between the luminescence of excitons and the decay of excitons into free carriers determines the photocurrent. It has been suggested that free charge carriers only appear at enough photo energies, and at relatively low photo energies, free charge carriers can only be created through a long-lived intermediate state. The long-lived intermediate state probably refers to the triplet excitons.
 Fig. 39  (A) Effect of a high-vacuum gauge on the photoconductivity of rubrene crystals, suggesting that the surface trapping due to the gauge effect significantly reduces the photoconductivity of rubrene crystals and hence implying that the photoconductivity is predominantly a surface phenomenon. (B) Typical dependence of photocurrent on the excitation power in rubrene crystals, showing three distinct regimes. Reprinted with permission from ref. 213. Copyright © 2015 Macmillan Publishers Limited. 
A quick response and a large current on/off ratio are necessary for the application of photoswitches in light detectors,638 light sensors, memories318 and circuits. Remarkably, photoswitches with on/off ratios as high as 103 and response times (rise and decay) less than 100 ms have been reported for some organic semiconductor crystals.67,83,355,403,639–642 The charge accumulation and release rates643 can be as high as 1012 cm−2 s−1. It should be noted that besides the light density, the photoresponse is also highly dependent on the wavelength of the light. The reason is that the energy levels of the organic semiconductor are discrete and hence can only be excited by photons with certain energies.639 Besides the aforementioned gauge effect and protective effect, another piece of evidence to manifest the surface-activated photoexcitation is that the switch ratio in the photoswitches639,644 is affected by the area of the crystals under illumination. Hence, the photocurrent can be tuned by modification of the crystal surface by exciton splitters.212 For some semiconductors (e.g., rubrene), the photoresponse is very slow or even irreversible. The persistent ON state could be ascribed to the photo-oxidation of the organic crystals.130,458,644,645 The photoresponse544 effect was also reported in single crystal charge-transfer interfaces, which were formed by laminating two PVT-grown crystals (TCNQ and rubrene) successively onto the substrate. Interestingly, the charge-transfer interface exhibited much higher photoresponse than the sum response of the two individual crystals. This could be ascribed to the interfacial band bending facilitating the photoexciton dissociation.
It is well recognized that gate bias can facilitate the dissociation of the excitons. In this situation, the device is defined as a phototransistor, which can display an even higher on/off ratio (as high as 104–106) than that of a photoswitch.109,301,402,403,502,639,641,646 The photoresponsivity is gate dependent and increases with the increase in the gate bias. This in turn confirms that the gate voltage (the photovoltaic effect567,636) can facilitate the dissociation of the photo-generated excitons.317,643 In photoswitches,641 the semiconductors may not recover back to their initial state after illumination due to residual charges in the conducting channels. However, the gate bias in phototransistors can repel the residual charges, resulting in low OFF current and a high on/off ratio.318 This residual charges may be ascribed to the traps or degradation of the semiconductors as mentioned above.644,647 Therefore, phototransistors exhibit higher sensitivity (signal amplification) and larger on/off ratios (lower noise) than photoswitches. In addition, the mobility643 of the transistor can also be significantly enhanced by exposure to light. It should be noted that the increment of the mobility is also dependent on the wavelength of the incident light.614,643 This is similar to the wavelength dependence of the photoswitch performance. Additionally, the enhancement is particularly large for devices with lower mobility, which can be ascribed to the filling of the traps by the photogenerated carriers. Finally, it is understandable that the photoresponse properties are highly dependent on the dimension of the crystals because the photocurrent can only be generated from the absorption. This is the reason that bottom contact devices result in better performance than top contact devices.643 It is also the reason that the photocurrent increases with the thickness until the current reaches a saturation at a thickness of about 6 μm for 2,6-di(naphthalene-2-yl)-9,10-bis(triisopropylsilylethynyl)anthracene (TIPSAntNa) single crystals.502 Based on the high sensitivity and large on/off ratio of phototransistors, a series of devices with potential applications can be fabricated.639
4.1.4.3 Memory devices. 
Many methods can be used for constructing memory devices such as using a floating gate, a ferroelectric dielectric layer or photoresponse.641,648 A good non-volatile memory should possess at least two stable states including the initial state (OFF state as “0”) and the “reading” state (ON state as “1”) after the “writing” process, as well as the ability to recover to the initial state through an “erasing” process. Taking an organic phototransistor as an example (Fig. 40),318 (i) when there is no light and the gate voltage is applied on the transistor, it works in the pristine state; (ii) after illumination, it exhibits high conductivity, and this illumination process is the “writing” process. If the highly conductive state is persistent, it can be used as the “reading” state; (iii) when the channel shifts from high conductivity to low conductivity by applying a suitable gate voltage or other approaches, it is an “erasing” process; (iv) a good memory means that it can be operated in at least one complete cycle of “reading”, “writing” and “erasing” processes. For example, rubrene can only be “written” once because of the persistent photoconductivity that originates from the degradation of the organic semiconductors (see Section 4.1.4.2). Kim and co-workers318 reported one unprecedented high photoresponsivity organic semiconductor (an anthracene derivative) crystal, whose phototransistor can be operated through repeated “writing”, “reading” and “erasing” processes as shown in Fig. 40. Similar examples614 can also be found for crystals based on two porphyrin derivatives by using monochromatic light of 474 nm to “write” and a gate voltage of −100 V to “erase”.
 Fig. 40  (A) Transfer characteristics of phototransistor ① in the dark and ② under light irradiation; (B) current dependent on the different process, ① “writing” process by applied illumination, ② “reading” process without illumination, ③–⑨ attempted “erasing” process by using different gate voltage; (C) current dependent on the different process through a whole operation cycle, ① “writing” process by applied illumination, ② “reading” process, ③ “erasing” process by an applied gate voltage of −90 V; (D) current dependent on the different process through two operation cycles with multi-stage process, ① first “writing” process by applied illumination, ② “reading” process, ③ second “writing” and “reading” process by an applied gate voltage of −50 V, ④ “erasing” process by an applied gate voltage of −90 V. Reprinted with permission from ref. 318. Copyright © 2011 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Polar ferroelectric polymers such as poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF) and its copolymers with trifluoroethylene (TrFE) (PVDF–TrFE) can be applied as the dielectric layer for data storage. The permanent dipole pointing from fluorine to hydrogen in the ferroelectric state can result in macroscopic polarization which can be switched by an electric field. However, this kind of polymers usually possess many structural defects such as grain boundaries, pinholes, residual solvents, and surface roughness, all of which affect the arrangement and morphology of the semiconductor thin film layers on them and hence result in unreliable data retention and high voltage operation. Semiconductor crystals make the studies on this area possible because they can avoid such effects due to their compact structures and inherently low defects. In order to reduce the leakage current of such memory devices, another insulating layer such as a metal oxide or a polymer can be inserted between the gate electrode and the ferroelectric layer. For example, memory devices based on TIPS-PEN crystals exhibited a substantially stable drain–source current modulation with an ON/OFF ratio higher than 103 at a gate voltage sweep of ±30 V and a data retention longer than 5 × 104 s by using PVDF–TrFE as the ferroelectric dielectric layer.649 However, this method usually results in a high voltage operation (e.g. ±30 V) accompanied with high power consumption. The blending of ferroelectric polymers with other polymers is not only capable of achieving smooth films, but is also beneficial for decreasing the thickness of the insulating layer which in turn can lead to low voltage operation. For instance, blends of PVDF–TrFE and PMMA650 showed lower surface roughness even at a thickness of 200–300 nm, which provided hysteresis of TIPS-PEN crystal devices with a drain current bistability of 103 and a data retention time of more than 15 h at ±15 V. A further decrease in the operation voltage651 can be achieved by confining the ferroelectric layer (PVDF–TrFE) in nanoscopic trenches to form a dense PVDF–TrFE layer. The polarization of PVDF–TrFE can be retained with similar crystal orientation, even after melting and recrystallization, because of the confined crystallization. Based on this hybrid ferroelectric layer, the ON/OFF bistability of the TIPS-PEN crystal device was approximately 102 under ambient conditions with a gate voltage sweep of ±8 V and the ON and OFF currents did not change obviously over 2 h. The stacking of ferroelectric materials on the semiconductors acting as a surface modification129 is another way for keeping the polarization as well as reducing the effect on the defects and growth of the semiconducting layer.
4.1.4.4 Sensors. 
As mentioned above, due to the ultra-sensitive conducting channel of OFETs to outside stimuli, e.g., pressure, temperature, light, gas, liquid or bio-analytes, OFETs are suitable for wide applications as sensors and actuators for accurate detection.418,648,652–654 For example, toxic gas molecules (e.g. NO2, CO or organic solvents) can be adsorbed onto the conducting channels and act as defects for charge carriers. This means that the drain–source current can be changed sharply before and after gas adsorption, which makes OFETs ideal for use as gas sensors. Pressure, especially mechanical pressure, can change the intermolecular interactions (Section 4.1.1.2) and light can generate excitons which can be separated into charge carriers (Section 4.1.4.2). Both of them in turn will change the OFET performance.
The electrical properties of the devices are highly dependent on the intermolecular distance or arrangement and therefore organic field-effect transistors can be used to investigate the atmosphere or mechanical pressure because they can change the intermolecular distance or even molecular arrangement. For example, pressure directly applied onto the crystals can change the performance of the devices,454 which can be ascribed to the change in the intermolecular interactions due to the stretching or compression of the crystals. Fig. 41 presents one example418 where the dielectric layer is used to test variations in the pressure. The field-effect transistors used for mechanical pressure sensing consisted of rubrene single crystals as the semiconductor layer and PDMS microstructures as the dielectric layer. The microstructure of the dielectric layer provided a sensitive regime for detecting the mechanical pressure because the pressure can decrease the dielectric thickness and thereby increase the capacitance. The dielectric capacitance is highly dependent on the thickness and compactness of the dielectric layers, which has been discussed in Section 4.1.1.3.2. The drain–source current can be affected by the dielectric capacitance, which is nonlinearly dependent on the pressure. Results suggest that the sensitivity and the pressure range can be adjusted by fine-tuning the shape of the PDMS structures.
 Fig. 41  (A) Schematic diagram of a FET sensor for mechanical pressure sensing based on rubrene crystals and PDMS microstructures as the semiconductor and insulator layer respectively. (B) Output curves at different pressures (loads). (C) The change of drain–source current is proportional to the change in capacitance. This is nonlinearly dependent on the pressure. Inset: Quick response and relaxation of the pressure sensor. Reprinted with permission from ref. 418 and 655. Copyright © 2010 Macmillan Publishers Limited and Copyright © 2012 Science China Press. 
Some organic semiconductor crystals are highly sensitive to light. Hence, devices based on these organic semiconductor crystals can be used for detecting irradiation by converting the light signal into an electrical signal. Under illumination, the semiconductor molecules can absorb photons and then generate excitons. When a bias is applied, the excitons can be separated into charge carriers. The generation of excitons is highly dependent on the light density and wavelength. For example, by using 4-hydroxycyanobenzene (4HCB) and 1,8-naphthaleneimide (NTI) as semiconductors, Fraboni and co-workers656 reported an X-ray detector with high stability and reproducibility. They found that high-Z electrodes and substrates can enhance the collected electrical signal, and this effect can be eliminated by shielding the electrodes during irradiation or by using low-Z materials such as organic materials as electrodes (e.g. PEDOT:PSS).
Chemical sensors are one of the most widely studied sensing applications of OFETs. Normally, the chemical is adsorbed onto or diffused into the channel which leads to polarization of the semiconductors,128,399,459 the charge traps,569 the formation of the D–A complex657 or even novel products due to the reaction between the adsorbed chemicals and the organic semiconductors.658 By investigating the change in the electric signal, the adsorbed objects can be detected. A good chemical sensor should possess high selectivity, high responsivity, fast response, fast recovery and high sensitivity. All signals such as conductivity and threshold voltage can be exploited as sensitive detection probes. For example, the adsorption of NO molecules481 onto the surface of indolo[3,2-b]carbazole (ICZ) crystals can positively shift the threshold voltage and increase the drain–source current. On the other hand, the adsorption of ethanol onto 2,8-dichloro-5,11-dihexylindolo[3,2-b]carbazole (CHICZ) crystals569 decreases the mobility and reproducible, sensitive and selective response is observed. Research suggests that charge carrier transport occurs at the interface between the semiconductor and insulator and the interface has a thickness of only several nanometers, as mentioned above. Hence, it is generally believed that the chemicals should diffuse, possibly through for example grain boundaries, into this interface for chemical sensing.652 From this point of view, the air dielectric can exhibit much higher sensitivity than the PMMA dielectric, because of the larger area available for adsorption of the toxic gas. The adsorption659 of SO2 enhanced the mobility of CuPc by 764% in 20 ppm SO2 by decreasing the shallow trap density and the air dielectric provided both high sensitivity (119% enhancement at 0.5 ppm) and a high resolution of 100 ppb. High selectivity is also the prerequisite for a senor for practical applications. Li and colleagues reported an ammonia gas sensor with high selectivity, high responsivity (low relative off current when exposed to ammonia), high sensitivity (low concentration detection ability), fast response and recovery rate, good reversibility and stability. This sensor was made by using dip-coated 2,7-dihexyl-dithieno[2,3-d;2′,3′-d′]benzo[1,2-b;4,5-b′]dithiophene (DTBDT-C6) crystals for their devices.292 Interestingly, the devices based on rubrene crystals128,459 exhibited a negligible response to normal acetone or water vapors. On the other hand, devices in which the crystals had been modified by SAMs displayed a clear sensitive action on such vapors, in particular for polar species such as acetone, ethanol and water.128,459 Because the chemicals are unlikely to diffuse into the crystals, the sensing of the chemicals is more probable to be a surface phenomenon.
The detection of analytes in solutions can also be accomplished using OFETs where the most convenient way is to use the solution as the dielectric layer. For example, a NaCl aqueous solution560–562 is adopted as the dielectric layer and Ag/AgCl wire or Pt meshes are used to provide the gate bias voltage, in a system that is widely adopted to investigate the pH values. Results suggest that the decrease in pH values can result in protonation which leads to the formation of delocalized radical cations and thereby the conductivity is increased. At the same time, the gate voltage is helpful for increasing the sensitivity with a reverse response at positive/negative voltage. At positive voltage, high pH values correspond to a low drain/source current, whereas at negative voltage, high pH values correspond to a high drain/source current.

4.2 Device physics in organic single crystal light-emitting diodes and light-emitting transistors
Organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs) consist of two terminals, with hole injection coming from a transparent anode (usually ITO) and the electron injection from a cathode. Holes and electrons combine to form excitons in the organic emitting layer and then decay occurs via irradiation to give off light.660 Normally, thin film OLEDs require at least double layers of organic semiconductors: one layer is p-type for the hole injection and transport, and another layer is n-type for electron injection and transport. The balance of hole and electron injection and transport is the key issue for the high efficiency of OLEDs. The crystal-based OLEDs and the corresponding light-emitting transistors discussed here are dedicated to the study of the intrinsic properties of the semiconductors, the exploration of the charge injection, transport and combination, the investigation of the structure–property relationship, and the examination of the important factors for high performance devices, considering the high quality, the anisotropy and the defect-free characters of the crystals. For example, based on the analysis of the crystal structures, one efficient way for enhancing the light emission is to prevent the quenching by tuning the molecular packing motifs, e.g. shifting the co-facial H-aggregation to head-to-tail J-aggregation.
The first OLEDs based on single crystals661 were reported in 1963 by using very high voltage potential (>400 V). For single layer devices, the active emitting layer should possess ambipolar properties for effective emission. In order to achieve ambipolar transport, one efficient method is to use low work function materials to facilitate electron transport, due to the lack of high performance n-type transport. For example, ambipolar transport was observed for α,ω-bis-biphenyl-4-yl-terthiophene (BP3T) crystals when Al or Li was used as the cathode and Au as the anode. The devices exhibited excellent electroluminescence.662 Similarly, orange emission was observed from perylene crystals663 when Ag and Mg:Ag were used as the two electrodes. 1,4-Bis(2-cyano-2-phenylethenyl)benzene (BCPEB) crystals664 have also been reported which showed green electroluminescence emission with Au as the anode and Al or LiF/Al as the cathode. The devices with LiF/Al as the cathode displayed better performance than those with Al as the cathode. This suggests the importance of the energy level matching between the LUMO energy level of the organic semiconductor crystals and the Fermi level of the cathode. By comparing two crystal materials with the same Au–Ca asymmetrical electrodes, Feng et al. found that the better energy level matching can ensure a more effective hole and electron injection,665 which will result in a lower carrier injection barrier and a lower onset voltage. High luminance intensity can be achieved. However, the external electroluminescence (EL) quantum efficiencies of these single-layer devices are still low, which can be ascribed to the low electron mobility and the poor charge balance between the hole and the electron mobilities. By using pure 1,4-bis(4-methylstyryl)benzene (BSB-Me), tetracene or pentacene doped BSB-Me crystals, three primary colors (blue, green and red respectively) can be achieved.666 The pentacene doped BSB-Me showed a high luminance of 820 cd cm−2 with a high EQE of 0.31%.
Double-layer structures are widely used in thin film OLEDs, which are more likely to be adopted in practical applications. Therefore, double-layer structures667 containing p- and n-type semiconductor crystals are ideal candidates for investigating these systems. It is indisputable that balancing hole and electron transport is necessary for achieving high performance OLEDs. By using 1,4-bis(4-methylstyryl)benzene (CH3-P3V2) and 5,5′-bis(4-trifluoromethyl pheny)[2,2′]bithiophene (P2TCF3) as the hole transport and electron transport materials respectively, the double-layer structure showed an external EL quantum efficiency of up to 0.025%. This is one order of magnitude higher than that of single layer devices, showing the importance of the p–n junction for light emission. Additionally, although the whole thickness of the device was much larger than that of the traditional thin film devices, a current density of 100 mA cm−2 can be obtained at 19 V. This was achieved at much lower electric fields compared with the traditional thin film device due to the high carrier mobility in the single crystals. In order to achieve high mobility to enhance the light emission, therefore, one efficient way is to fabricate double layer devices by utilizing films which contain many crystals as the active layer. For example, coronene nanowires336 are drop-cast onto the n-SiC substrate to form a heterojunction with the n-SiC. This exhibits visible emission with a full width at half maximum (FWHM) of about 90 nm at 20 V by using ITO coated quartz and Ti/Au as the cathode and anode respectively. Similar results337 were also reported by using DPA nanostructures as the p-type active layers.
As mentioned above, the gate voltage can control the charge injection and transport in the conducting channels, and this provides an efficient way to control the combination of electrons and holes if an additional gate voltage is applied to the light-emitting diodes. This kind of devices are called organic light-emitting transistors (OLETs), and were first demonstrated in 2003 for tetracene films.632,648,668–671 Due to the planar geometry of OLETs, this is a direct way to access electroluminescence visually compared to the traditional vertically stacked OLEDs. Not surprisingly, because of their potential applications in organic lasers and integrated organic optoelectronic devices such as displays, OLETs have attracted a lot of attention.
In early studies, light emitting properties were observed in unipolar field-effect transistors. In p-type conducting channels, when holes transport to the drain electrode, they combine with electrons from the drain electrode and then emit light near the drain electrode. Because the light emission region is confined to near the drain electrode within a very narrow region it follows that the efficiency of the devices is very low. The efficiency is limited by the injection of electrons and most of the holes are wasted during the propagation rather than contributing to light emission. The efficiency can be improved by the use of an asymmetric electrode configuration to facilitate the electron injection (e.g., BP3T with LUMO at 2.8 eV, HOMO at 5.1 eV). The use of low work function materials as one electrode (Al: 4.3 eV or Mg:Ag alloy:3.7 eV) and high work function materials as another electrode (Au: 5.1 eV)527 is one option. It is generally accepted that low work function materials benefit the electron injection and transport due to the match between their work function and the LUMO energy levels of the organic semiconductors.451
In order to enhance the electron injection and decrease the exciton quenching at the metal electrodes, ambipolar charge transport is preferred. In 2007, Takenobu and co-workers672 reported the first ambipolar OLETs based on organic semiconductor single crystals. Tetracene crystals were adopted as the semiconducting layer and Ag–Ag symmetrical electrodes or Mg–Au asymmetrical electrodes were used as the drain/source electrodes. When the gate voltage was kept constant and the drain voltage was applied, ambipolar transport and light emission were obtained. In addition, the position of the recombination or emission zone could be tuned along the conducting channel by adjusting the drain voltage. Unlike the aforementioned unipolar OLET based on BP3T,527 the ambipolar transport obtained from tetracene can probably be ascribed to the PMMA modification of the substrate which reduced the electron traps between the crystal and the dielectric layer, and the high drain voltage which repelled (or injected) the electrons into the channel. By inserting PMMA between the BP3T crystal and the SiO2 dielectric layer and using Au/Ca asymmetrical electrodes to facilitate the electron transport, ambipolar BP3T610 transport was also reported with hole and electron mobilities as high as 1.64 and 0.17 cm2 (V s)−1, respectively. Fig. 42 presents the device configuration and the light emission of the BP3T crystals. Results confirm that the PMMA modification can significantly decrease the traps in the semiconductor/insulator interface.451 Similar results451,589,673 were found by using Au/Ca asymmetrical devices and the resulting ambipolar FETs can emit light when the gate voltage is almost half of the drain voltage.673,674 All these results indicate that, as has been mentioned in the previous section, the passivation of the dielectric layer and the matching of the energy levels between the Fermi level of the electrodes and the HOMO/LUMO energy levels of the semiconductors are very crucial for efficient charge transport.
 Fig. 42  (A) Weak excitation photoluminescence image of a BP3T crystal on a PMMA/SiO2/Si substrate; (B) schematic diagram of an OLET device configuration and the imitation of the injection and recombination of the charge carriers; (C) top view and (D) the side view of the edge emission of the BP3T crystals showing that the emission moves with an increase in the drain voltage. Reprinted with permission from ref. 610. Copyright © 2009 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. 
Because the commonly adopted electrodes for electron injection are susceptible to air, it is useful to keep the fabrication and characterization process free from air and increase the deposition rate and the thickness of electrodes. Moreover, a buffer layer inserted between the electrode/semiconductor interface can facilitate the injection of the charge carriers, For example,675 due to the energy level mismatch between the HOMO energy level of oligo(p-phenylenevinylene)p-bis[(p-styryl)styryl]benzene (P5V4) and the work function of the Au electrode, the threshold voltage was found to be as high as −80 ± 3 V. However, the threshold voltage could be reduced to 2 ± 3 V by inserting a thin MoOx layer (2 nm) and the minimum current density increased to 100 A cm−2 under the same conditions. This was two orders of magnitude higher than that of the devices without the MoOx layer. Similarly, it was observed that alkali halide modification of the electrodes, such as by using CsF, can facilitate electron injection, and this led to a higher mobility of 0.2 cm2 (V s)−1 and a lower threshold voltage of 50 V. In the absence of CsF modification, the mobility was 0.1 cm2 (V s)−1 and the threshold voltage was at 120 V.676
Although various attempts have been made to improve the device performance of the OLETs, almost all of them adopted electrode materials with low work functions with a view to facilitating electron injection. These unstable low work function materials usually resulted in low device performance and made the devices unstable. Yamao and co-workers677 reported a kind of OLETs involving an alternating current (AC) bias on the gate. At low frequencies, the device worked similar to that using DC voltage. The device gave off light only when the gate voltage was negative and the major carriers were holes recombining with electrons close to the source electrode. Here the source electrode was defined as the terminal associated with electron injection. However, at high frequencies, when the gate voltage becomes positive, there will be a region of depleting holes near the source electrodes, and the quick injection of electrons will result in highly efficient emission. The results suggest that high frequencies will lead to enhanced and apparently continuous light compared to the blinking light which is observed at low frequencies. These results are quite attractive because the device is based on a symmetrical electrode configuration with stable and high work function electrodes, and no treatment is necessary for the substrates, which of course will significantly simplify the fabrication process. Where the practical application of OLETs is concerned, the emission light should be strong enough and as narrow as possible. By using a square-wave bias instead of the sinusoidal wave, Yamao et al.678 reported a device which showed emission ten times stronger than that of a normal sinusoidal wave AC gate bias and a much narrower spectral line with a FWHM of 1.1 nm. The emission679,680 can also be narrowed by using grating dielectric layers.681 There is no doubt that the recombination of the electrons and holes was confined in the crystals. Therefore, it is to be expected that the recombination of the charge carriers and the luminescence can be confined by current confinement through etching of the crystals.676 By using this method, the current path was confined to the remaining parts of the crystals and thereby current density as high as 33 kA cm−2 could be obtained.
Double-layer structures were also studied for investigating the double-layer OLETs. In bilayer-crystal devices two crystals are laminated onto the substrate successively. For example, AC5-CF3 and BP1T crystals682 were successively placed on the substrate to form the heterojunction. However, the device only exhibited p-channel conduction under the DC bias due to the non-direct contact of the n-type crystal with the electrodes. This device produced much brighter light emission from both p- and n-type crystals under AC bias. The emission was hundreds of times higher than that of the single-layer devices. Further studies683 showed that an additional intermediate contact between the two crystal layers and an additional bottom contact (in this situation every crystal of the double-layer crystals has both bottom and top-contact) made the direct injection of electrons and holes possible. This in turn facilitated the light emission even without any gate bias. Devices based on double-layer crystals such as a coupler and an optical resonator have been addressed.684 We will summarize the representative semiconductor crystals, their chemical structures, growth conditions and properties for OLETs in Table 4.

4.3 Device physics in organic single crystal photovoltaic cells
Organic photovoltaic cells (OPVs) utilize the photoelectrical effect of organic semiconductors to convert light into electricity. This is the reverse process of generating light from electricity found in OLEDs. OPVs include several basic processes: the absorption of light by organic semiconductors to generate excitons, diffusion and dissociation of excitons into free charge carriers in organic semiconductors, the transport of the free charge carriers, and the collection of charge carriers by the electrodes.689 When the electron jumps into the LUMO orbit after absorption of a photon, the spin property of the electron may remain the same or convert to its opposite spin, leading to singlet or triplet states respectively. After its generation, the exciton may diffuse, or evolve ranging from dissociation into electron–hole pairs, fusion of two excitons into one exciton, fission into two excitons, trapping, annihilation or emission (Sections 2.5, 4.1.4.2 and 4.2). In order to achieve efficient OPVs, the enhanced absorption of the photons, the long-range diffusion and efficient dissociation of the excitons, and the fast transport of the electron and hole free charge carriers to reduce the recombination of the electron–hole pairs are of significant importance. Singlet excitons can split into two triplet excitons and this singlet fission process leads to carrier multiplication in photovoltaics.217,690 The singlet has a very short lifetime (∼ns) and hence the fission of singlets into triplets (lifetime: ∼μs) is essential for OPVs. Crystals open a route for fission of singlet excitons. Moreover, the triplets have a large diffusion length (∼μm) in crystals. The long lifetime and diffusion length of the triplet excitons is helpful in enhancing the dissociation of the excitons, reducing the recombination and quenching and thereby increasing the conversion efficiency.214 From the above sections, it is clear that the crystals are also beneficial for the transport and collection of the free charge carriers, due to the absence of the grain boundary, the ordered molecular stacking and the minimal defect density (Sections 2 and 4). On the other hand, after creation of the excitons, the exciton has to move to an interface where it can be ionized into an electron–hole pair. Therefore, the presence of a heterojunction is essential for the photovoltaic effect which provides an internal built-in potential for dissociating the excitons. In view of these two points, crystals with a p–n junction interface are preferred for prolonging the lifetime of the excitons, extending the diffusion length of the excitons, facilitating the dissociation of excitons, accelerating the charge transport and reducing the recombination. However, until now, only a few reports have addressed OPVs based on organic semiconductor crystals. This is probably due to the difficulty in fabricating heterojunctions based on crystals. Hence, only several examples and perspectives on organic semiconductor crystals are presented here.
The most efficient way to achieve large heterojunctions for dissociation of excitons is to produce structures with regioregular p–n junctions, such as by blending p–n junctions. This method can provide a large p–n junction interface but is less successful in creating highly ordered structures. This results in a slow drifting speed of the charge carriers with subsequent large recombination.691 Recent research on co-crystals297,299,315,316,692 has the potential to pave an avenue for creating organic photovoltaic cells based on organic semiconductor crystals. This kind of crystals contain D–A molecules, which exhibit ambipolar charge transport and can be regarded as blending p–n heterojunctions on the molecular scale.
Another method is to produce structures with separated p–n layers, such as coaxial structures with p–n core–shell structures.693 This method is more likely to achieve ordered structures (or crystals) to facilitate the charge transport, decrease the recombination probability and subsequently improve the power conversion efficiency. However, it suffers from the presence of small p–n junction interfaces simultaneously, which works against dissociation of the excitons and leads to lower device performance. An additional challenge is the need to fabricate this kind of double layer crystal structures.694 The p–n junction can also be achieved by using segments of p- and n-semiconductor crystals. For example, Hu and co-workers119 reported a p–n junction by using epitaxial growth, which exhibited highly ordered structures. The p-type nanowire seeds and the epitaxially grown n-type nanowires were produced by using a PVT method. Then p–n junction nanowire crystals were successfully obtained. The power conversion efficiency of an individual nanowire was estimated to be 0.007% with an open-circuit voltage (VOC) of 0.35 V. This VOC value is similar to the theoretical value of 0.4 V. The schematic diagram, SEM images, the band diagram and the current–voltage characteristics of the p–n junction crystals are all presented in Fig. 43. Although the power conversion efficiency is not very high, it should be emphasized that this value is obtained from an individual nanowire, which has a very small illuminated area and a small p–n junction interface. Yao et al.382 reported another p–n junction where they used the subsequent simultaneous epitaxial growth of CuPc onto n-type 5,10,15,20-tetra(4-pyridyl)-porphyrin (H2TPyP) hollow structures. The individual nanowire device exhibited a power conversion efficiency of 0.08% with a photocurrent density (JSC) of 0.029 mA cm−2, an open-circuit voltage of 0.64 V and a fill factor (FF) of 23%. Similar attempts were also made by using orthogonal solvents to deposit the second nanostructure onto the first crystals. For example, HBC nanowires287 were spin-coated onto PCBM crystals, which are obtained by using a slow dip-coating technique. Although the efficiency was found to be a little low, it is understandable considering the low illumination intensity, the small area of the heterojunction interface and the parallel heterojunction interface to the irradiation. The results suggested that the potential application of these heterojunctions in optoelectronics is highly dependent on the density and size of the nanowires. Crystal films, which contain well oriented heterojunction arrays, can probably provide a higher performance due to the increase in the interfacial areas for efficient dissociation of the excitons and the ordered structures reducing the recombination of the charge carriers. Bilayer crystal films695 were reported by using the solution containing p- and n-type semiconductors, which gave an improved PCE of 0.46%. The Schottky interface between the rubrene crystal and the Al or LiF/Al electrode696 was also utilized for producing the photovoltaic effect. The magnitude of the photovoltage was found to be equal to the built-in potential of the Schottky junction, which equals the work-function differences. The insertion of LiF was found to be efficient for increasing the photovoltage because it can reduce the work function of the Al electrode.
 Fig. 43  (A) Schematic diagram, (B) current–voltage characteristics, (C) band diagram and (D) SEM images of the individual nanowire for OPVs with CuPc as the p-type crystal seed and F16CuPc as the epitaxially grown n-type crystal. Adapted with permission from ref. 119. Copyright © 2010 American Chemical Society. 
It is more common to utilize one n- or p-type semiconductor crystal with thin films of another type of semiconductor to construct OPVs. This can be done in two ways: either by blending them or just using two separated layers. In this situation, the crystals provide an efficient route to transport one kind of charge carrier to the electrodes and the thin films guarantee the necessary large area of the heterojunction interface. All of these can of course facilitate higher power conversion efficiency due to the increase in efficiency for charge dissociation and decreasing the possibility of charge recombination. For example, by using tetracene crystals and C60 thin films to form the heterojunction,697 devices exhibit a short-circuit current density (JSC) of 1.12 mA cm−2, an open-circuit voltage (VOC) of 0.57 V, a fill factor (FF) of 54% and a power conversion efficiency (PCE) of 0.34%. The single-crystal devices show that the PCE was enhanced by decreasing the thickness of the crystal, which probably can be ascribed to the better conformity on the substrate and lower resistance of the thinner crystals. The lower resistance is helpful in facilitating the charge collection because of the less traveling distance. Furthermore, the anisotropic adsorption and anisotropic photovoltaic efficiency were also observed. The recent development of hybrid organic–inorganic lead halide perovskite materials for photovoltaic applications698,699 has also attracted extensive attention on the single crystals of these materials. The results suggest that high quality single crystals can be a better choice for fundamental studies and solar energy conversion applications, due to the low impurity and defect concentration (trap densities700,701 can be as low as 109–1010 cm−3, compared with the trap densities of 1015 cm−3 in thin films) and the absence of crystal boundaries which improve the charge transport, increase the carrier diffusion length and reduce the recombination of the excitons.702 Large single crystals of MAPbX3 (MA = CH3NH3+; X = Br− or I−) with volume exceeding 100 cm3 can be obtained by using a temperature-lowering method in hydroiodic acid,703 a solvent-vapour diffusion method (anti-solvent vapour89)700 or using crystal seeds.701 The diffusion length of the carriers could exceed 100 μm for single crystal perovskite materials,701 compared with the nm-level diffusion length for thin films.699 For example, the application704 of MAPbI3 crystals with micrometer grain size and high surface coverage shows a good photovoltaic efficiency of 10.6%.
5 Conclusions and outlook
Over the recent few decades, organic semiconductor crystals have attracted increasing attention with respect to revealing the intrinsic properties of organic semiconductors, providing a powerful tool not only for examining structure–property relationships and investigating the important factors for enhancing the device performance but also for studying the charge transport mechanism in organic semiconductors and guiding further molecular design and synthesis.
A wide range of techniques for organic semiconductor crystals have been developed, from controllable crystal growth to device applications. For example, the PVT method251 was developed for producing high quality crystals with a view to avoiding the low thermal stability of organic semiconductors. Various solution-processing techniques314,352 were studied in order to lower the cost and to solve the problem of those semiconductors which are thermally unstable. In the case of device fabrication based on small crystals, the usage of “organic ribbons” as masks makes the device fabrication possible. Devices with channel lengths down to micro- and nanometer sizes can be achieved.85 The “stamp” method was explored with the intention of thoroughly eliminating the thermal irradiation (present in the traditional deposition methods) of the crystals.453 In addition, new techniques for the study of the charge transporting properties of crystals have also been developed. For example, in the early study of organic semiconductor crystals, the mobility of the crystals was obtained by using TOF measurements,167 the space-charge limited current (SCLC) method705 or using the Hall mobility.706,707 Currently, great progress has also been made with respect to devices based on single crystals and advances in theoretical calculations. In 1996, the first single crystal OFET device446 was fabricated based on sexithiophene and it had a mobility of 0.075 cm2 (V s)−1. After that, tremendous progress has been achieved. For example, a mobility of up to 8 cm2 (V s)−1 was obtained from rubrene single crystals252,257 in 2003. Now, the highest single crystal mobility36 has increased to 40 cm2 (V s)−1, which is comparable to that found for silicon polycrystals. The crystal growth techniques, device fabrication methods, techniques for the study of charge transporting properties and progress of the computation for fundamental physics provide a footstone for investigating the molecular arrangements, the intrinsic properties and the structure–property relationships of the organic semiconductor crystals and the charge transport mechanism. For instance, the suitable growth method and the purification of the crystals provide visual evidence that the thermally activated transport is not due to the small-polaron hopping in which the carrier is localized in the molecule. Similarly, the mechanism of generation, diffusion, fission and fusion of the excitons and their dissociation into charge carriers or degradation into light irradiation can be revealed.
In this review, we have attempted to give an overview of the structure–property relationships of organic semiconductor crystals, summarized the various crystal growth methods and discussed the fundamental physics of organic semiconductor crystals in optoelectronics. For example, it is generally accepted that long-range ordered molecular arrangement and strong intermolecular interactions especially π–π stacking are essential to achieve high performance devices. The energy level matching between the semiconductor and the electrodes, and the compatibility between the semiconductor and the electrodes/insulator are also important factors which will affect the device performance significantly. The recent research also suggests that the intrinsic charge transport mechanism should be the band-like transport (large polaron), in which the charge carriers are delocalized in several molecules and the small-polaron is unlikely to exist in the organic semiconductor crystals especially in the high performance devices. From this systematic analysis, we believe that some guidelines can be provided to the readers on a range of topics. These include (1) the current progress of organic semiconductor crystals, including the growth methods for organic semiconductor crystals, the factors for achieving high performance devices, the intrinsic properties of the materials, the structure–property relationships, and the charge transport mechanism; (2) selection of a suitable method for achieving high quality organic semiconductor crystals with the desired size and dimension. For example, by using PVT, drop-casting, solvent-exchange or solvent-diffusion methods, etc., large high quality crystals can be obtained due to the slow process; (3) optimization of the device performance to achieve the intrinsic properties of the semiconductors through reasonable adjustment of the relative factors. For example, the air/vacuum dielectric layer can be utilized for reducing the dielectric constant and the defects between the semiconductor/insulator interface; (4) prediction of the morphology, molecular arrangement and properties. At the same time, we also give some guidance for the future research of organic semiconductor crystals. These include (i) the urgency to produce high performance n-type, heterojunctions,708 or ambipolar organic semiconductors for inverters, integrated circuits, organic light-emitting diodes, organic light-emitting transistors and organic photovoltaic cells. We also want to point out that the recent research on D–A co-crystals provides a possible solution to meeting these requirements;297,299,315,316,692 (ii) the challenges and opportunities in achieving large organic semiconductor crystals and 2D large crystals for potential practical applications. Finally, it should be noted that thin films are more suitable for practical applications. However, they are facing other challenges, mainly originating from the low long-range order, grain boundaries and defects. For example, it is suggested that the influence of defects is more pronounced in 1D semiconductors than in 2D or 3D ones. Such phenomena can be fatal for thin film devices because of the weak coupling and challenging transport route along other orientations.28 We hope that this review will pave a way to boost further study of organic semiconductor crystals. This is crucial to achieve high performance optoelectronic devices, investigate the fundamental device physics underlying these topics and meet the shortfall in inorganic semiconductors as well as maintain the current advantages of organic semiconductors. An in-depth survey on the organic semiconductor crystals will benefit further understanding of organic semiconducting materials and promote their applications in organic optoelectronics. The applications of crystals in transistors, light-emitting transistors, photovoltaic cells and lasers485,709,710 bring infinite possibilities to the future.

Table Content:

Chengliang Wang
	Chengliang Wang is a Professor in Huazhong University of Science and Technology (HUST). He received his Bachelor's degree in Chemistry from Nanjing University in 2005 and PhD degree from the Institute of Chemistry, Chinese Academy of Sciences (CAS) in 2010. He then worked in the Chinese University of Hong Kong, University of Muenster and Technical University of Ilmenau. He was selected in the National 1000-Talents Program for Young Scholars and joined HUST in 2016. His research topics include the design and synthesis of novel conjugated materials, crystals, nanostructures and their applications in organic optoelectronics and flexible batteries.

Huanli Dong
	Huanli Dong is a Professor at the Institute of Chemistry, CAS. She received her MS degree (2006) from Fujian Institute of Material Structure, CAS, and her PhD (2009) from the Institute of Chemistry, CAS. She was awarded the Outstanding Young Scientist of NSFC in 2012 and the Prize for Young Chemists of Chinese Chemical Society in 2014. Her research interests include molecular materials, crystals and devices of organic/polymeric semiconductors. Currently, she has more than 140 refereed publications with over 4500 citations.

Lang Jiang
	Lang Jiang is a Professor at the Institute of Chemistry, Chinese Academy of Sciences (ICCAS). He received his BS degree in Applied Physics from the School of Physics and Microelectronics Science, Hunan University in 2004 and his PhD degree from ICCAS in 2011. Then he became an Assistant Professor in ICCAS. In 2013, he worked as a postdoctoral fellow with Prof. Henning Sirringhaus in Cavendish Laboratory, University of Cambridge. In 2016, he moved back to ICCAS, as part of the Hundred Talents Program. His current research focuses on molecular electronics including crystalline organic semiconductors and device engineering and their circuit applications.

Wenping Hu
	Wenping Hu is a Professor at Tianjin University. He received his PhD from the Institute of Chemistry, Chinese Academy of Sciences in 1999. Then he joined Osaka University and Stuttgart University as a research fellow of Japan Society for the Promotion of Sciences and Alexander von Humboldt, respectively. In 2003 he returned to the Institute of Chemistry from Nippon Telephone and Telegraph (Japan), and was then promoted to full professor in the institute. He was awarded the National Science Fund for Distinguished Young Scholars in 2007 and was appointed as Cheung Kong Professor of Ministry of Education of China in 2014. He was elected as an assistant president of Tianjin University in 2013 and vice president of the university in 2016. His research interests include organic semiconducting materials, single crystals and devices. He has published more than 470 refereed publications with over 15000 citations (H index = 62) and has edited 4 books for Wiley-VCH, etc. He was an associate editor of Polymer Chemistry (RSC) from 2013 to 2016, and an editorial member of several journals including Advanced Energy Materials, Advanced Electronic Materials, Nano Research, Science China Materials, etc.

 	Fig. 7  (A) Representative trap density of states (DOS) data in small molecule organic semiconductors. The trap densities of single crystal (SC) FETs (red lines) are much lower than those of thin film transistors (TFTs, black lines), indicating that the structural defects such as grain boundaries tend to dominate in thin films. The trap densities of bulk single crystals (blue lines) are lower than those of SC-FETs. This suggests that the trap densities are much more likely to be on the surface or interface. Importantly, the highly hydrophobic CytopTM gate dielectric can significantly reduce the trap density. Adapted with permission from ref. 133. Copyright © 2010 The American Physical Society. (B) Distribution of band and localized states for polycrystalline pentacene. Acceptor-like states are formed around 0.29 eV above the VBM. Reprinted with permission from ref. 139. Copyright © 2009 Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH & Co. KGaA, Weinheim. VB: valence band, CB: conduction band.
